Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Universal Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues against the necessity of defining all concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the necessity of definitions for all concepts and identify his alternative epistemological sources.

Core Idea
Not every concept needs a definition; infinite regress proves some knowledge is foundational.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a cornerstone of classical logic: the claim that every concept (tasawwur) requires a definition (hadd). He argues that if definitions were needed for all concepts, we would face either an infinite regress or circularity, which is impossible. Therefore, some concepts must be known without definition — either as self-evident (badihi), theoretical (nadhari), or through concurrent transmission (tawatur). In this lesson, we unpack his logical argument and its implications for epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
What is the logical claim that Ibn Taymiyya is attacking? Find the exact statement in the passage that states that definitions are necessary for all concepts.
The logicians assert that every concept must have a definition to be known.
2
Trace Ibn Taymiyya's conditional
Ibn Taymiyya points out that if every concept required a definition, then either the definition would itself require a definition (infinite regress) or some definitions would mutually depend on each other (circularity). Identify where he states this.
Look for the words 'dawr' (circularity) and 'tasalsul' (infinite regress).
3
Identify the logical conclusion
Since infinite regress and circularity are impossible, what must follow? Ibn Taymiyya concludes that some concepts are known without definitions. He says knowledge of them is either 'badihi' (intuitive) or 'nadhari' (theoretical). But note he also introduces 'tawatur' later.
This is a classic modus tollens argument: If P then Q, not Q, so not P.
4
Analyze the examples
Ibn Taymiyya gives examples: relational matters (like father/son) are understood without definition. Also, many existential matters are known by tawatur. Explain why these are counterexamples to the logicians' claim.
These examples show that real knowledge exists without formal definition.
5
Assess the critique's validity
Does Ibn Taymiyya's argument hold within the framework of classical logic? Consider whether the logicians could respond that definitions are needed for theoretical concepts, not all concepts. Evaluate the strength of his objection.
Ibn Taymiyya's critique is epistemological: he challenges the exclusivity of definition as a source of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه كلمات [؟] في هذه الكليات التي يكثر منها اضطراب الناس. ويعلم أنه يمكن تصور المحدود بدون أحد (الحد) وهو المطلوب. ثم إنهم معترفون بأن من التصورات ما لا يحتاج إلى حد وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل. فيكون العلم بها بديهيا أو نظريا. والأمور الإضافية مثل كون القصب [؟] أوطنه. وقد يغني عنه [؟] وهذا غلط عظيم وهو مخالف للواقع، فإن من الأمور الوجودية ما يعلم بالتواتر.
These are comprehensive words about these universals from which people's confusion frequently arises. It is known that it is possible to conceive the defined without the definition, and that is the goal. Then they admit that among concepts there are those that do not need a definition, otherwise there would be circularity or infinite regress. Thus knowledge of them is either intuitive or theoretical. Relational matters, such as a reed being [؟] its homeland. And it may suffice without it. This is a great error, contrary to reality, for among existential matters there are those known by concurrent transmission.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the logicians' claim that every concept (tasawwur) requires a definition (hadd). Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that it is possible to conceive something without its definition, and that this is precisely the point at issue. He then notes that the logicians themselves admit that some concepts are understood without definitions; otherwise, an infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr) would necessarily follow. This implies that knowledge must ultimately be either self-evident (badihi) or acquired through theoretical reasoning (nadhari). He gives the example of relational matters (al-umur al-idafiyya) like being a father or a son, which are understood without explicit definition. He concludes that the logicians' insistence on definitions for all concepts is a great error contrary to reality, because many matters, such as those known by tawatur (concurrent testimony), are known without any formal definition. This argument is central to Ibn Taymiyya's broader critique of Greek logic: he denies that definition is the exclusive path to knowledge.

Key Terms

حد
بديهي
نظري
تواتر

B) Infinite regress or circularity
C) Consensus among scholars
D) Knowledge by concurrence (tawatur)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that requiring definitions for all concepts leads to a logical impossibility, thus proving that some knowledge is foundational and not definition-dependent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Critique of the Necessity of Definitions
Author refutes the logicians' claim that every concept requires a definition, invoking circularity and regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma argument against the necessity of definitions and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
Not every concept needs a definition; demanding one for all leads to circularity or regress.

This lesson unpacks a key critical argument from Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the logicians. He targets the claim that every concept (tasawwur) requires a definition (hadd). By posing a dilemma, he exposes a fatal flaw in that requirement: it either makes definitions unnecessary or pushes us into an infinite regress. Understanding this critique sharpens our ability to evaluate foundational assumptions in Islamic logic and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' doctrine
Identify the position Ibn Taymiyya is attacking: that every concept must be defined by a proper hadd (genus + differentia) to be known.
This is the standard Aristotelian-Avicennian view criticized here.
2
Extract the dilemma premise
Notice the author's question: 'Is knowledge of this definition intuitive (badihi) or theoretical (nazari)?' This creates two exclusive options.
The logicians' own classification of knowledge is turned against them.
3
Analyze each horn
If the definition is known intuitively, then the concept it defines could be known intuitively without a definition—making the definition unnecessary. If it is theoretical, then it itself requires a definition, leading to an infinite regress or circularity.
The terms 'dawr' (circularity) and 'tasalsul' (regress) are explicitly mentioned.
4
Evaluate the critique's effectiveness
Assess whether the logicians can escape the dilemma. For example, they might argue that some definitions are self-evident or that the regress stops at primitive concepts. Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya's objection is decisive or merely exposes a need for nuance.
This step requires thinking beyond the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع أنه يمكن تصور المحدود بدون حد، وهو المطلوب. الحادي عشر: أنتم تعترفون أن من التصورات ما نضطر إلى حد، وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل. فيقال: أيكون العلم به بديهياً أو نظرياً؟
While it is possible to conceive the defined without a definition, and that is what is required. The eleventh [point]: You acknowledge that among concepts there are those that we need a definition for, otherwise circularity or regress would follow. So it is said: Is knowledge of it intuitive or theoretical?
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents a critical argument against the logicians' requirement that every concept must be defined. The author points out that it is possible to conceive something without a definition, which is the desired conclusion. Then he raises the eleventh objection: the logicians themselves admit that some concepts require a definition, otherwise there would be circularity or infinite regress. The question then becomes: is the knowledge of these concepts intuitive (badihi) or theoretical (nazari)? If they are intuitive, then definition is not needed; if theoretical, then they require definitions, leading to regress. The author is exposing a dilemma in the logicians' position.

Key Terms
الحد
التصور
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It makes concepts too complicated.
B) It leads to circularity or infinite regress.
C) Definitions are always impossible.
D) The logicians themselves do not use definitions.

Key Takeaway
Demanding a definition for every concept is self-defeating because it either makes definitions unnecessary or triggers an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a key concept from fiqh, such as 'riba' (usury). Attempt to define it with a full hadd (genus + differentia). Then evaluate whether that definition avoids the dilemma: is your definition known intuitively or theoretically? If theoretically, can you define its terms without regress?
?
Find a passage in a usul al-fiqh text that defines a term (e.g., 'qiyas'). Use Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma to test whether the definition is necessary or if the concept could be understood without it.

Essential vs Accidental Attributes in Definition
Author defines essential and accidental attributes in definition theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and accidental attributes and identify the two subtypes of necessary accidents (lāzim al-māhiyya and lāzim al-wujūd) in a given definition.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are definition-dependent; accidental attributes may still be necessary but not part of the definition.

In constructing definitions, logicians separate what is essential (dhātī) from what is accidental ('araḍī). However, some accidental properties are so closely tied to the essence that they cannot be conceptually removed. This passage dissects the accidental into necessary (lāzim) and contingent ('āriḍ), and further distinguishes between properties necessary for the quiddity versus those necessary for existence. Understanding this hierarchy is vital for evaluating definitional completeness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined terms
The passage defines 'al-dhātī' (essential) and 'al-'araḍī' (accidental). Note that 'al-dhātī' is that upon which the definition depends; it is the opposite of 'al-'araḍī'.
Essential attributes are required for a correct definition.
2
Divide the accidental into necessary and contingent
The author states that the accidental is either lāzim (necessary) or 'āriḍ (contingent). A necessary accident cannot be separated from the essence in reality, while a contingent one can.
This division is crucial because not all accidents are eliminable.
3
Subdivide the necessary accident
The necessary accident is further split into lāzim al-māhiyya (necessary for the quiddity) and lāzim al-wujūd (necessary for existence). The passage gives examples: evenness for four (lāzim al-māhiyya) and laughter for a horse (lāzim al-wujūd).
The example 'laughter for a horse' in the source is uncertain; likely it refers to humans.
4
Apply the distinguishing test
The key difference: for lāzim al-wujūd, the essence cannot be conceived as existing without it. For lāzim al-māhiyya, the property is intrinsic to the quiddity but the essence can logically exist without it? Actually, the passage states lāzim al-māhiyya is also inseparable, but the distinction is nuanced. Practice applying this test to concepts like 'barking for a dog' or 'oddness for three'.
This test helps avoid confusing necessary accidents with essential properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذاتي هو الذي توقف المحدود عليه خلاف العرضي. والعرضي إما لازم وإما عارض. واللازم إما لازم لوجود الماهية دون حقيقتها كالضحك للفرس[؟] وإما لازم للماهية كالزوجية والفردية للأربعة والثلاثة. والفرق بين لازم الماهية ولازم وجودها أن لازم وجودها لا يمكن أن تعقل الماهية موجودة دونه.
The essential is that upon which the defined depends, contrary to the accidental. The accidental is either necessary or contingent. The necessary is either necessary for the existence of the essence without its reality, like laughter for the horse[?], or necessary for the essence, like evenness and oddness for four and three. The difference between that which is necessary for the essence and that which is necessary for its existence is that that which is necessary for its existence cannot allow the essence to be conceived as existing without it.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes as used by logicians in definition theory. The author states that the essential is that upon which the definition of a thing depends, the accidental being its opposite. The accidental is then divided into necessary (lazim) and contingent ('arid). The necessary is further subdivided: some necessary attributes are necessary for the existence of the essence (like laughter for a horse, meaning that a horse must have the capacity to laugh, though not actually), while others are necessary for the essence itself (like evenness for the number four). The key difference between these two types of necessary attributes is that the existence-necessary attribute is such that the essence cannot be conceived as existing without it, whereas the essence-necessary attribute is intrinsic to the quiddity. This distinction is crucial for understanding how logicians construct definitions and identify essential properties.

Key Terms
الذاتي
العرضي
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Evenness for the number four

C) Animality for a human
D) Oddness for the number three

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are the core of a definition; accidental attributes, even if necessary, must be distinguished to avoid conflating definitional content with concomitant properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the definition of 'prayer' (ṣalāh) as 'statements and actions beginning with takbīr and ending with taslīm'. Identify one essential attribute (e.g., takbīr) and one necessary accident (e.g., facing the qibla). Explain whether the necessary accident is lāzim al-māhiyya or lāzim al-wujūd.
?
2. In the definition of 'fasting' (ṣawm) as 'abstention from food, drink, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset', is 'intention' (niyya) essential or a necessary accident? Apply the test from the passage.

Ibn Taymiyya's Dilemma Against Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that every definition either requires another definition or does not, contradicting the logicians' claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's disjunctive argument against the necessity of definitions and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
Every definition either requires another definition (regress/circularity) or does not, undermining the claim that definitions are indispensable.

This lesson focuses on a classic critique from Ibn Taymiyya's Radd al-Mantiqiyyin. He targets the logicians' assertion that definitions (hadd) are the only path to conceptualizing essences. Using a disjunctive dilemma, he forces the opponent into an impossible choice: either each definition requires another (leading to infinite regress or circularity), or it does not (making the logicians' claim false). By breaking down this argument, you will see how Ibn Taymiyya turns the logicians' own tools against them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
The logicians claim that a thing (mahiyya) is only known through a definition (hadd). This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Clarify the meaning of 'hadd'
Ibn Taymiyya notes that logicians use 'hadd' to mean 'a statement that indicates the essence', not the thing itself. This precision is crucial for the argument.
3
Extract the disjunctive premise
The definer either defines the thing by another definition (hadd) or by a non-definition (ghayr hadd). These are the two horns of the dilemma.
This is an exclusive disjunction: either one or the other, not both.
4
Analyze the first horn (definition by definition)
If the definer uses another definition, then either that second definition also requires a definition (leading to infinite regress, tasalsul) or the chain eventually circles back to the first (prior circularity, dawr qabli). Both are impossible by consensus of rational people.
Ibn Taymiyya appeals to the universal impossibility of infinite regress and circularity in causal chains.
5
Analyze the second horn (definition by non-definition)
If the definer uses a non-definition, then the thing is known without a definition, directly contradicting the logicians' claim that it is only known by a definition.
This horn shows the logicians' claim is false, not just problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: أن يقال: الحد يراد به «نفس المحدود»، وليس هذا مرادهم هنا. ویریدون به القول الدال على ماهية المحدود»، وهو مرادهم هنا، وهو تفصیلُ ما دل عليه الاسم بالإجمال. فيقال: إذا كان الحد» قول الحادّ ، فالحادّ إما أن يكون قد عرَّف المحدود بحد يستلزم وإما أن يكون عرَّفه بغير حدّ . فإن كان الأول فالكلام في الحد الثاني كالكلام في الأول، وهو مستلزم للدور القبلي أو التسلسل في الأسباب والعلل، وهما ممتنعان باتفاق العقلاء. وإن كان عرفه بغير حدّ بطل سَلْبُهم ، وهو قولهم «إنه لا يُعرَف إلا بالحد» .
The second aspect: that it is said: 'hadd' (definition) is used to mean 'the defined itself', and that is not their intended meaning here. They intend by it 'the statement that indicates the essence of the defined', and that is their intended meaning here, and it is the detailed explanation of what the name indicates in brief. So it is said: if the definition is the statement of the definer, then the definer either has defined the defined by a definition that necessitates [another definition] or has defined it by a non-definition. If the first, then the discussion of the second definition is like the discussion of the first, and it necessitates prior circularity or infinite regress in causes and effects, and both are impossible by the agreement of rational people. And if he defined it by a non-definition, then their negation is invalidated, which is their statement 'it is not known except by definition'.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This is the second argument (wajh) in the first station. Ibn Taymiyya focuses on the nature of definition (hadd). He clarifies that the logicians use 'hadd' to mean a statement that reveals the essence (mahiyya) of the thing defined, not the thing itself. Then he poses a dilemma: if the definer defines the thing, either he uses another definition or a non-definition. If he uses another definition, that second definition either also requires a definition (leading to infinite regress tasalsul) or the chain circles back (dawr qabli). Both are impossible by universal consensus (ittifaq al-'uqala'). If he uses a non-definition, then the logicians' claim that the thing is only known by definition is false, because it was known by a non-definition. Thus, their central claim is logically unsustainable.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): A definition that states the essence (mahiyya) of a thing, composed of genus and differentia. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses the logicians' definition against them; he does not endorse it.]
الدور القبلي (al-dawr al-qabli): Prior circularity: a circularity where A is defined by B and B by A, which is logically vicious. [Note: Used in logic and usul al-fiqh to critique circular definitions.]
التسلسل (al-tasalsul): Infinite regress: an infinite chain of definitions where each requires a further definition. [Note: Common argument in kalam against infinite chains.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what happens if the definer defines the thing by another definition?
A) It is accepted without further issue
B) It leads to either infinite regress or circularity
C) It becomes a non-definition
D) It proves the claim

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's disjunctive argument exposes that the logicians' claim about the necessity of definitions is either self-defeating (via regress or circularity) or false (since non-definitional knowledge is possible).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Ibn Taymiyya's method, construct a disjunctive argument against the statement: 'You cannot know what a hukm (legal ruling) is unless you have a definition of hukm.' Show that either each definition requires another (leading to regress or circularity) or it does not (making the claim false).
?
Identify whether the following involves prior circularity (dawr qabli) or infinite regress: 'To know what fiqh is, you need to know what hukm is; to know what hukm is, you need to know what fiqh is.' Explain why.

Ibn Taymiyya's Empirical Refutation: People Know Without Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya points out that all nations and professions know things without using the logicians' definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's empirical argument against the necessity of definitions and evaluate its force against the logicians' claim.

Core Idea
If definitions were necessary for knowledge, no one would know anything — yet everyone knows.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya delivers a devastating blow to a core claim of Aristotelian logic: that conceptualization (tasawwur) depends on definition (hadd). He appeals to plain observation: people of all nations, from scholars to artisans, know the things they need without ever consulting the logicians' definitions. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how it undermines a foundational principle of the logicians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The logicians assert that all conceptualization requires a definition. Without a proper definition (hadd), one cannot have certain knowledge of a concept. Find this claim in the preceding context (not in this short passage, but it is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's attack).
This claim is found in earlier sections of the text where Ibn Taymiyya sets up the logicians' position.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's evidence
He points to 'all nations' — including people of knowledge, discourse, practice, and crafts — who know and realize the things they need without definitions. This is an empirical observation, not a logical deduction.
3
Reconstruct the refutation as a modus tollens
If the logicians were right, then no one could have knowledge without definitions. But Ibn Taymiyya observes that people do have knowledge without definitions. Therefore, the logicians' claim is false. Write this as: If P (definitions necessary) then Q (no knowledge without definitions); not Q (knowledge exists without definitions); therefore not P.
This logical structure shows the argument is valid, though Ibn Taymiyya does not state it explicitly.
4
Assess the strength of the argument
Ibn Taymiyya's observation is a broad inductive generalization. Are there exceptions? The logicians might argue that people use implicit or tacit definitions. But Ibn Taymiyya's point is that explicit formal definitions as prescribed by Aristotle are not used. Consider whether this argument completely refutes the necessity of definitions or merely shifts the burden of proof.
The argument is not a knockdown refutation of all uses of definition, but it effectively disproves the strong claim that definition is universally necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إن الأمم جميعهم من أهل العلم والمقالات وأهل العمل والصناعات يعرفون الأمور التي يحتاجون إلى معرفتها ويحققون ما يعانونه من العلوم والأعمال من مفردات العلوم بغير الحدود
The third aspect: that all nations – including the people of knowledge and discourse, and the people of practice and crafts – know the things they need to know and realize what they undertake of sciences and practices from the particulars of sciences without definitions.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This is the third argument against the logicians' claim. Ibn Taymiyya appeals to empirical observation: throughout human history, all communities – scholars, theologians, artisans, and workers – have acquired knowledge of the things they need without ever consulting the logicians' definitions (hudud). They understand concepts, practice crafts, and engage in sciences using ordinary language and experience. This is a practical refutation: if the logicians were right, no one could know anything without a formal definition, which is obviously false. Therefore, the claim that conceptualization requires definition is not only logically unproven but also contradicted by reality.

Key Terms
الأمم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Empirical observation of various nations and professions
C) Religious scripture
D) Authority of philosophers

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's empirical observation undercuts the logicians' claim that definitions are necessary for knowledge, demonstrating that human cognition operates through natural and communal experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemological Critique: The Negator's Burden of Proof
Author opens the negative station on definitions and presents the first argument against the logicians' claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the logicians' claim about definitions is a statement without knowledge and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
If the logicians' foundation is a baseless negation, their whole system collapses.

Ibn Taymiyya opens his negative station by targeting the logicians' claim that non-evident concepts can only be known through definition. He argues that this claim is a negative proposition that is not self-evident, so the logicians must provide proof. Without proof, it is a statement without knowledge—a fatal flaw for a system claiming to be the instrument that guards the mind from error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
Find the exact statement: 'Non-evident concepts are only attained through definition.' This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
The claim is about the necessity of definition for conceptual knowledge.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's premise on burden of proof
He states that the negator (nāfī) has the burden of proof if his negation is not self-evident, just like the affirmant. This applies to all non-self-evident propositions, whether negative or affirmative.
This is an epistemological principle: knowledge requires justification.
3
Classify the logicians' claim as a negative proposition
The claim 'concepts are only attained through definition' is a negative proposition (sāliba) because it denies that concepts can be attained without definition. It is not self-evident.
Ibn Taymiyya treats it as a qadiyya saliba.
4
Apply the critique: lack of proof
Since the claim is non-self-evident and negative, the logicians must provide a proof. They have not done so; therefore, their assertion is a statement without knowledge (qawl bilā 'ilm).
The critique is that the foundation of their 'instrument of knowledge' is itself baseless.
5
Evaluate the implications
Ibn Taymiyya rhetorically asks how a statement without knowledge can be the basis for the measure of knowledge. This undermines the logicians' entire project.
The argument is devastating because it attacks the most basic premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الأول : المقام السلبي في «الحدود والتصورات» في قولهم : إن التصورات غير البديهية لا تنال إلا بالحد» والكلام على هذا من وجوه : أحدها: أن يقال : لا ريب أن النافي عليه الدليل، إذا لم يكن نفيه بديهياً، كما أن المثبت عليه الدليل. فالقضية - سواء كانت سلبية أو إيجابية - إذا لم تكن بديهية فلا بد لها من دليل . وأما السَّلب بلا علم فهو قول بلا علم فقول القائل إنه لا تحصل هذه التصورات إلا بالحد قضية سالبة، وليست بديهية. فمن أين لهم ذلك ! وإذا كان هذا قولاً بلا علم كان في أوّل ما أسسوه القولُ بلا علم فكيف يكون القول بلا علم أساساً لميزان العلم ولما يزعمون أنه آلة قانونية تَعْصِمُ مراعاتُها الذهن أن يَزِلّ في فكره؟
The First Station: The Negative Station concerning Definitions and Concepts. Regarding their statement: 'Non-evident concepts are only attained through definition.' The discussion on this is from several aspects. One of them: It is said: There is no doubt that the negator has the burden of proof if his negation is not self-evident, just as the affirmant has the burden of proof. So the proposition—whether negative or affirmative—if it is not self-evident, must have a proof. As for negation without knowledge, it is a statement without knowledge. So the statement of the one who says 'These concepts are only attained through definition' is a negative proposition and is not self-evident. From where do they have that? And if this is a statement without knowledge, then the first thing they have established is a statement without knowledge. So how can a statement without knowledge be the foundation for the measure of knowledge and for what they claim is a legal instrument whose observance protects the mind from error in its thinking?
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage opens the first negative station of Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the logicians. He begins by stating the target: the logicians' assertion that non-self-evident concepts are only attained through definition. Then he presents the first of several arguments against this claim. The argument is epistemological: every non-self-evident proposition, whether affirmative or negative, requires a proof (dalil). The claim in question is a negative proposition (it says that concepts are not attained except through definition) and it is not self-evident. Therefore, the logicians must provide a proof for it. If they assert it without proof, they are making a statement without knowledge. This is a devastating critique because the very foundation of their science would be built on a baseless claim. Ibn Taymiyya then rhetorically asks: how can a statement without knowledge be the basis for the measure of knowledge, the instrument that supposedly protects the mind from error?

Key Terms
المقام السلبي
التصورات غير البديهية
الحد
القضية السالبة

Ibn Taymiyya's Empirical Case Against Necessary Definitions
Author argues that experts in various fields conceive their subject matter without logical definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's inductive argument that experts in Islamic sciences know their subject matter without logical definitions, and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
Knowledge of concepts does not require logical definitions.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that definitions are necessary for conceptualization. He points to the actual practice of experts in fields like fiqh, grammar, and medicine. These experts clearly understand the concepts of their disciplines, yet they do not use Aristotelian definitions. This observation forms an inductive argument that conceptual knowledge is independent of logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
The logicians' doctrine that definition (al-ḥadd al-manṭiqī) is necessary for conceptualization (taṣawwur).
2
List Ibn Taymiyya's examples
Jurists, grammarians, physicians, accountants, and craftsmen – all know the particulars of their sciences without using logical definitions.
3
Understand the inductive structure
Ibn Taymiyya generalizes from these examples to all nations and sciences. This is an inductive generalization (istiqrā').
Induction from observed cases to a universal claim.
4
Evaluate the strength of the induction
Are the examples representative? Does the absence of logical definitions among these experts prove that definitions are not used? Consider potential counterexamples like theoretical physics or philosophy.
5
Extract the conclusion
Conceptualization is independent of definitions (istighnā' al-taṣawwur 'an al-ḥudūd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إن الأمم جميعهم من أهل العلم والمقالات وأهل العمل والصناعات يعرفون الأمور التي يحتاجون إلى معرفتها ويحققون ما يعانونه من العلوم والأعمال من مفردات العلم بغير حدّ منطقي . ولا نجد أحداً من أئمة العلوم يتكلم بهذه الحدود، لا أئمة الفقه، ولا النحو، ولا الطب ولا الحساب ولا أهل الصناعات، مع أنهم يتصوّرون مفردات علمهم . فعلم استغناء التصور عن هذه «الحدود».
The third aspect: All the nations, including the people of knowledge and opinions, and the people of practice and crafts, know the matters they need to know and realize what they deal with from the sciences and works, the particulars of knowledge, without a logical definition. And we do not find any of the leading scholars speaking with these definitions—not the leading jurists, nor grammarians, nor physicians, nor accountants, nor craftsmen—despite their conceiving the particulars of their science. So it is known that conceptualization is independent of these 'definitions'.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
Ibn Taymiyya presents empirical evidence from various fields: jurisprudence, grammar, medicine, arithmetic, and crafts. The practitioners of these sciences conceive the concepts they need without using the technical definitions of the logicians. The leaders of these sciences—jurists, grammarians, doctors, etc.—do not employ such definitions. This demonstrates that conceptual knowledge is independent of definitions.

Key Terms
الحد المنطقي (al-hadd al-mantiqi): A definition formulated according to the rules of Aristotelian logic, composed of genus and differentia. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses this to distinguish ordinary conceptual understanding from formal logical definition.]
مفردات العلم (mufradat al-'ilm): The basic concepts or terms of a given science. [Note: General term.]

Ibn Taymiyya's Argument Against Logicians' Definitions
Author argues that no definition in any field has been established without objection per logicians' standards.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that no definition meets logicians' criteria and explain why he considers this a form of sophistry.

Core Idea
Definitions according to logicians' own standards have never succeeded, making their epistemology self-defeating.

Ibn Taymiyya here presents his fourth argument against the logicians' theory of definition. He claims that despite centuries of effort, no definition in any field—grammar, usul al-fiqh, philosophy, medicine—has been established that is free from objections according to the logicians' own principles. If knowledge of concepts required such definitions, then we would know nothing, which is sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
The logicians claim that definitions (hadd) are the primary means to acquire concepts (tasawwur). Ibn Taymiyya targets the requirement that definitions must be free from any objection according to their own strict criteria.
The passage does not state the logicians' claim explicitly but it is implied by the context of the argument.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's deconstruction through examples
He lists well-known definitions: man as rational animal, sun, noun (in grammar), qiyas (in usul al-fiqh), and others. He notes that all have well-known objections; even if some are sound, they are extremely rare.
The examples span multiple disciplines: philosophy, grammar, usul al-fiqh, medicine, kalam.
3
Draw the consequence for knowledge
If conception (tasawwur) depends on perfect definitions, then since no such definitions exist, no conception has occurred. Since assent (tasdiq) depends on conception, no knowledge can exist. Therefore, the logicians' own position leads to universal ignorance.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: their principles lead to a conclusion they would reject.
4
Identify the critique type and charge of sophistry
Ibn Taymiyya labels this consequence as 'the worst sophistry' (safsata). The critique is epistemological: the logicians' standards for definitions are so stringent that they make knowledge impossible, which contradicts the evident fact that people do have knowledge.
Sophistry here means the denial of the possibility of knowledge, which Ibn Taymiyya accuses the logicians of falling into.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أنه إلى الساعة لا يُعلم للناس حد مستقيم على أصلهم. بل أظهر الأشياء لا يعلم حد «الإنسان» وحده بـ «الحيوان الناطق» عليه الاعتراضات المشهورة. وكذلك حد «الشمس»، وأمثال ذلك حتى إن النحاةَ لَمّا دخل متأخروهم في الحدود ذكروا لـ «الاسم» بضعة وعشرين حداً، وكلها معتَرَض عليها على أصلهم، وقيل: إنهم ذكروا لـ «الاسم» سبعين حداً لم يصح منها شيء، كما ذكر ذلك ابنُ الأنباري المتأخر . والأصوليون ذكروا لـ «القياس» بضعة وعشرين حدّا، وكلها معترض على أصلهم . وعامة الحدود المذكورة في كتب الفلاسفة والأطباء والنحاة، والأصوليين، والمتكلمة معترضة على أصلهم ؛ وإن قيل بسلامة بعضها كان قليلاً، بل منتفياً. فلو كان تصور الأشياء موقوفاً على الحدود لم يكن إلى الساعة قد تَصَوَّر الناسُ شيئاً من هذه الأمور ؛ والتصديق موقوف على التصور، فإذا لم يحصل تصور لم يَحْصُل تصديق ؛ فلا يكون عند بني آدم علم في عامة علومهم . وهذا من أعظم السَّفْسَطة .
The fourth aspect: That up to now, no correct definition according to their principles is known to people. Rather, the most obvious things—the definition of 'man' as 'rational animal' alone has well-known objections against it. Likewise the definition of 'sun', and similar things, such that when later grammarians entered into definitions, they mentioned for 'noun' several and twenty definitions, all of which are objected to according to their principles. It is said that they mentioned for 'noun' seventy definitions, none of which were valid, as mentioned by the later Ibn al-Anbari. And the usuliyyun mentioned for 'qiyas' several and twenty definitions, all objected to according to their principles. And most of the definitions mentioned in the books of philosophers, physicians, grammarians, uṣūliyyūn, and mutakallimūn are objected to according to their principles. If it is said that some are sound, they are few, rather non-existent. So if the conception of things were dependent on definitions, then up to now people would not have conceived anything of these matters. And assent is dependent on conception, so if conception does not occur, then assent does not occur. So there would be no knowledge among human beings in most of their sciences. And this is the worst sophistry.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
Ibn Taymiyya argues that despite centuries of effort, no definition has been established that is free from objections according to the logicians' own principles. He gives the example of the definition of 'man' as 'rational animal', which has well-known objections. Similarly, definitions in grammar (like 'noun' with dozens of definitions) and usul al-fiqh (like 'qiyas') are all contested. Even the definitions in philosophy, medicine, grammar, usul, and kalam are all subject to objections. If any are sound, they are extremely rare. The consequence is devastating: if knowledge of concepts depended on definitions, then no one would know anything. Since knowledge of concepts is the foundation for assent (tasdiq), the entire edifice of human knowledge would collapse. This would be pure sophistry.

Key Terms
حد مستقيم (hadd mustaqim): A correct definition that meets the logicians' criteria without objections. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses this to highlight the failure of logicians to produce any such definition.]
السفسطة (al-safsata): Sophistry; the denial of the possibility of knowledge. [Note: Standard term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the impossibility of perfect definitions according to logicians' criteria leads to the collapse of all knowledge, which is the essence of sophistry.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the True Definition
Author argues that true definition is either impossible or extremely difficult.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the true definition (al-hadd al-haqiqi) is unattainable, and explain why this refutes the logicians' claim that conception depends on definition.

Core Idea
If perfect definition is impossible, yet we know essences, then definition is not the door to knowledge.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents his fifth argument against the logicians' theory of definition. He does not deny that definitions can be useful, but he challenges the claim that true conception (tasawwur) of essences can only be achieved through an essential definition composed of genus and differentia. By showing that such a definition is either impossible or extremely difficult—a point the logicians themselves concede—he argues that our everyday conceptual knowledge must have another source.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' claim
Identify the logicians' position: true conception (tasawwur) of an essence is only obtained through a true definition (al-hadd al-haqiqi) composed of genus and differentia.
This is the standard view in Classical Mantiq.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya asserts this definition is either impossible (mut'adhdhir) or extremely difficult (mut'asir). He points out that the logicians themselves acknowledge this difficulty.
The phrase 'as they have acknowledged' refers to logicians' own admissions.
3
Derive the consequence
If the true definition were the only way to conceive essences and it is impossible or extremely difficult, then no one would conceive most realities, or only rarely.
This is the logical implication of accepting both premises.
4
Present the counterevidence
Ibn Taymiyya states that realities have been conceived by people, even without such definitions. This is empirical evidence against the necessity of the true definition.
5
Conclude the independence of conception
Since conception occurs despite the unattainability of the true definition, conceptual knowledge is independent of the logicians' technical definition.
This is the main conclusion of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : إن تصور الماهية إنما يحصل عندهم بالحدّ الذي هو الحقيقي المؤلف من الذاتيات المشتركة والمميّزة ، وهو المركب من الجنس» و«الفصل». وهذا الحد إما متعذر أو متعسّر، كما قد أقروا بذلك، وحينئذ فلا يكون قد تُصوّر حقيقة من الحقائق دائماً أو غالباً. وقد تُصوّرت الحقائق، فعلم استغناء التصورات عن الحد .
The fifth aspect: That the conception of the essence, according to them, is only obtained through the definition which is the true one composed of the common and distinguishing essential properties, and it is the compound of genus and differentia. This definition is either impossible or extremely difficult, as they have acknowledged. And in that case, no reality would ever be conceived, always or mostly. Yet realities have been conceived. So it is known that conceptions are independent of definition.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
Ibn Taymiyya examines the concept of 'true definition' as defined by the logicians: it is a compound of the genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) that captures the essence. He notes that such a definition is either impossible (mut'adhdhir) or extremely difficult (mut'asir). This is acknowledged by the logicians themselves. Yet people do conceive the realities (haqa'iq) without such definitions. This proves that conceptual knowledge is independent of these technical definitions.

Key Terms
الحد الحقيقي
الجنس والفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is always possible

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's fifth argument demonstrates that the logicians' ideal of a perfect essential definition is not necessary for conceptual knowledge, which is often acquired through alternative means.

Limitations of Definitions to Composite Realities
Author argues that definitions require genus and species, so simple realities cannot be defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify composite and simple realities and explain why the logician's definition method fails for simple realities.

Core Idea
Logicians' definitions only work for composite realities; simple realities are known without definition.

Ibn Taymiyya points out a key limitation in the Aristotelian definition method. Definitions via genus and differentia require composition. But simple realities—those without genus—are still known. This challenges the claim that definition is the only path to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about composite realities
Find in the passage where Ibn Taymiyya states that true definitions are only for composite realities (al-haqa'iq al-murakkaba) that have a genus and differentia. These are the species (anwa').
2
Identify what simple realities are and why they cannot be defined
Locate the phrase 'what has no composition' and understand that simple realities have no genus and do not fall under a higher category. Therefore, according to the logicians, they cannot be defined by genus and differentia.
Simple realities are known directly or through other means, not through definition.
3
Draw the conclusion about knowledge without definition
Realize that Ibn Taymiyya uses this to argue that the logicians' definition method is not necessary for knowledge. Simple realities are known even though they cannot be defined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : إن الحدود الحقيقية عندهم إنما تكون للحقائق المركبة، وهي «الأنواع» التي لها جنس وفصل. وأما ما لا تركيب فيه، وهو ما لا يدخل مع غيره تحت «جنس»، فإنه لا يمكن حده عندهم.
The sixth aspect: That true definitions according to them are only for composite realities, which are the 'species' that have a genus and a differentia. As for that which has no composition, i.e., that which does not enter with another under a genus, it cannot be defined according to them.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
Ibn Taymiyya observes that the logicians' method of definition through genus and differentia only applies to composite realities (haqa'iq murakkaba) that have a genus and a species. As for simple realities that have no genus and do not fall under a higher genus, they cannot be defined according to this method. Nonetheless, these simple realities are known. This further demonstrates the superfluousness of definitions.

Ibn Taymiyya's Fifth Argument: The Impossibility of Real Definition
Ibn Taymiyya argues that real definition is impossible or very difficult.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the real definition (hadd haqiqi) is impossible or very difficult, and evaluate its implication for the necessity of definitions.

Core Idea
If real definitions are impossible, then knowledge cannot depend on them.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya launches a devastating critique against the Aristotelian notion that true conceptualization requires a perfect definition composed of genus and differentia. He argues that such definitions are either impossible or extremely difficult to achieve, and yet people clearly acquire knowledge of realities. Therefore, definitions cannot be a necessary condition for knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find the logical claim Ibn Taymiyya is attacking: that conceptualization (tasawwur) of the quiddity (mahiyya) can only be obtained through the real definition (hadd haqiqi) composed of genus and differentia.
2
Extract the description of hadd haqiqi
Note how Ibn Taymiyya characterizes it: composed of the essential attributes that are common (genus) and distinguishing (differentia). This is the standard Aristotelian definition.
3
Find the assessment of possibility
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that this definition is either impossible (mut‘addhir) or very difficult (mut‘assir), and he claims the logicians themselves acknowledge this.
4
Derive the consequence
If such definitions are impossible or difficult, then according to the logicians' own theory, no reality would be conceptualized always or for the most part.
5
Observe the empirical counter and conclusion
But realities have been conceptualized (people do know things). Therefore, conceptualization does not depend on definition.
This is a clear modus tollens argument: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : إن تصور الماهية إنما يحصل عندهم بالحدّ الذي هو الحقيقي المؤلف من الذاتيات المشتركة والمميّزة ، وهو المركب من الجنس» و«الفضل». وهذا الحد إما متعذر أو متعسّر، كما قد أقروا بذلك، وحينئذ فلا يكون قد تُصوّر حقيقة من الحقائق دائماً أو غالباً. وقد تُصوّرت الحقائق، فعلم استغناء التصورات عن الحد .
The fifth aspect: Indeed, conceptualization of the quiddity according to them is only obtained by the definition that is the real one composed of the essential attributes that are common and distinguishing, and it is composed of 'genus' and 'difference'. And this definition is either impossible or very difficult, as they themselves have acknowledged. And if so, then no reality would be conceptualized always or mostly. And realities have been conceptualized, so it is known that conceptualizations are independent of definition.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's fifth argument against the necessity of definitions. He states that according to the logicians, conceptualization of the quiddity (mahiyya) is only achieved through the real definition (hadd haqiqi), which consists of the essential attributes shared and distinguishing (i.e., genus and differentia). This definition, as the logicians themselves admit, is either impossible (mut‘addhir) or very difficult (mut‘assir). If that were the case, then no reality would ever be conceptualized, or at least rarely. But realities are conceptualized (people do know things), so it is evident that conceptualization does not depend on definition. The point: since the logicians' own standard for definition is so hard to meet, yet we clearly have knowledge, definition cannot be a necessary condition.

Key Terms
الحد الحقيقي (al-hadd al-haqiqi): The real definition composed of genus and differentia, which is supposed to give the essence of a thing. [Note: This is a standard term in Aristotelian logic; Ibn Taymiyya uses it to critique its attainability.]
ماهية (mahiyya): Quiddity or essence, what a thing is in itself. [Note: In this context, it refers to the object of definition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what follows from the impossibility or difficulty of the real definition?
A) All definitions are useless and should be abandoned.
B) Conceptualization is independent of definition.
C) Only logicians can obtain real definitions.
D) Realities cannot be known.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that since real definitions are unattainable yet knowledge exists, conceptualization cannot depend on them.

Identify an Islamic concept (e.g., 'īmān', 'kufr') and explain how it can be conceptualized without a perfect hadd haqiqi, referencing the types of definitions used by scholars.

Ibn Taymiyya on Knowing Things Without Definition
Ibn Taymiyya argues that things without genus/differentia are known without definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that things without genus and differentia are known without definition and evaluate its implications for the necessity of real definitions.

Core Idea
If intellects are known without definition, species known by sense are even easier.

In this sixth aspect, Ibn Taymiyya targets the logicians' claim that real definitions are necessary for all conceptualization. He points out that the logicians themselves admit that some things, like intellects, have no genus and therefore no real definition, yet they still claim to know them. This exposes a contradiction in their system. He then argues that species, being closer to sensory experience, are even more knowable without definition, undermining the need for hadd altogether.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claimed scope of definition
According to the logicians, real definitions (hadd haqiqi) only apply to composite realities (species) that have a genus and a differentia. They do not apply to simple things with no composition.
2
Find the counterexample: simple things without genus
Ibn Taymiyya gives the example of 'intellects' (al-‘uqul) which are simple entities that do not fall under a genus. According to the logicians, such things cannot have a real definition.
3
Note the logicians' admission and contradiction
Despite claiming that simple things cannot be defined, the logicians themselves define and know these things. This shows that conceptualization does not require a definition.
4
Examine the argument from simplicity to compositeness
If simple things (like intellects) can be known without definition, then composite species should be even easier to know without definition, because they are closer to sensory experience and their individuals are observable.
5
Evaluate the critique's logic
The critique is valid: if the simplest realities are knowable without definition, then definition is not necessary for knowledge. The burden of proof shifts to the logicians to show why species still require definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : إن الحدود الحقيقية عندهم إنما تكون الحقائق المركبة، وهي «الأنواع» التي لها جنس) وفضل». وأما ما لا تركيب فيه، وهو ما لا يدخل مع غيره تحت «جنس»، كما مثله بعضهم بـ«العقول فليس له حدّ ، وقد عَرَّفوه، وهو من التصورات المطلوبة عندهم، فعلم استغناء التصوّرات عن الحد، بل إذا أمكن معرفة هذه بلا حد فمعرفة «الأنواع» بغير حد أولى، لأنها أقرب إلى الحس، وأن أشخاصها مشهودة .
The sixth aspect: Indeed, the real definitions according to them are only for composite realities, which are the 'species' that have genus and difference. As for that which has no composition, which does not enter with another under a 'genus', as some exemplified with 'intellects', it has no definition, yet they have defined it, and it is among the conceptualizations sought after according to them. So it is known that conceptualizations are independent of definition. Rather, if knowledge of these can be had without definition, then knowledge of 'species' without definition is even more appropriate, because they are closer to sense and their individuals are observable.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
In this sixth argument, Ibn Taymiyya notes that the logicians' real definitions apply only to composite realities (species) that have a genus and differentia. Things that are not composite, such as intellects ('uqul) — which have no genus — cannot have a real definition in this sense. Yet the logicians themselves claim to know and define such things. This shows that conceptualization does not require definition. Moreover, Ibn Taymiyya argues that if simple things (no composition) can be known without definition, then composite things like species are even more knowable without definition, because they are closer to sensory experience and their instances are observable. The argument is that the most basic realities, which are not composed, are known directly, and by extension, less basic things are also knowable without formal definition.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: The Circularity of Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that learning from definitions is circular because words must be known first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that definitions require prior knowledge of terms and explain why definitions cannot be the foundation of all conceptualization.

Core Idea
Definitions presuppose the very concepts they claim to teach.

Ibn Taymiyya presents a powerful critique that challenges the foundational role of definitions in logic. He argues that definitions cannot be the starting point for knowledge because they rely on prior understanding of their component terms. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
Ibn Taymiyya is attacking the logicians' claim that definitions (hadd) are the proper way to acquire concepts. He argues that definitions are composed of words, each signifying a meaning. To understand the definition, one must already know the meanings of those individual words.
The logicians believed definitions are essential for tasawwur.
2
Trace the circularity
Understanding a definition requires knowing the words and their signification. But knowing that a word signifies a meaning requires prior conceptualization (tasawwur) of that meaning. Thus, definitions presuppose the concepts they aim to teach, creating a circular dependency.
Ibn Taymiyya gives examples: bread, water, sky, earth.
3
Extract the epistemological conclusion
If definitions require prior concepts, they cannot be the source of first concepts. First concepts must be acquired through direct experience, intuition, or divine guidance, not through definitions.
This conclusion undermines the Aristotelian view that all knowledge begins with definitions.
4
Apply the critique to Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, definitions of terms like 'riba' or 'qiyas' presuppose knowledge of their constituents. For example, defining 'qiyas' as 'extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on an effective cause' requires prior understanding of 'ruling', 'original case', and 'effective cause'.
This shows that definitions are not the starting point but rather a clarification after concepts are already known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع : إن مستمع الحد الذي هو مركب من ألفاظ كلٌّ منها لفظ دال على معنى. فإن لم يكن عارفاً قبل ذلك بمفردات تلك الألفاظ ودلالتها على معانيها لم يمكنه فهم الكلام ) . والعلم بأن اللفظ دالّ على المعنى أو موضوع له له العلم بدلالة اللفظ عليه مسبوق بتصوّر المعنى، فمن لم يتصوّر مسمَّى الخبز والماء، والسماء والأرض،
The seventh aspect: Indeed, the listener of a definition, which is composed of words, each of which is a word signifying a meaning, if he was not previously knowledgeable about the individual words and their signification of their meanings, he cannot understand the speech. And the knowledge that the word signifies the meaning or is used for it is preceded by conceptualization of the meaning. So whoever has not conceptualized the referent of bread and water, the sky and the earth...
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument: definitions are composed of words, each of which signifies a meaning. To understand the definition, one must already know the meanings of the individual words. But knowledge that a word signifies a meaning requires prior conceptualization of that meaning. Therefore, definitions cannot be the source of first concepts; they presuppose them. Ibn Taymiyya gives the examples of bread, water, sky, earth — basic things that must be known before any definition can be understood. This argument shows that definitions cannot be the foundation of all conceptualization; there must be prior knowledge.

Key Terms

First Station: Species More Knowable Without Definition
Ibn Taymiyya's argument that species are more knowable without definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's a fortiori argument that species are known without definition and evaluate its force against the logicians' claim that definition is necessary for conceptualization.

Core Idea
If things outside genera need no definition, species — closer to sense — need even less.

In this first station of his critique, Ibn Taymiyya targets the very foundation of Aristotelian logic: the necessity of definition for conceptualization. He uses the logicians' own admission that intellects ('uqul) are known without definition to argue that species, being directly perceptible, are even more certainly known without it. This short but powerful argument exposes an inconsistency in the logicians' system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
Ibn Taymiyya is refuting the claim that definition (hadd) is necessary for all conceptualization (tasawwur). Find where in the passage he references this claim indirectly.
The phrase 'عندهم' (according to them) points to the logicians.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's first premise
He notes that some things falling outside a genus—like the 'intellects' ('uqul)—are admitted by logicians to have no definition, yet they are still known and conceptualized.
Look for 'ليس له حدّ وقد عَرَّفوه' (they have no definition, yet they have been defined).
3
Extract the second premise
Species (anwa') are even closer to sensory experience than those things outside genera. Their individuals are witnessed and directly perceived.
The phrase 'أقرب إلى الحس' (closer to sense) is key.
4
Understand the analogy
If the 'uqul are known without definition despite being remote from sense, then species—being more perceptible—are even more knowable without definition. This is an a fortiori (awla) argument.
The term 'أولى' (more fitting) signals the inference.
5
Assess the conclusion
The conclusion is that conceptualization does not require definition. If we can know species without definition, the logicians' general claim fails.
Ibn Taymiyya states 'فعلم استغناء التصورات عن الحد' (so it is known that conceptualizations are independent of definition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الأول : إذا أمكن معرفة ما لا يدخل تحت جنس بغير حد فمعرفة «الأنواع» أولى كما مثله بعضهم بـ«العقول فليس له حدّ ، وقد عَرَّفوه، وهو من التصورات المطلوبة معرفة «الأنواع» عندهم، فعلم استغناء التصوّرات عن الحد، بل إذا أمكن معرفة هذه بلا حد فمعرفة بغير حد تلك الأنواع أولى، لأنها أقرب إلى الحس، وأن أشخاصها مشهودة .
The first station: If it is possible to know that which does not fall under a genus without a definition, then knowledge of the 'species' is more fitting. As some of them exemplified by 'intellects' – they have no definition, yet they have been defined, and they are among the conceptualizations (tasawwurat) for which knowledge of the 'species' is sought according to them. So it is known that conceptualizations are independent of definition. Rather, if it is possible to know these without definition, then knowledge of those species without definition is even more fitting, because they are closer to sense perception and their individuals are witnessed.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians' claim that definition is necessary for conceptualization. Ibn Taymiyya presents the first station of his critique. He argues that if things that do not fall under a genus – like the intellects ('uqul) – can be known without a definition, then species (anwa') are even more knowable without definition. The reason given is that species are closer to sensory experience and their individuals are observed. The implication is that if the logicians admit that some concepts can be known without definition, then they cannot consistently claim that all conceptualization depends on definition. This is a direct attack on the foundational role of definition in their epistemology.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Essential-Accidental Distinction
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the essential-accidental distinction is subjective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the essential-accidental distinction is subjective and evaluate its implications for definition theory.

Core Idea
Essential vs. accidental is not in the thing, but in the speaker's intention.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: the distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi) attributes. Logicians claim this distinction is objective, rooted in the reality of things. Ibn Taymiyya argues it is merely a function of how we use words and what we intend to convey. This lesson dissects his critique and its consequences for the concept of quiddity (mahiyya).

Guided Path
1
Identify the target
What logical distinction is Ibn Taymiyya critiquing? The distinction between essential and accidental attributes as objective features of reality.
2
Understand the semantic tools
How does he use 'tadammun' (implication) and 'luzum' (concomitance) to redefine the distinction? Essential = what is included by tadammun in the speaker's intention; accidental = what accompanies by luzum.
3
Trace the subjectivization
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why does this distinction not reflect objective reality? Because it depends on the speaker's intention, not on the thing itself.
4
Assess the broader claim
How does Ibn Taymiyya extend this critique to the concept of 'mahiyya' (quiddity) itself? He argues mahiyya is a mental construct, not something existing in external reality.
5
Evaluate the implications
If definitions do not capture objective essences, what happens to the science of definition (hadd) as the logicians conceive it? It becomes arbitrary or relative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما جعل بعض الصفات داخلة في حقيقة الموصوف وبعضها خارجةً فلا الحقائق الثابتة يعود إلى أمر [٧] حقيقي، وإنما يعود ذلك إلى جعل الداخل ما دَلَّ عليه اللفظ بـ «التضمن»، في الأذهان والخارج اللازم ما دلّ عليه اللفظ بـ «اللزوم»، فتعود الصفات الداخلة في الماهية إلى ما دخل في مراد المتكلم بلفظه، والخارجةُ اللازمة للماهية إلى ما يلزم مراده بلفظه . وهذا أمر يتبع مراد المتكلم فلا يعود إلى حقيقة ثابتة في نفس الأمر الموصوف. وقد بسطنا ألفاظهم في غير هذا الموضع وبينا ذلك بياناً مبسوطاً يبين أن ما سموه «الماهية» أمر يعود إلى ما يقدَّر في الأذهان، لا إلى ما يتحقق في الأعيان والمقدر في الأذهان بحسب ما يقدّره كلُّ أحد في ذهنه فيمتنع أن تكون الحقائق الموجودة تابعة لذلك.
As for making some attributes part of the essence of the described thing and others external, this does not return to an objective reality. Rather, that returns to making what is included that which the word denotes by 'implication' (tadammun) in the mind, and the necessary external attribute that which the word denotes by 'accompaniment' (luzum). So the essential attributes of the quiddity (mahiyya) return to what is included in the speaker's intention by his word, and the external necessary attributes return to what accompanies his intention by his word. And this is a matter that follows the speaker's intention and does not return to a fixed reality in the actual described thing. And we have elaborated their expressions elsewhere and explained that in a detailed exposition showing that what they call 'quiddity' is a matter that returns to what is mentally postulated, not to what exists in external reality, and the mentally postulated is according to what everyone postulates in his mind, so it is impossible for existing realities to be subordinate to that.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi) attributes. He argues that this distinction is not based on objective reality but stems from the speaker's intention when using a word. The attributes considered 'essential' are those that the speaker intends to include by the implication (tadammun) of the word, while 'accidental' attributes are those that accompany the word by necessary concomitance (luzum). Therefore, the determination of which attributes are essential is subjective, varying with the speaker's purpose. Ibn Taymiyya then expands: the concept of 'quiddity' (mahiyya) itself is a mental construct, not something existing in the external world. Consequently, the logicians cannot claim that definitions capture objective essences.

Key Terms
التضمن
اللزوم
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The empirical observation of the attribute

Key Takeaway
The essential-accidental distinction is not a discovery of objective reality, but a reflection of the speaker's conceptual organisation.

The Vicious Circle in Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definitions involve circularity because understanding component words presupposes prior conceptualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's circularity argument that definitions cannot be the source of new conceptualization.

Core Idea
Definitions presuppose concepts; therefore they cannot be the original source of knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that definitions provide new conceptualization (taswir). He shows that understanding a definition requires prior knowledge of its component words, which in turn requires prior conceptualization of their meanings. This creates a vicious circle, proving that definitions are not the origin of concepts. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about definition
The logicians hold that a definition (hadd) gives a depiction (taswir) of the essence of a thing, allowing one to conceive it for the first time. Ibn Taymiyya targets this claim.
The claim is implicit in the passage: definitions are supposed to teach new concepts.
2
Analyze the components of a definition
A definition is composed of words, each of which denotes a meaning. For example, 'rational animal' uses 'animal' and 'rational'. To understand the definition, one must already know the meanings of these component words.
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'if he was not knowledgeable before that of the individual words... he cannot understand the speech.'
3
Trace the circularity
Knowing that a word indicates a meaning requires prior conceptualization of that meaning (e.g., to know 'bread' means bread, you must have the concept of bread). Thus, understanding the definition would require prior concepts, yet the definition is supposed to give those concepts. This is a vicious circle (dawr qabli).
Ibn Taymiyya states: 'it is impossible to say that he conceived it only by hearing the word, because in that is a vicious circle.'
4
Extend the argument to names and definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues the same circularity applies to both names and definitions. Both indicate meanings; definitions only add detail. Therefore, definitions cannot be the original source of conceptualization for either.
He says: 'just as this is mentioned in the indication of names... so exactly does it apply to the indication of definitions.'

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع : يسمع الحد الذي هو مركب من ألفاظ كلٌّ منها لفظ دال على معنى. فإن لم يكن عارفاً قبل ذلك بمفردات تلك الألفاظ ودلالتها على معانيها لم يمكنه فهم الكلام . والعلم بأن اللفظ دالّ على المعنى أو موضوع له مسبوق بتصوّر المعنى، فمن لم يتصوّر مسمَّى الخبز والماء، والسماء والأرض، والأب والأم لم يعرف دلالة اللفظ عليه. وإذا كان متصوراً لمسمى اللفظ ومعناه قبل استماعه - وإن لم يعرف دلالة اللفظ عليه - امتنع أن يقال: إنه إنما تصوّره باستماع اللفظ، لأن في ذلك دَوْراً قَبْلياً، إذ يستلزم أن يقال : «لم يتصور المعنى حتى سمع وفَهمه، ولم يمكن أن يفهم المراد باللفظ حتى يكون قد تصوّر ذلك المعنى قبل ذلك». وهذا كما أنه مذكور في دلالة الأسماء على مسمياتها المفردة فهو بعينه وارد في دلالة الحدود على المحدودات، إذ كلاهما إنما يدل على معنى مفرد لكن الحد يفيد تفصيل ما دل عليه الاسم بالإجمال . وقد يكون فيه عند المنطقتين تفصيلُ صفاته المشتركة والمختصة، وإن كان في الحدود للمتكلمين في الحد طريق آخر، إذ لا يحدّون إلا بـ «الخاصة» المميزة الفاصلة دون المشتركة، بل يمنعون من التركيب الذي يوجبه المنطقيون وهو - لَعَمْري - أقرب إلى المقصود، كما سنبينه إن شاء الله تعالى ونبين أن فائدة الحدود التمييز لا التصوير . وإذا كان المطلوب التمييز فإنما ذاك بالمميّز فقط دون المشترك، ولأنه كلما كان من الحقيقة اسم أوجز وأجمعَ وأخص كان أحسن، كالأسماء. فليس الحد في الحقيقة إلا اسماً من الأسماء، أو اسمين أو ثلاثة، كقولك حيوان ناطق». وكذلك قيل في تعليم آدمَ الأسْمَاءَ كلَّهَا: تعليم حدودها، وهي من جنس الحدود المذكورة في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَجْدَرُ أَلَّا يَعْلَمُوا حُدُودَ مَا أَنزَلَ اللهُ عَلَى رَسُولِهِ ﴾ [سورة التوبة : ٩٧]
The seventh aspect: One hears the definition which is composed of words, each of which is a word denoting a meaning. For if he was not knowledgeable before that of the individual words and their indication of their meanings, he cannot understand the speech. And the knowledge that the word indicates the meaning or is posited for it is preceded by the conceptualization of the meaning. So whoever has not conceptualized the named of bread, water, sky, earth, father and mother does not know the indication of the word upon it. And if he has conceptualized the named of the word and its meaning before hearing it – even if he does not know the indication of the word upon it – it is impossible to say that he conceived it only by hearing the word, because in that is a vicious circle (dawr qabli). For it necessitates saying: 'He did not conceive the meaning until he heard and understood it, and he could not understand what is meant by the word until he had conceived that meaning before that.' And just as this is mentioned in the indication of names upon their individual named things, so exactly does it apply to the indication of definitions upon the defined things. For both indicate a simple meaning, but the definition gives a detailed account of what the name indicates in summary. And according to the two logicians (?), it may contain the detailing of its common and specific attributes. However, in definitions, according to the theologians (mutakallimun), there is another method: they only define by the specific property (khassa) that distinguishes and separates, without the common, and they prevent the composition which the logicians require. And it is – by my life – closer to the intended goal, as we will show if God wills, and we will show that the benefit of definitions is distinction (tamyiz), not depiction (taswir). And if the desired thing is distinction, then that is only by the distinguishing property alone without the common. And because whenever the name of the essence is more concise, comprehensive, and specific, it is better, like names. So the definition is in reality nothing but a name from among names, or two or three names, like your saying 'rational animal'. And similarly it was said in the teaching of Adam all the names: teaching their definitions, and they are of the same kind as the definitions mentioned in the verse: 'And they are more worthy of not knowing the limits (hudud) of what Allah has revealed to His Messenger' (Quran 9:97).
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is a dense passage presenting the seventh aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique. He argues that definitions are composed of words, each of which denotes a meaning. If the listener does not already know the meanings of those individual words, they cannot understand the definition. Moreover, knowing that a word indicates a meaning requires prior conceptualization of that meaning. This creates a circularity (dawr qabli): one cannot conceive the meaning of a word without first hearing and understanding it, but one cannot understand the word without already having the concept. Therefore, definitions cannot be the original source of conceptualization; they presuppose prior concepts. Ibn Taymiyya then extends this to the indication of names and definitions: both indicate simple meanings, but definitions provide the detail. He contrasts the method of the logicians with that of the mutakallimun (theologians): the mutakallimun define only by the specific property (khassa) that distinguishes, avoiding the complex compound definitions the logicians require. For Ibn Taymiyya, the true purpose of definition is distinction (tamyiz), not depiction (taswir) of the essence. To illustrate, he says a definition is essentially a name or a short phrase like 'rational animal'. He then mentions the teaching of Adam the names as teaching definitions, and quotes a Quranic verse about knowing the limits (hudud) of Allah's revelation, tying the linguistic practice to revelation.

Key Terms

الحد
التصوير

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why cannot a definition be the original source of conceptualization?
A) Because definitions are often inaccurate
B) Because understanding the definition's words requires prior concepts, creating a circularity
C) Because definitions are too long to be understood
D) Because definitions are only for names, not meanings

Key Takeaway
Definitions cannot teach new concepts because they presuppose the very concepts they aim to impart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Using Ibn Taymiyya's circularity argument, explain why this definition cannot be the source of the concept of iman for a person who has never heard it. What prior concepts must the listener already have?
?
