Distinction Over Depiction: The True Purpose of Definition
Ibn Taymiyya presents the mutakallimun's method of definition by specific property and argues that the true benefit is distinction (tamyiz).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the logicians' and mutakallimun's methods of definition and explain why Ibn Taymiyya holds that the true benefit of definition is distinction (tamyiz), not depiction (taswir).

Core Idea
The real benefit of definition is to distinguish (tamyiz), not to depict essence (taswir).

After exposing the circularity in the logicians' theory of definition, Ibn Taymiyya proposes an alternative: definitions are not for depicting essences but simply for distinguishing one thing from another. He aligns this with the method of the mutakallimun (theologians), who define only by the specific property (khassa). Let's explore this alternative and its implications for Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods of definition
Logicians define by combining genus and differentia (e.g., 'rational animal' for human). Mutakallimun define only by the specific property (khassa) that distinguishes, without including the common genus.
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'the theologians... only define by the specific property that distinguishes and separates, without the common.'
2
Understand the purpose: tamyiz vs. taswir
Taswir (depiction) aims to give a mental picture of the essence. Tamyiz (distinction) aims only to differentiate the defined thing from all others. Ibn Taymiyya argues the latter is sufficient and more practical.
He states: 'the benefit of definitions is distinction (tamyiz), not depiction (taswir).'
3
Analyze why the mutakallimun's method is preferable
Since the goal is distinction, only the distinguishing property is needed. Adding the genus is redundant and can lead to circularity or unnecessary complexity. A simple, concise definition is better, like a name or a short phrase.
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'whenever the name of the essence is more concise, comprehensive, and specific, it is better, like names.'
4
Connect to Islamic precedent
Ibn Taymiyya links this understanding to the teaching of Adam's names (which are definitions of things) and the Quranic concept of 'hudud' (limits/revealed definitions). This shows that definitions in the Islamic tradition are about distinguishing boundaries set by revelation, not human philosophical speculation.
He references the verse: 'And they are more worthy of not knowing the limits (hudud) of what Allah has revealed to His Messenger.'

The Mutakallimun's Method of Definition by Khāṣṣa
Mutakallimun's method of definition by khāṣṣa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the mutakallimun's method of definition using only the distinctive property (khāṣṣa) and contrast it with the logicians' composite definition, using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning.

Core Idea
Definition is discrimination, not depiction: only the distinctive property is needed.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents a fundamental disagreement between the logicians and the mutakallimun on how to define things. The logicians require a composite definition including both common and specific attributes, typically genus and differentia. The mutakallimun, by contrast, insist on defining solely by the distinctive property (khāṣṣa) that uniquely distinguishes the thing. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this method is closer to the true purpose of definition, which is discrimination (tamyīz), not producing a mental image of the essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing methods
Locate in the Arabic where Ibn Taymiyya describes the logicians' method (involving common and specific attributes) and the mutakallimun's method (using only the distinctive property). Notice the contrast between 'composite' and 'by the distinctive alone'.
The logicians require both common (mushtaraka) and specific (mukhtaṣṣa) attributes; the mutakallimun use only the distinctive (khāṣṣa).
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians
Ibn Taymiyya says the mutakallimun 'forbid the composition that the logicians require' and that their method 'is closer to the intended purpose.' Identify the phrase that indicates this preference and his agreement with the mutakallimun.
The phrase 'wa huwa - la-ʿamrī - aqrab ilā al-maqṣūd' shows Ibn Taymiyya's endorsement.
3
Grasp the purpose of definition: tamyīz vs. taṣwīr
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states that 'the benefit of definitions is discrimination (tamyīz), not depiction (taṣwīr).' Reflect on why discrimination only requires the distinguishing feature, not common features.
Tamyīz is about telling things apart; common features do not help in distinguishing.
4
Evaluate the analogy with names
Ibn Taymiyya says that 'definition in reality is nothing but a name from among names, or two names, or three.' Consider the example 'rational animal' as a two-name definition for 'human'. Why does this support his view?
A name summarizes the distinctive reality; a definition is just an equivalent expression.
5
Apply the Qur'anic example of teaching Adam the names
Ibn Taymiyya interprets the teaching of 'all the names' to Adam as teaching their definitions. How does this connect to the idea that definitions are about discrimination?
Teaching names is teaching the distinguishing properties of things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يكون فيه عند المنطقتين تفصيلُ [٨] صفاته المشتركة والمختصة، وإن كان في الحدود للمتكلمين في الحد طريق آخر، إذ لا يحدّون إلا بـ «الخاصة» المميزة الفاصلة دون المشتركة، بل يمنعون من التركيب الذي يوجبه المنطقيون وهو - لَعَمْري - أقرب إلى المقصود، كما سنبينه إن شاء الله تعالى ونبين أن فائدة الحدود التمييز لا التصوير. وإذا كان المطلوب التمييز فإنما ذاك بالمميّز فقط دون المشترك، ولأنه كلما كان من الحقيقة اسم أوجز وأجمعَ وأخص كان أحسن، كالأسماء. فليس الحد في الحقيقة إلا اسماً الأسماء، أو اسمين أو ثلاثة، كقولك حيوان ناطق». وكذلك قيل في تعليم آدمَ الأسْمَاءَ كلَّهَا: تعليم حدودها
And among the logicians it may involve a detailing of its common and specific attributes, although among the mutakallimun in definitions there is another method, for they do not define except by the distinctive distinguishing property (al-khāṣṣa) without the common [properties]; rather they forbid the composition that the logicians require, and it - by my life - is closer to the intended purpose, as we will explain, God willing, and we will show that the benefit of definitions is discrimination (tamyīz), not depiction (taṣwīr). And if what is sought is discrimination, then that is only by the distinguisher alone without the common, and because whenever the name from the reality is more concise, more comprehensive, and more specific, it is better, like names. So definition in reality is nothing but a name from among names, or two names, or three, like your saying 'rational animal'. And likewise it was said concerning the teaching of all the names to Adam: it was the teaching of their definitions.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage describes a key methodological difference between the logicians (manṭiqiyyūn) and the mutakallimūn (theologians) concerning definition (ḥadd). The logicians insist on a composite definition that includes both common (mushtaraka) and specific (mukhtaṣṣa) attributes, typically genus and differentia. In contrast, the mutakallimūn define using only the distinctive property (khāṣṣa) that uniquely distinguishes the thing from everything else, and they do not include common attributes. Ibn Taymiyya endorses the mutakallimūn's approach, calling it 'closer to the intended purpose' (aqrab ilā al-maqṣūd). He then states that the true benefit of definitions is discrimination (tamyīz) – telling things apart – not the production of new concepts (taṣwīr). For discrimination, only the distinguishing feature is needed; adding common features only makes the definition longer and less precise. He gives an example: defining 'human' as 'rational animal' is essentially using a name ('rational animal') that is equivalent to the name 'human'. This aligns with the idea that teaching Adam the names of all things was in fact teaching their definitions (ḥudūd). Thus, definitions are simply equivalent names that summarize the reality.

Key Terms
الخاصة
تمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the main benefit (fāʾida) of definitions?
A) Depicting the essence (taṣwīr al-dhāt)
B) Teaching new concepts
C) Discrimination (tamyīz)
D) Providing a complete list of all attributes

Key Takeaway
For Ibn Taymiyya and the mutakallimūn, definition is not about painting a complete picture but about drawing a clear boundary around a thing using its unique mark.

Naqḍ in Definitions: The Conditions of Ṭard and ʿAks
Ibn Taymiyya explains naqḍ and muʿāraḍa in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the conditions of ṭard and ʿaks to test whether a definition is complete, and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique that testing definitions presupposes prior knowledge of the definiendum.

Core Idea
To test a definition, you must already know what it defines.

Ibn Taymiyya here takes the logicians' own weapon—the methods of naqḍ (refutation) and muʿāraḍa (counter-argument)—and turns it against them. He explains that the validity of a definition depends on two conditions: ṭard (coextensiveness) and ʿaks (converse). But crucially, to apply these tests, you must already have an independent grasp of the definiendum. This reveals a circularity in the logicians' claim that definitions generate new conceptual knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for a valid definition
Find in the Arabic the terms ṭard and ʿaks. Ṭard means wherever the definition exists, the definiendum exists; ʿaks means wherever the definition is absent, the definiendum is absent.
2
Test the examples given
Apply the conditions: why does 'human is animal' fail ṭard? Because 'animal' includes non-human animals, so the definition is broader. Why does 'human is Arab' fail ʿaks? Because non-Arab humans exist, so the definition is too narrow.
These examples illustrate naqḍ in ṭard and ʿaks respectively.
3
Extend the concept of naqḍ
Ibn Taymiyya says naqḍ applies also to evidence (dalīl), universal propositions (qaḍiyya kulliyya), and causes (ʿilla). Note the differences: for evidence and universal propositions, conversion is not always required unless they are correlates; for definitions, conversion is mandatory; for causes, if complete it must be coextensive, if incomplete the judgment may lag.
This shows the logicians' own standards for different logical items.
4
Uncover the presupposition
Reflect: to test whether a definition meets ṭard and ʿaks, what must you already know about the definiendum? You must know what instances belong to it and what do not. Thus, definitions cannot be the source of new conception; they only clarify known concepts.
This is Ibn Taymiyya's critical point.

Source Text

الوجه العاشر: إنهم يقولون إن للمعترض أن يطعن على حد الحادّ بـ«النقض» و «المعارضة». «النقض» إما في «الطَّرد»، وإما في العكس». أما «الطرد فهو أنه حيث وجد الحد وجد المحدود، فيكون الحد مانعاً، فإذا بين وجود الحد، ولا محدود، لم يكن مطّرداً، ولا مانعاً بل دخل فيه غيره، كما لو قال في حدّ«الإنسان» إنه «الحيوان» وأما العكس وهو أن يكون حيث انتفى الحدُّ انتفى المحدود لكون الحد جامعاً. فإذا لم يكن جامعاً، انتفى الحدّ مع بقاء بعض المحدود، كما لو قال في حد «الإنسان» إنه «العربي». ، فلا يكون الحدّ منعكساً، ولو استعمل لفظ الطرد في موضع العكس لكان سائغاً. والمقصود أنه لا بد من اتفاق الحدّ والمحدود في العموم والخصوص، فلا بد أن يكون مطابقاً للمحدود، لا يدخل فيه ما ليس من المحدود ولا يخرج منه ما هو من المحدود، فمتى كان أحدهما أعمّ كان باطلاً بالاتفاق، وسمي ذلك «نقضاً». / فإن «النقض» يرد على الحد، والدليل، والقضية الكلية، والعلة. لكن الدليل والقضية الكلية لا يجب فيهما الانعكاس إلا بسبب منفصل، مثل المتلازمين إذا استدل بأحدهما على الآخر، فهنا يكون الدليلُ مطَّرداً منعكساً. وأما الحد فلا بد فيه من الانعكاس. و«العلة» إن كانت تامّةً وجب طَرْدُها، وإن لم تكن تامة جاز تخلُّفُ الحكم عنها لفوات شرط، أو وجود مانع، ويسمى ذلك «تخصيصاً» و«نقضاً». وأما وجود الحكم بلا علة
The tenth aspect: They say that the objector may attack the definer's definition by 'naqḍ' (refutation) and 'muʿāraḍa' (counter-argument). 'Naqḍ' is either in 'ṭard' (coextensiveness) or 'ʿaks' (converse). As for 'ṭard', it is that wherever the definition is found, the definiendum is found, so the definition is exclusive (māniʿ). Thus if the existence of the definition is shown while there is no definiendum, it is not coextensive (muṭṭarid) nor exclusive; rather, something else enters into it, as if one says in the definition of 'human' that it is 'animal'. As for 'ʿaks', it is that wherever the definition is absent, the definiendum is absent, because the definition is comprehensive (jāmiʿ). So if it is not comprehensive, the definition is absent while some of the definiendum remains, as if one says in the definition of 'human' that it is 'Arab'. Then the definition is not converse (munʿakis), though using the term 'ṭard' in the place of 'ʿaks' would be permissible. The point is that definition and definiendum must agree in generality and particularity; it must correspond to the definiendum, not including what is not from the definiendum nor excluding what is from it. Whenever one is more general, it is invalid by consensus, and that is called 'naqḍ'. For 'naqḍ' applies to the definition, the evidence (dalīl), the universal proposition (al-qaḍiyya al-kulliyya), and the cause (ʿilla). But in the evidence and the universal proposition, conversion (inʿikās) is not required except for a separate reason, like two correlates when one is used as evidence for the other; here the evidence is coextensive and converse. As for the definition, conversion is necessary. As for the 'cause' (ʿilla), if it is complete (tāmma), its coextensiveness is required; if it is not complete, the judgment may lag behind it due to the absence of a condition or the presence of an impediment, and that is called 'takhṣīṣ' (specification) and 'naqḍ'. And as for the existence of the judgment without a cause...
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is the tenth objection, where Ibn Taymiyya explains the logicians' own method of criticizing definitions through naqḍ (counterexample) and muʿāraḍa (counter-argument). He describes two types of naqḍ: naqḍ in ṭard (coextensiveness) and naqḍ in ʿaks (converse). Ṭard means that wherever the definition is found, the definiendum must be found; if there is an instance where the definition applies but the definiendum does not, the definition is not coextensive (muṭṭarid) and fails to be exclusive (māniʿ). Example: defining 'human' as 'animal' fails because 'animal' includes animals that are not human (horses, etc.). ʿAks means that wherever the definition is absent, the definiendum must be absent; if there are cases where the definiendum is found without the definition, the definition is not comprehensive (jāmiʿ). Example: defining 'human' as 'Arab' fails because non-Arab humans exist. The definition must perfectly correspond to the definiendum in generality and particularity; otherwise it is invalid and 'naqḍ' applies. Ibn Taymiyya then extends the concept of naqḍ to other logical items: evidence (dalīl), universal proposition (qaḍiyya kulliyya), and cause (ʿilla). For evidence and universal propositions, conversion is not always required unless they are correlates (mutalāzimān). For definitions, conversion (inʿikās) is mandatory. For a cause (ʿilla), if it is complete (tāmma), it must be coextensive (mustawfiyya); if incomplete, the judgment may fail due to missing conditions or impediments, and that is called takhṣīṣ (specification) and still naqḍ. The passage ends mid-sentence on page 53. Ibn Taymiyya's point is that the very act of criticizing a definition presupposes that one already knows the definiendum independently of the definition. Thus, definitions cannot be the source of new conception; they must be tested against prior knowledge.

Key Terms
نقض
طرد
عكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the definition 'human is animal' considered invalid?
A) Because it fails in ʿaks (converse) – some humans are not animals
B) Because it fails in ṭard (coextensiveness) – there are animals that are not humans
C) Because 'human' is not a species of 'animal'
D) Because the definition is too short

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must match its definiendum in generality and particularity, failing either condition results in naqḍ (invalidation).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Take the fiqh definition 'Zakat is a fixed portion of wealth given to the poor.' Does this definition meet the condition of ṭard (coextensiveness)? Does every instance of zakat fit? Does it meet ʿaks (converse)? Does every instance of a fixed portion given to the poor count as zakat? Identify which condition fails.
?
Task 2: Ibn Taymiyya argues that to test a definition, you already know the definiendum. Choose a definition from the Hanafi school (e.g., 'Salah is specific actions and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim') and explain what prior knowledge is needed to test its ṭard and ʿaks.

Knowledge Through Senses: Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemology
Author states that all existents are perceived by external and internal senses, independent of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that sensory perception is independent of logical definition and explain how knowledge of the unseen is acquired through analogy.

Core Idea
All knowledge begins with senses, not definitions.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a foundational claim of Aristotelian logic: that definitions (hudud) are necessary for conceptual knowledge. In this passage, he argues that both external and internal senses are the real sources of all perception, and that definitions are neither required nor sufficient for knowing existents. This sets the stage for his broader critique of Greek logic in favor of a natural, experience-based epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of senses
Read the first sentence and note the division: external senses (zahira) like taste, color, smell; and internal senses (batina) like hunger, love, joy. Ibn Taymiyya claims all existents are perceived through these alone.
Contrast with logicians who prioritize conceptual definitions over sensory perception.
2
Distinguish particular vs. universal perception
Notice he says each perception can be specific (mu'ayyan) or absolute/general (mutlaq/amm). This addresses the logicians' concern about universals — they are still sensory in origin.
Ibn Taymiyya does not deny universals; he grounds them in sensory experience.
3
Extract the independence-from-definition claim
He states 'all these perceptions are independent of definition (hadd)'. Identify the Arabic phrase 'غنية عن الحد' and connect it to his rejection of the logicians' view that definition is essential for knowledge.
This is the core epistemological claim of the passage.
4
Analyze the method for knowing the absent (gha'ib)
The final part explains that what is not directly perceived is known through 'qiyas' (analogy) and 'i'tibar' (consideration) of what has been witnessed. This replaces the logician's reliance on syllogisms from definitions.
Qiyas here is not Aristotelian syllogism but analogical reasoning common in Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الأول : جميع الموجودات يتصورها الإنسان بمشاعره فقط كالطعم، واللون والريح والأجسام التي تحمل هذه الصفات؛ وإما أن يتصورها جميع بمشاعره الباطنة كما يَتَصَوَّر الأمور الحسية الباطنة الوجدية، مثل الجوع والشبع، والحب والبغض، والفرح والحزن، واللذة والألم، والإرادة والكراهية والعلم والجهل، وأمثال ذلك. وكل من الأمرين قد يتصوره معيّناً، وقد يتصوره [٩] مطلقاً أو عاماً. وهذه التصورات جميعها غنيّة عن الحد ولا يمكنه تصور شيء بدون مشاعره الظاهرة والباطنة وما غاب عنه يعرفه بالقياس والاعتبار بما شاهده . الموجودات الإنسان بمشاعره فقط
First station: All existents are perceived by man only through his senses, such as taste, color, smell, and the bodies that bear these qualities; or he perceives them all through his internal senses as he perceives internal, emotional sensory matters, like hunger and satiety, love and hate, joy and sadness, pleasure and pain, will and aversion, knowledge and ignorance, and the like. And each of the two [types] he may perceive as particular, or he may perceive it [9] as absolute or general. And all these perceptions are independent of definition, and he cannot perceive anything without his external and internal senses, and what is absent from him he knows through analogy and consideration of what he has witnessed. Existents, man, through his senses only.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological stance. It begins by stating that all existents are perceived by man only through his senses, then divides perception into external senses (like taste, color, smell) and internal senses (like emotions and inner states such as hunger, love, etc.). He notes that each of these can be perceived as particular or as general (universal). Then he makes a key claim: all these perceptions are independent of definition (had). He argues that man cannot perceive anything without his senses, and what is absent from him he knows through qiyas (analogy) and i'tibar (consideration) of what he has witnessed. This directly challenges the logicians' claim that definitions are necessary for knowledge of universals. The passage is foundational for Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic.

حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By logical definition (hadd)
B) By analogy and consideration (qiyas and i'tibar) based on what they have witnessed
C) By innate knowledge
D) By revelation only

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya grounds all knowledge in sensory perception, making definitions secondary and analogical reasoning the tool for the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that a child knows hunger without needing a definition. Use the example of a toddler who cries for food but cannot define 'hunger' — how does this support his claim that perceptions are independent of hadd?

Testing Definitions: Tard and 'Aks
Author defines the method of criticizing definitions through naqd in tard or 'aks

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate any given definition by applying the tests of tard (whether the definition is too broad) and 'aks (whether it is too narrow) to determine its validity.

Core Idea
A correct definition must perfectly match its defined term in extension — no more, no less.

In his critique of the logicians, Ibn Taymiyya explains how an objector can attack a definition by showing it fails in either tard or 'aks. Tard checks if the definition includes everything it should (but may be too broad), while 'aks checks if it excludes what it should not (but may be too narrow). Mastering these two tests equips you to critically examine definitions used in Islamic sciences, from fiqh to kalam, and avoid flawed reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its defined term
Read the passage and locate the proposed definition and the term it claims to define. For example, the passage gives 'human' defined as 'animal' or as 'Arab'.
The defined term is the 'muhaddid' (محدود), the definition is the 'hadd' (حد).
2
Apply the tard test: is the definition co-extensive?
Ask: does the definition apply only to the defined, or does it also apply to things outside? If the definition is found where the defined is not, tard fails. Example: 'animal' includes horses and birds, not just humans, so it fails tard.
Tard ensures no extraneous items are included (الطرد: أن يكون حيث وجد الحد وجد المحدود).
3
Apply the 'aks test: is the definition convertible?
Ask: whenever the definition is absent, is the defined also absent? If the definition is missing but the defined still exists, 'aks fails. Example: 'Arab' excludes non-Arab humans, so 'aks fails.
'Aks ensures no members of the defined are excluded (العكس: أن يكون حيث انتفى الحد انتفى المحدود).
4
Determine the validity of the definition
If either test fails, the definition is invalid (naqd). Only a definition that passes both tests is acceptable. The author notes that definition requires convertibility, unlike proofs where convertibility may not be necessary.
Naqd (نقض) is the rebuttal showing the definition fails tard or 'aks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه العاشر : العاشر: إنهم يقولون إن للمعترض أن يطعن على حد الحادّ بـ«النقض» لا يمكن و «المعارضة». و«النقض» إما في «الطَّرد»، وإما في العكس». أما «الطرد فهو أنه حيث وجد إلا بعد تصور المحدود بدون الحد الحد وجد المحدود، فيكون الحد مانعاً، فإذا بين وجود الحد، ولا محدود، لم يكن مطّرداً، ولا مانعاً بل دخل فيه غيره، كما لو قال في حدّ«الإنسان» إنه «الحيوان» وأما العكس وهو أن يكون حيث انتفى الحدُّ انتفى المحدود لكون الحد جامعاً. فإذا لم النقض يكن جامعاً، انتفى الحدّ مع بقاء بعض المحدود، كما لو قال في حد «الإنسان» إنه «العربي». ، فلا يكون الحدّ منعكساً، ولو استعمل لفظ الطرد في موضع العكس لكان سائغاً. والمقصود أنه لا بد من اتفاق الحدّ والمحدود في العموم والخصوص، فلا بد أن يكون مطابقاً للمحدود، لا يدخل فيه ما ليس من المحدود ولا يخرج منه ما هو من المحدود، فمتى كان أحدهما أعمّ كان باطلاً بالاتفاق، وسمي ذلك «نقضاً». فإن «النقض» يرد على الحد، والدليل، والقضية الكلية، والعلة. لكن الدليل والقضية الكلية لا يجب فيهما الانعكاس إلا بسبب منفصل، مثل المتلازمين إذا استدل بأحدهما على الآخر، فهنا يكون الدليلُ مطَّرداً منعكساً. وأما الحد فلا بد فيه من الانعكاس.
The tenth aspect: They say that the objector can attack the definer's definition through 'naqd' (rebuttal) and 'mu'arada' (opposition). And 'naqd' is either in 'tard' (co-extensiveness) or in 'aks' (converse). As for 'tard', it is that wherever the definition is found, the defined is found, so the definition is preventing. If the definition is found without the defined, then it is not co-extensive, nor preventing, but rather something else enters it, as if one said in defining 'human' that it is 'animal'. And as for 'aks', it is that wherever the definition is absent, the defined is absent because the definition is comprehensive. If it is not comprehensive, the definition is absent while some of the defined remains, as if one said in defining 'human' that it is 'Arab'. Thus the definition is not convertible. And if one used the term 'tard' in place of 'aks', it would be permissible. And the point is that it is necessary for the definition and the defined to agree in generality and particularity, so it must be conformable to the defined, not including what is not of the defined nor excluding what is of the defined. So when one of them is more general, it is invalid by agreement, and this is called 'naqd'. Indeed, 'naqd' applies to definition, proof, universal proposition, and cause. But proof and universal proposition do not require convertibility except due to a separate reason, like the two necessary correlates, when one is used as proof for the other, then the proof is co-extensive and convertible. As for definition, it must have convertibility.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage explains the method of criticizing a logical definition (hadd) through 'naqd' (rebuttal). The author states that an objector can attack a definition by pointing out a failure in either 'tard' or 'aks'. Tard means that wherever the definition applies, the defined applies; if the definition applies but the defined does not, the definition is not co-extensive (muttaqid) and allows entry of non-defined things. Example: if one defines 'human' as 'animal', the definition is broader. 'Aks' means that wherever the definition is absent, the defined is absent; if the definition is absent but the defined still exists, the definition is not comprehensive (jami'). Example: if one defines 'human' as 'Arab', the definition is narrower. The author emphasizes that a correct definition must be precisely equivalent to the defined in extension. He then notes that naqd applies also to proofs and universal propositions, but those do not require convertibility unless there is a specific relation (like necessary correlates). In contrast, a definition absolutely requires convertibility (in'ikas). This distinction is crucial for understanding Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' insistence on definitions.

طرد
عكس
مطرد
منعكس

Checkpoint

A) Defining 'human' as 'animal'
B) Defining 'human' as 'Arab'
C) Defining 'human' as 'rational animal'
D) Defining 'human' as 'mortal'

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be co-extensive (tard) and convertible ('aks) with the defined term; failing either is a naqd (rebuttal) against the definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Test the following definition from fiqh: 'Salah (prayer) is the act of reciting Qur'an and bowing.' Apply the tard test: does this definition include any act that is not salah? (Consider that reciting Qur'an alone is not salah.) Apply the 'aks test: does this definition exclude any valid salah? (Consider that salah also includes prostration and concluding taslim.) Is this a valid definition?
?
In kalam, the definition of 'iman' (faith) is often debated. One classical definition is 'iman is belief in the heart, speech on the tongue, and action of the limbs.' Test this definition for tard and 'aks: does it include any non-iman? Does it exclude any valid iman?

Ibn Taymiyya on the Relativity of Self-Evident Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya argues that self-evident knowledge is relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the badihi/nazari classification is relative and identify its implications for the necessity of definitions.

Core Idea
Self-evidence is not a fixed property of knowledge; it varies between people.

In classical logic, some concepts are considered self-evident (badihi) and need no definition. But is this classification absolute? Ibn Taymiyya challenges this assumption by arguing that what is self-evident depends on the individual's prior knowledge and experience. In this lesson, you will unpack his argument step by step: from his main claim that badihi/nazari is relative, through the empirical evidence he provides, to the conclusion that definitions are not universally required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya states that badihi/nazari is among relative (idafi) matters, not necessary properties (lazim) of the known. What does he mean by 'idafi'? He is denying that a piece of knowledge is inherently self-evident or theoretical; it depends on the knower.
The phrase 'من الأمور الإضافية' is key.
2
Examine the empirical evidence
Ibn Taymiyya gives several examples: Zayd may be certain of what 'Amr considers probable; what is self-evident to Zayd may require inference for another; sensory knowledge for one is transmitted for another. These show that the same proposition can be badihi for one and nazari for another.
Notice the contrast between 'حسّيات' and 'متواترات', and 'ظنّيّات' and 'مجهولات'.
3
Connect to the necessity of definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that if badihi is relative, then a definition is only needed when a concept is not self-evident for a particular person. Since logicians acknowledge some concepts are self-evident (to avoid circularity), they cannot claim that all concepts require definitions.
He uses the logicians' own admission that some concepts are badihi to argue that definition is not universally necessary.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that it is a great mistake to think that a particular knowledge is badihi or nazari for everyone. The reality is that people vary greatly in perception. Therefore, a definition is not an essential prerequisite for concept-formation.
This directly challenges the foundational role of definitions in Aristotelian logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الأول : كون العلم بديهياً أو نظرياً من الأمور الاضافية وليس من لوازم المعلومات . وأما المعارضة للحدّ بحدّ آخر فظاهر . المعارضة فإذا كان المستمع للحد يُبطله بـ«النقض» تارة، وبـالمعارضة» أخرى، ومعلوم أن كليهما لا يمكن إلا بعد تصور المحدودِ عُلم أنه يمكن تصوّر المحدود بدون الحدّ . وهو المطلوب. الحادي عشر : أن يقال: هم معترفون بأن من التصورات ما يكون بديهياً لا يحتاج إلى حد، وإلا لزم الدَّوْر أو التسلسل . الوجه الحادي عشر : البداهة وحينئذ فيقال: كون العلم بديهياً أو نظرياً هو من الأمور النسبية الإضافية، مثل المعلومات كون «القضية يقينية أو ظنّيّة، إذ قد يتيقّن زيد ما يظنه ،عمرو، وقد يبده زيداً من المعاني ما لا يعرفه غيره إلا بالنظر، وقد يكون حسّياً لزيد من العلوم ما هو خَبَري عند عمرو. وإن كان كثير من الناس يحسب أن كون العلم المعيَّن ضرورياً أو كسبيّاً، أو بديهياً، أو نظرياً، هو من الأمور اللازمة له بحيث يشترك في ذلك جميع الناس. وهذا غلط عظيم، وهو مخالف للواقع . فإن مَنْ رأى الأمور الموجودة في مكانه وزمانه كانت عنده من الحسيات / «المشاهدات» ، وهي عند مَنْ علمها بالتواتر من المتواترات»، وقد يكون بعض الناس إنما علمها بخبر ظنّي فتكون عنده من باب الظنّيّات»، ومَنْ لم يسمعها فهي عنده من «المجهولات». وكذلك العقليات»، فإن الناس يتفاوتون في الإدراك تفاوتاً لا يكاد ينضبطُ طَرَفاه. ولبعضهم من العلم البديهي عنده والضروري ما ينفيه غيره، أو يشكّ فيه . وهذا بين في التصورات» و«التصديقات». التصورات وإذا كان ذلك من الأمور النسبية والإضافية أمكن أن يكون بديهياً عند بعض الناس من التصوّرات ما ليس بديهياً لغيره فلا يحتاج إلى «حدّ»، وهذا هو الواقع .
The first station: Knowledge being self-evident or theoretical is among relative and accidental matters, not necessary properties of the known. As for countering a definition with another definition, it is clear. Countering: When the listener refutes the definition sometimes by 'naqd' and other times by 'mu'arada', and it is known that both are impossible except after conceiving the defined, it is known that the defined can be conceived without the definition. This is what is required. The eleventh point: It is said: They acknowledge that among concepts, some are self-evident and do not need a definition; otherwise, circularity or infinite regress would follow. The eleventh aspect: Self-evidence. Then it is said: Knowledge being self-evident or theoretical is among relative and accidental matters, like the fact that a proposition is certain or probable, for Zayd may be certain of what 'Amr considers probable, and what is self-evident to Zayd of meanings may be unknown to another except through inference, and what is sensory for Zayd may be transmitted for 'Amr. Many people suppose that a particular knowledge being necessary or acquired, or self-evident or theoretical, is among the necessary properties of it such that all people share in that. This is a great mistake, contrary to reality. For whoever sees the things existing in his place and time, they are for him among the sensory/observed; for those who know them by tawatur, they are among the mutawatirat; and some people may know them by probable report, so they are among the probable; and those who have not heard them, they are among the unknowns. Similarly for intellectual matters: people vary in perception to an extent whose extremes can hardly be grasped. For some, what is self-evident and necessary knowledge for them is denied or doubted by others. This is clear in concepts and assents. Concepts: And if this is among relative and accidental matters, it is possible that what is self-evident to some people among concepts may not be self-evident to others, so they do not need a definition. This is reality.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion arguing that the classification of knowledge as self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nazari) is relative and accidental, not an inherent property of the known. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the topic: knowledge's badihi/nazari status is relative. He then discusses how definitions can be challenged by naqd and mu'arada, but these challenges presuppose conceiving the defined, showing that definition is not necessary for conception. The eleventh point asserts that logicians recognize some concepts as self-evident and needing no definition, to avoid circularity or regress. However, Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that what is self-evident varies among individuals: one person's certainty is another's probability; one person's sensory experience is another's hearsay. He gives examples: sensory observations vs. mutawatirat; probable reports vs. unknowns; intellectual differences in perception. Thus, a definition is not universally required because badihi differs per person. This is a critique of the logicians' claim that definitions are essential for all concepts.

Key Terms
بديهي
نظري
اضافي
ضروري
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because definitions are always circular.
B) Because what is self-evident varies among individuals, and some concepts are self-evident for some but not others.
C) Because definitions are impossible for certain concepts.
D) Because the logicians reject definitions.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's argument that badihi/nazari is relative undermines the logicians' assumption that definitions are necessary for all concepts.

Definitions Only Distinguish, Not Conceptualize
Ibn Taymiyya denies that definitions conceptualize essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two competing views on the function of definitions (hadd) and explain why Ibn Taymiyya argues that definitions only provide tamyiz (distinction).

Core Idea
Definitions do not reveal essences; they only separate one thing from another.

Ibn Taymiyya opens the second station with a direct challenge to Greek logic: definitions do not give us mental images of essences. He states that the verified investigators agree that the only benefit of a definition is distinguishing the defined from other things, just like a name. This is a fundamental departure from the logicians' claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the question of the second station: 'هل يمكن تصور الأشياء بالحدود' (can things be conceptualized through definitions?).
This is the claim he will refute.
2
Locate the two opposing claims
One claim is from the logicians (taswir of essence), the other from the verified investigators (tamyiz only). Underline the phrases that state each.
3
Analyze the phrase 'كالاسم'
Ibn Taymiyya says a definition is like a name. What does a name do? It points out without explaining essence. That is the function of hadd as well.
This shows the radical reduction of definition's role.
4
Identify the source of the logicians' claim
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly attributes the taswir claim to 'أهل المنطق اليوناني' (people of Greek logic) and 'أتباع أرسطو' (followers of Aristotle).
This is a genealogical critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني: وهو أنه هل يمكن تصور الأشياء بالحدود؟ فيقال: المحققون من النظار يعلمون أن الحد فائدته التمييز بين المحدود وغيره كالاسم. ليس فائدته تصوير المحدود وتعريف حقيقته؛ وإنما يدعي هذا أهل المنطق اليوناني، أتباع أرسطو، ومن سلك سبيلهم وحذا حذوهم تقليداً لهم من الإسلاميين وغيرهم. فأما جماهير أهل النظر والكلام من المسلمين وغيرهم فعلى خلاف هذا. وإنما دخل هذا في كلام من تكلم في أصول الدين والفقه بعد أبي حامد في أواخر المئة الخامسة وأوائل المئة السادسة. فإن أبا حامدٍ وَضَعَ مقدمة منطقية في أوّل «المستصفى»، وزعم أن من لم يُحِطْ بها علماً فلا ثقة له بشيء من علومه. وصنف في ذلك «محك النظر» و «مغيار العلم»؛ وداما اشتدّت به ثقته. وأعجَبُ من ذلك أنه وضع الكلام على كتاب سمّاه «القسطاس المستقيم» ونسبه إلى أنه تعلمه من الأنبياء، وإنما تعلمه من ابن سينا، وابن سينا تعلّمه من كتب أرسطو. وهؤلاء الذين تكلموا في «الأصول» بعد أبي حامد هم الذين تكلموا في «الحدود» بطريقة أهل المنطق اليوناني. وأما سائر طوائف النظار من جميع الطوائف - المعتزلة، والأشعرية، والكرَّاميَّة، والشيعة، وغيرهم ممن صنف في هذا الشأن من أتباع الأئمة الأربعة وغيرهم فعندهم إنما تفيد الحدودُ «التمييز بين المحدود وغيره». بل أكثرهم لا يسوغون الحدَّ.
As for the second station: whether things can be conceptualized through definitions? It is said: The verified investigators among theorists know that the benefit of a definition is distinguishing between the defined and others, like a name. Its benefit is not to form a mental image of the defined or to define its essence; rather, this is claimed only by the people of Greek logic, the followers of Aristotle, and those who follow their path and imitate them in blind following among Muslims and others. As for the majority of theorists and theologians among Muslims and others, they are contrary to this. This only entered into the speech of those who spoke in Usul al-Din and Fiqh after Abu Hamid in the late fifth century and early sixth century. For Abu Hamid placed a logical introduction at the beginning of al-Mustasfa, and claimed that whoever does not encompass it thoroughly, there is no reliability in any of his sciences. And he composed regarding that 'Mihakk al-Nazar' and 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm'; and his confidence in it grew excessively. And more astonishing than that, he composed a book called 'al-Qistas al-Mustaqim' and attributed it to having learned it from the prophets, but he only learned it from Ibn Sina, and Ibn Sina learned it from the books of Aristotle. Those who spoke in Usul after Abu Hamid are the ones who spoke about definitions in the manner of Greek logic. As for the rest of the groups of theorists from all sects - the Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, Karramiyya, Shi'a, and others from among those who authored in this field from the followers of the four Imams and others - according to them, definitions only provide 'distinction between the defined and others.' Rather, most of them do not permit the definition [to do more].
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that definitions provide conceptualization (tasawwur) of essences. Ibn Taymiyya begins the second station by stating the question: can things be conceptualized through definitions? He then gives the answer: the verified investigators know that the benefit of a definition is only distinction (tamyiz) between the defined and others, like a name. It does not provide a mental image or define the essence. This claim of conceptualization is only made by Greek logicians and their blind followers among Muslims. The majority of Muslim theologians and theorists disagree. Ibn Taymiyya traces the introduction of this Greek logical doctrine into Islamic disciplines to al-Ghazali, who wrote a logical introduction in al-Mustasfa and composed works like Mihakk al-Nazar and Mi'yar al-'Ilm. He criticizes Ghazali's al-Qistas al-Mustaqim as being falsely attributed to prophetic inspiration, actually derived from Ibn Sina and Aristotle. Those who followed Ghazali in Usul al-Din and Fiqh also adopted this Greek logical approach. In contrast, all other theological schools—Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, Karramiyya, Shi'a, and others—hold that definitions only serve to distinguish the defined from others, and most do not even permit definitions to do more than that.

Key Terms
حد
تمييز
تصوير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the true benefit of a definition?
A) To reveal the essence of the thing defined.
B) To distinguish the defined from other things.
C) To provide a proof for the existence of the thing defined.
D) To produce new knowledge about the thing defined.

Key Takeaway
Definitions only serve to distinguish; they do not provide conceptualization of essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Greek Origin of the False Claim: Ibn Taymiyya on Ghazali's Error
Ibn Taymiyya critiques Ghazali's introduction of Greek logic into Islamic sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace Ibn Taymiyya's historical criticism of Ghazali's adoption of Greek logic and explain why the majority of Islamic theologians rejected the logicians' view of definition.

Core Idea
Ghazali imported Greek logic into Islamic disciplines, misattributing it to the prophets.

Ibn Taymiyya does not stop at theoretical refutation; he diagnoses how the error entered Islamic thought. He points to Abu Hamid al-Ghazali as the key transmitter of Greek logic into Usul al-Din and Fiqh. Ibn Taymiyya even questions Ghazali's claim that his logical works were inspired by prophethood, revealing their true origin in Ibn Sina and Aristotle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that the logicians' view of definitions entered Islamic discourse after Abu Hamid (Ghazali).
This is a historical causality argument.
2
List Ghazali's logical works mentioned
Ibn Taymiyya names al-Mustasfa, Mihakk al-Nazar, Mi'yar al-'Ilm, and al-Qistas al-Mustaqim. Note the claim that al-Qistas was falsely attributed to prophetic inspiration.
3
Trace the chain of transmission
Ibn Taymiyya says Ghazali learned from Ibn Sina, who learned from Aristotle. This chain delegitimizes the doctrine as foreign.
4
Contrast with the majority view
Ibn Taymiyya lists major Islamic theological schools (Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, etc.) that hold definitions only provide tamyiz.
This shows the minority status of the Greek view.

Its benefit is not to form a mental image of the defined or to define its essence; rather, this is claimed only by the people of Greek logic, the followers of Aristotle, and those who follow their path and imitate them in blind following among Muslims and others. As for the majority of theorists and theologians among Muslims and others, they are contrary to this. Everything with a Reality Has a Definition
Al-Qadi Baqillani asserts that everything with a reality has a definition, whether affirmative or negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Baqillani's principle that every existing thing has a definition and distinguish between affirmative and negative definitions.

Core Idea
Everything real is definable, whether by affirmation or negation.

Al-Qadi Baqillani, a leading Ash'ari theologian, addresses a foundational question: what can be defined? He asserts that anything with a verified reality (muhaqqaq) has a definition, regardless of whether that definition is affirmative or negative. In this lesson, you will examine his reasoning and understand how definitions can take both positive and negative forms. This principle is crucial for Islamic disciplines like Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh, where precise definitions are essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Locate Baqillani's key statement: 'What has a reality has a definition'. Notice he acknowledges that some things are not defined, but insists that everything real is definable.
2
Understand the condition for definition
The passage says definition refers to the reality of the thing. So only things with a reality can be defined. Recognize that 'reality' (haqiqa) is what makes a thing what it is.
3
Recognize the types of definition
Baqillani explicitly states that definition can be negative or affirmative. Negative definitions describe what a thing is not (e.g., 'the infinite is what has no end'), while affirmative definitions state positive properties (e.g., 'man is a rational animal'). This broadens the common understanding.
4
Note the purpose of definition
The purpose is to mention that reality by which a thing is distinguished from all others. Distinction comes from the thing itself, not from the definer's words. So a valid definition picks out the objective essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال القاضي من الأشياء ما يُحَدِّ، ومنها ما لا يُحَدِّ. وما من محقق إلا وله حقيقة. ومن صار إلى أن الحدّ يرجع إلى حقيقة المحدود يقول : ما من ذي حقيقة إلا له حد، نفياً كان أو إثباتاً والغرضُ من التحديد التعرُّض لحقيقة الشيء التي بها يتميز عن غيره والشيء إنما يتميّز عن غيره بنفسه وحقيقته، لا بقول القائل .
Then al-Qadi said: Among things, some are defined and some are not defined. There is nothing with a reality (muhaqqaq) except that it has a reality. And whoever holds that the definition refers to the reality of the defined says: There is nothing possessing a reality except that it has a definition, whether negative or affirmative. And the purpose of definition is to mention the reality of the thing by which it is distinguished from others. And a thing is distinguished from others only by itself and its reality, not by the statement of the speaker.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a definitional statement from al-Qadi (Baqillani) about the scope of definition. It begins by noting that some things can be defined and some cannot, but immediately clarifies that everything that has a reality should have a definition. The key point is that if someone accepts that definition refers to the reality of the defined, then he must hold that every entity with a reality has a definition, whether that definition is negative (e.g., 'what is not a substance') or affirmative. The purpose of definition is to mention that reality by which the thing is distinguished from all else. The final sentence emphasizes that distinction comes from the thing's own reality, not from the definer's words. This aligns with the mutakallimun's view that definition is about objective properties, not subjective statements.

Key Terms
محقق (muhaqqaq): Something that has a verified reality or existence; a thing with a definite essence.
نفياً كان أو إثباتاً (nafyan kana aw ithbatan): Whether the definition is negative (e.g., 'the infinite is what has no end') or affirmative (e.g., 'man is a rational animal').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does al-Qadi say about things that have a reality?
A) They cannot be defined because definitions are verbal
B) They all have a definition, whether negative or affirmative
C) Only those with a specific property can be defined
D) Definitions apply only to sensory objects

Key Takeaway
Everything with a reality has a definition, which may be affirmative or negative, because definition aims to capture the thing's distinguishing reality.

Conditions of Ittiraad and In'ikaas for Definitions
Abu al-Ma'ali explains that ittiraad and in'ikaas are necessary conditions for a valid definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a definition satisfies ittiraad and in'ikaas using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A valid definition must both cover all instances (ittiraad) and exclude all non-instances (in'ikaas).

Abu al-Ma'ali explains that a definition that does not fully capture its defined term is not a true definition. The verifiers established two necessary conditions: ittiraad (comprehensiveness) and in'ikaas (exclusiveness). In this lesson, we will learn how to apply these tests to definitions, using examples from the definition of knowledge ('ilm).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition being tested
Find the statement that defines a term, e.g., 'knowledge is accident' or 'knowledge is cognition'.
2
Check ittiraad (comprehensiveness)
Ask: Does the definition apply to every instance of the defined? If there is an instance of the defined that the definition does not cover, ittiraad fails.
Example: 'Knowledge is accident' fails because not every accident is knowledge.
3
Check in'ikaas (exclusiveness)
Ask: Does the definition only apply to instances of the defined? If there is something that the definition covers that is not an instance of the defined, in'ikaas fails.
Example: 'Knowledge is originated cognition' fails because there is unoriginated knowledge (Allah's knowledge).
4
Determine if the definition passes both tests
A valid definition must satisfy both ittiraad and in'ikaas, making it coextensive with the defined term.
The correct definition 'knowledge is cognition' passes both: every knowledge is cognition and every cognition is knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال أبو المعالي : قال المحققون الاطّراد والانعكاس من شرائط الحد». وإذا كان الغرضُ من الحدّ تمييز المحدود بصفته عما ليس منه فليس يتحقق ذلك إلا مع الاطّراد والانعكاس. فَـ «الطّرد» هو «تحقق المحدود مع تحقق الحدّ» و «العكس» هو «انتفاء المحدود مع انتفاء الحدّ». وإذا قيل : حدُّ «العلم» هو «العَرَض» لم يطّرد ذلك، إذ «ليس كل عرض علماً»، فهذا «نقض الحدّ». ولو قلنا في حدّ العلم كل معرفة حادثة فهذا لا ينعكس، إذ ثبت علم ليس بحادث، والسائل عن حدّ «العلم» لم يقصد حد ضرب منه تخصيصاً وإنما أراد «الإحاطة بمعنى سائر العلوم. وإذا قلنا : «العلم» هو «المعرفة» فَـ «كلّ معرفة علم» و«كلّ علم معرفة»، و«كل ما ليس بعلم فليس بمعرفة وكل ما ليس بمعرفة فليس بعلم. وهذه عبارات أربع - عبارتان في النفي وعبارتان في الإثبات ولا تستقيم الحدود دون ذلك .
Then Abu al-Ma'ali said: The verifiers said that ittiraad (comprehensiveness) and in'ikaas (exclusiveness) are among the conditions of the definition. Since the purpose of the definition is to distinguish the defined by its property from what is not it, this cannot be achieved except with ittiraad and in'ikaas. So 'ittiraad' is 'the realization of the defined with the realization of the definition' and 'in'ikaas' is 'the negation of the defined with the negation of the definition.' If one says: the definition of 'knowledge' is 'accident', it does not meet ittiraad, because not every accident is knowledge. This is a 'refutation (naqd) of the definition.' And if we say in the definition of knowledge: 'every originated cognition' (kull ma'rifa haditha), this does not meet in'ikaas, because there exists knowledge that is not originated. And the questioner about the definition of 'knowledge' did not intend a definition of a particular type of it, but rather wanted 'comprehension of the meaning of all knowledges.' And if we say: 'knowledge' is 'cognition' (ma'rifa), then 'every cognition is knowledge' and 'every knowledge is cognition', and 'everything that is not knowledge is not cognition', and 'everything that is not cognition is not knowledge.' These are four expressions - two in negation and two in affirmation. Definitions are not valid without that.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the two essential conditions for a valid definition: ittiraad (comprehensiveness) and in'ikaas (exclusiveness). Ittiraad means that whenever the definition applies, the defined also applies; in other words, the definition covers all instances of the defined. In'ikaas means that whenever the definition does not apply, the defined does not apply; that is, the definition excludes all non-instances. The author gives examples from knowledge ('ilm). If one defines 'ilm as 'accident' (arad), this fails ittiraad because some accidents, like movement, are not knowledge. This is called naqd al-hadd (refutation of the definition). If one defines 'ilm as 'every originated cognition' (kull ma'rifa haditha), this fails in'ikaas because there is knowledge that is not originated, such as the knowledge of Allah. The questioner wants a definition that covers all types of knowledge, not just a subset. A correct definition should be bidirectional: 'every knowledge is cognition' and 'every cognition is knowledge', and the corresponding negations. The four expressions (two affirmative, two negative) are required for a proper definition. This is consistent with the classical logical requirement that definitions be coextensive with the defined.

الانعكاس
نقض الحد

Key Takeaway

The Unity of Meaning in Definition Composition
Abu al-Ma'ali discusses the criterion for valid composition in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a multi-word definition violates the prohibition of composition by checking if it expresses a single unified description.

Core Idea
A definition is one in meaning, not in wording; multiple words are fine if they convey one attribute.

This passage from Abu al-Ma'ali clarifies a common misunderstanding: that a definition must be a single word. He explains that the prohibition of composition (tarkib) is about unity of meaning (ittihad al-ma'na), not about the number of words. Using examples from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, he demonstrates that definitions like 'what accepts accident' or 'that which necessitates knowledge' are not composite because they express a single description. This principle is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under scrutiny
Locate the multi-word definition in the text. For example, 'that which necessitates that its locus be knowing' for 'knowledge'.
The definition may consist of several Arabic words.
2
Extract the meanings carried by the words
List the distinct conceptual components mentioned. In the knowledge example, the components are 'necessitation' and 'locus being knowing'. Determine if each component represents an independent attribute or merely elaborates one attribute.
3
Assess unity of meaning
Ask: Do the components jointly point to a single description (sifa wahida), or do they introduce two independent descriptions? If they reduce to one attribute, the definition is not composite. For knowledge, the single attribute is 'necessitation'.
The passage states that the definition of substance ('what accepts accident') reduces to 'acceptance' alone.
4
Apply the division of composition types
If the definition contains two independent meanings where one is superfluous, it is invalid by agreement. If the meanings are unified into one description, it is permissible and not considered composite.
The passage distinguishes agreed invalid composition from disputed cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو المعالي : فإن قال قائل : هل يجوز تركيب الحدّ من وصفين أم لا؟ قلنا : هل يجوز اختلف المتكلّمون . فذهب كثير منهم إلى أن المركب ليس بحدّ ، وشيخُنا أبو الحسن يميل إلى ذلك وقدح في التركيب وليس المراد بمنع التركيب تكليف المسئول أن يأتي في حدّ ما يُسأل عنه بعبارة واحدة، إذ المقصود «اتحاد المعنى بدون اللفظ»، والعبارات لا تقصد لأنفُسِها، وليست هي حدوداً، بل هي منبئة عن الحدود. وقال شيخنا أبو الحسن في حدّ «العلم» - مع منعه التركيب - هو ما أوجب كونُ محله عالماً»، وهذا يشتمل على كلمات، ولم يعد هذا تركيباً، فإن المقصود بالحدّ التعرُّض لصفة واحدة، وهو إيجاب العلمِ حُكْمَه». وكذلك إذا قيل في حد «الجوهر»: «ما قبل العرض» فليس/ بمركب، وإن ذُكر العَرَضُ وقبوله إياه، ولكن المقصود بالحدّ «التعرض للقبول» فقط. ثم التركيب فيه تقسيم . فمنه باطل بالاتفاق، ومنه مختلف فيه. فالمتفق عليه هو التركيب، أن يذكر الحاد معنيين يقع الاستقلال بأحدهما، وذكر الآخر لغو في مقصود الحدّ منه باطل وشرطه .
Abu al-Ma'ali said: If one says: Is it permissible to compose the definition from two descriptions or not? We say: The mutakallimun differed. Many went that the composite is not a definition, and our teacher Abu al-Hasan inclined toward that and criticized composition. But the intended meaning of prohibiting composition is not to burden the respondent to produce the definition of what he is asked about in a single phrase, because the purpose is 'the unity of meaning, not the wording.' Statements are not intended for themselves; they are not definitions, but rather they indicate definitions. And our teacher Abu al-Hasan said in the definition of 'knowledge' - despite his prohibition of composition - it is 'that which necessitates that its locus be knowing.' This includes words, but he did not consider this composition, because the intended purpose of the definition is to mention one description, which is 'the necessitation of knowledge of its ruling.' Similarly, if it is said in the definition of 'substance' (jawhar): 'what accepts accident' (ma qabila al-arad), this is not composite, even though the accident and its acceptance are mentioned, but the intended purpose of the definition is only 'to mention acceptance.' Then composition has a division: some is invalid by agreement, and some is disputed. What is agreed upon as invalid is when the definer mentions two meanings, one of which is independent, and the other is superfluous in the purpose of the definition.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of whether a definition can be composed of multiple descriptions (tarkib al-hadd). Abu al-Ma'ali reports that mutakallimun differed: many, including Shaykh Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari), held that a composite definition is not a true definition. However, the prohibition is not about using multiple words, but about the meaning being non-unified. The purpose of a definition is to capture a single, unified description. For example, Abu al-Hasan defined 'knowledge' as 'that which necessitates that its locus be knowing' – this phrase contains several words but expresses one description (the necessitation), so it is not considered composite. Similarly, defining 'substance' as 'what accepts accident' uses multiple words but the single meaning is 'acceptance'. The passage then distinguishes between two types of composition: one that is unanimously invalid (where the definer mentions two independent meanings, one of which is superfluous) and another that is disputed. The key criterion is whether the multiple words can be reduced to a single unified description (sifa wahida).

Key Terms
تركيب الحد
اتحاد المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It must consist of only two descriptions
C) It must express a single unified description, regardless of word count

Key Takeaway
A definition can use multiple words as long as they refer to a single attribute; the prohibition of composition targets multiplicity of meanings, not multiplicity of words.

The Conditions of Itrād and Inʿikās for Definitions
Explains ṭard and ʿaks as necessary conditions for a sound definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a definition satisfies the conditions of ṭard and ʿaks using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A proper definition must cover all instances and exclude all non-instances.

In this passage, the author explains that definitions must meet two conditions: ṭard (coextensiveness) and ʿaks (converseness). These ensure that the definition genuinely distinguishes the definiendum from everything else. We'll see how these conditions are applied to examples like the definition of 'knowledge' and learn to construct the four propositions that verify a correct definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed definition
Find the definition being tested. For example, 'knowledge is accident' or 'knowledge is every originated cognition'.
2
Test for ṭard (coextensiveness)
Ask: Does every instance of the definiendum fall under the definition? If not, the definition fails ṭard. Example: 'knowledge is accident' fails because not every accident is knowledge.
A violation is called naqḍ (breach) of the definition.
3
Test for ʿaks (converseness)
Ask: Does every non-instance of the definiendum fall outside the definition? If not, the definition fails ʿaks. Example: 'knowledge is every originated cognition' fails because God's knowledge is not originated.
This ensures the definition is not too narrow.
4
Construct the four verifying propositions
For a correct definition like 'knowledge is cognition', verify four statements: (i) All knowledge is cognition; (ii) All cognition is knowledge; (iii) Whatever is not knowledge is not cognition; (iv) Whatever is not cognition is not knowledge.
These four are necessary for a sound definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - مذهب المتكلمين في «الحدود». وله حد، نفياً كان أو إثباتاً والغرضُ من التحديد التعرُّض لحقيقة الشيء التي بها يتميز عن غيره والشيء إنما يتميّز عن غيره بنفسه وحقيقته، لا بقول القائل . ثم قال أبو المعالي : قال المحققون الاطّراد والانعكاس من شرائط الحد». وإذا كان الغرضُ من الحدّ تمييز المحدود بصفته عما ليس منه فليس يتحقق ذلك إلا مع الاطراد والانعكاس. فَـ «الطّرد» هو «تحقق المحدود مع تحقق الحد» و «العكس» هو «انتفاء المحدود مع انتفاء الحدّ . وإذا قيل : حدُّ «العلم» هو «العَرَض» لم يطّرد ذلك، إذ «ليس كل عرض علماً»، فهذا «نقض الحدّ». ولو قلنا في حدّ العلم كل معرفة حادثة فهذا لا ينعكس، إذ ثبت علم ليس بحادث، والسائل عن حدّ «العلم» لم يقصد حد ضرب منه تخصيصاً وإنما أراد «الإحاطة بمعنى سائر العلوم. وإذا قلنا : «العلم» هو «المعرفة» فَـ «كلّ معرفة علم» و«كلّ علم معرفة»، و«كل ما ليس بعلم فليس بمعرفة وكل ما ليس بمعرفة فليس بعلم. وهذه عبارات أربع - عبارتان في النفي وعبارتان في الإثبات ولا تستقيم الحدود دون ذلك .
The second section: The doctrine of the mutakallimūn concerning 'definitions'. Every thing has a definition, whether negative or affirmative, and the purpose of definition is to expose the reality of the thing by which it is distinguished from other things. And a thing is only distinguished from other things by its own self and its reality, not by the statement of the speaker. Then Abū al-Maʿālī said: The verifying scholars said: 'Al-ṭard and al-ʿaks are among the conditions of the definition.' And if the purpose of definition is to distinguish the definiendum by its attribute from what is not it, then this cannot be realized except with al-ṭard and al-ʿaks. So 'al-ṭard' is the realization of the definiendum with the realization of the definition, and 'al-ʿaks' is the negation of the definiendum with the negation of the definition. And if it is said: The definition of 'knowledge' is 'accident', that does not have ṭard, for not every accident is knowledge; this is a 'breach' (naqḍ) of the definition. And if we say in the definition of knowledge: 'every originated cognition', this does not have ʿaks, for there exists knowledge that is not originated. And the questioner about the definition of 'knowledge' does not intend the definition of a particular type of it, but rather he intends 'comprehension of the meaning of all knowledges'. And if we say: 'Knowledge' is 'cognition', then every cognition is knowledge and every knowledge is cognition, and whatever is not knowledge is not cognition and whatever is not cognition is not knowledge. These are four expressions: two negative expressions and two positive expressions, and definitions are not sound without that.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage introduces the second section on the mutakallimūn's view of definitions. It first states the purpose of definition: to expose the reality of a thing so that it is distinguished from others. Distinction is by the thing's own reality, not by verbal description. Then it cites Abū al-Maʿālī who reports that the verifying scholars consider ṭard (coextensiveness) and ʿaks (converseness) as conditions for a correct definition. The author explains: since the goal is to mark off the definiendum from what it is not, these conditions are necessary. Ṭard means that whenever the definition applies, the definiendum applies; ʿaks means that whenever the definition is absent, the definiendum is absent. Two examples illustrate violations. First, defining 'knowledge' as 'accident' fails ṭard because not every accident is knowledge – this is a 'naqḍ' (breach). Second, defining 'knowledge' as 'every originated cognition' fails ʿaks because there is knowledge not originated (e.g., God's knowledge). The author notes that the questioner seeks a definition covering all knowledge, not just one type. The correct definition 'knowledge is cognition' satisfies both ṭard and ʿaks, expressed in four categorical propositions: two affirmative and two negative. These four are necessary for a sound definition.

العكس
نقض الحد

Key Takeaway

When Composite Definitions Are Invalid by Consensus
Ibn Taymiyya on the agreed-upon invalid composition in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which a composite definition is invalid by consensus and distinguish it from definitions that use multiple words but express a single attribute.

Core Idea
Not every multi-word definition is composite; only if it uses two independent attributes where one is redundant.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya reports the mutakallimūn's dispute on composite definitions. He first clarifies that definitions like Abū al-Ḥasan's 'what necessitates its locus being knowing' for 'ilm are not composite even though they contain multiple words, because the intent is a single attribute. The same applies to defining substance as 'what accepts accidents'. The agreed-upon invalid composition occurs when two meanings are mentioned, one independent and the other superfluous. This lesson will help you apply this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed-upon invalid composition
Look for the description: 'the definer mentions two meanings, one of which stands independently and the mention of the other is superfluous'.
This is the only type of composition invalid by consensus.
2
Examine the counterexamples
See how the definitions of 'ilm and jawhar are defended as single-attribute definitions despite having multiple words.
The key is that all words point to one attribute.
3
Extract the condition
The definition must expose a single attribute; words are just indicators.
The passage: 'the intent of the definition is to expose a single attribute'.
4
Test a definition
Given a proposed definition, decide if it falls under the agreed invalid type.
If it has two independent attributes with one superfluous, it's invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو المعالي : فإن قال قائل : هل يجوز تركيب الحدّ من وصفين أم لا؟ قلنا : هل يجوز اختلف المتكلّمون . فذهب كثير منهم إلى أن المركب ليس بحدّ ، وشيخُنا أبو الحسن يميل إلى ذلك أم لا؟ ويقدح في التركيب وليس المراد بمنع التركيب تكليف المسئول أن يأتي في حدّ ما يُسأل عنه بعبارة واحدة، إذ المقصود «اتحاد المعنى بدون اللفظ»، والعبارات لا تقصد ، لأنفُسِها، وليست هي حدوداً، بل هي منبئة عن الحدود. وقال شيخنا أبو الحسن في حدّ «العلم» - مع منعه التركيب - هو ما أوجب كونُ محله عالماً»، وهذا يشتمل على كلمات، ولم يعد هذا تركيباً، فإن المقصود بالحدّ التعرُّض لصفة واحدة، وهو إيجاب العلمِ حُكْمَه». وكذلك إذا قيل في حد «الجوهر»: «ما قبل العرض» فليس/ بمركب، وإن ذُكر العَرَضُ وقبوله إياه، ولكن المقصود بالحدّ «التعرض للقبول» فقط - ثم التركيب فيه تقسيم . فمنه باطل بالاتفاق، ومنه مختلف فيه. فالمتفق عليه هو التركيب، أن يذكر الحاد معنيين يقع الاستقلال بأحدهما، وذكر الآخر لغو في مقصود الحدّ منه باطل وشرطه . ومنه مختلف فيه. كتاب الرد على المنطقيين وأما المختلف فيه فكما يقول المعتزلة في حدّ المَرْني»: «ما يكون لوناً أو متلوّناً». فهم يصححون هذا الحدّ، ولا يرون هذا التركيب قادحاً. قالوا : لأن المقصود من الحد حصرُ المحدود مع التعرّض للحقيقة، فإذا قامت الدلالة على أن المتحيّزَ يرى، وعلى أن الألوان مرئيّة، ولا تجتمع الألوان والجوهر في حقيقة واحدة، إذ الأوصافُ الجامعة لها محدودة، منها الوجود والحدوث، ويبطل تحديد المرئي بالموجود أو المحدث، إذ يلزم منه رؤية الطعوم والروائح والعلوم ونحوها. فإذا لم يمكن الجمع بين الجواهر والألوان في صفة جامعة لها في حكم الرؤية غير منتقضة، فلا وجة إلا ذكرُ الجواهر بخاصتها وذكرُ الألوان بحقيقتها . ومعظم المتكلمين على الامتناع من مثل ذلك في الحدود. وقالوا: المتحيّز وكونُ اللون هيئة حُكمان متنافيان فينبغي أن لا يثبت لهما - مع تباينهما - حكم لا تباين فيه، وهو كونُ المَرْني مَرْنيا . ضبط آحاد واحدة وبصفتين قال الأستاذ أبو المعالي وأحسن طريقة في هذا ما ذكره القاضي، فإنه قال : ما المحدود بصفة يُذكر في معرفة الحدود ينقسم، فربما يتأتى ضبط آحاد المحدود بصفة واحدة تشترك فيها جملة الأحاد، نحو تحديد «العلم» بـ «المعرفة» و«الشيئية» بـ «الوجود»، وربما لا يتأتى ضبط جميع آحاد المحدود في صفة واحدة تشترك جميعها فيها، فلو ذكر في حدّها صفة جامعة لبطل. فإذا كان الأمر كذلك وتأتي ضبط ما يُسأل عنه بذكر صفتين يشتمل الحد ما أحاط بالمحدود إحداهما على قبيل من المسئول عنه والأخرى على القبيل الآخر لصح تحديده . قال القاضي : ولو حَقَّق ذلك لزال فيه الخلاف ـ فإن الذي يحدّ بصفتين لو قيل له : «أتدّعي اجتماعَ القبيلين في صفة واحدة! لما ادّعاه، ولو قيل لمطالبه : أتنكر تحقق الانحصار عند ذكر الصفتين ! » لما وَجَد سبيلاً إلى إنكار ذلك . والحدّ ليس بموجب، وإنما هو بيان وكشف، وهذا المعنى يتحقق في الصفتين تحققه في الواحدة، فإن الكلام إلى مناقشة في العبارة.
Abū al-Maʿālī said: If a questioner says: Is it permissible to compose a definition from two attributes or not? We say: The mutakallimūn differed. Many of them held that the composite is not a definition. Our teacher Abū al-Ḥasan inclined to that and criticized composition. The prohibition of composition does not mean requiring the respondent to produce a definition of what is asked about in a single expression, because the objective is 'unity of meaning without the wording', and expressions are not intended for themselves; they are not definitions, but rather they indicate the definitions. And our teacher Abū al-Ḥasan said concerning the definition of 'knowledge' – despite his prohibition of composition – 'it is what necessitates that its locus be knowing', and this includes words, and this is not considered composition, because the intent of the definition is to expose a single attribute, which is the obligation of knowledge's ruling. Similarly, if it is said in the definition of 'substance': 'what accepts accidents', it is not composite, even though accident and its acceptance are mentioned, but the intent of the definition is 'exposure of acceptance' only. Then composition has a division: part of it is invalid by consensus, and part of it is disputed. What is agreed upon is that composition is invalid if the definer mentions two meanings, one of which stands independently and the mention of the other is superfluous to the purpose of the definition. And its condition. As for the disputed part, it is like what the Muʿtazila say in the definition of 'the visible': 'that which is either a color or colored'. They validate this definition and do not consider this composition detrimental. They said: Because the purpose of definition is to encompass the definiendum while exposing the reality. So if the evidence establishes that the extended entity is seen, and that colors are visible, and that colors and substance do not share a single reality, since the comprehensive attributes for them are limited, such as existence and origination, and it is invalid to define the visible by existent or originated, because that would imply seeing tastes, odors, cognitions, and the like. So if it is not possible to combine substances and colors in a single attribute that encompasses them in the ruling of visibility without breach, then there is no way but to mention substances by their specificity and colors by their reality. But most of the mutakallimūn forbid such a thing in definitions. They said: The extended entity and the quality of being a color have two mutually exclusive rulings, so it is not proper to establish for them, despite their diversity, a ruling that has no diversity, namely being visible. [Then] Controlling individuals by one attribute or two attributes. Al-Ustādh Abū al-Maʿālī said: The best method in this is what al-Qāḍī mentioned. He said: That which is defined by one attribute in the knowledge of definitions divides. Sometimes it is possible to control the individuals of the definiendum by one attribute which the collective individuals share, like defining 'knowledge' by 'cognition' and 'thingness' by 'existence'. Sometimes it is not possible to control all individuals of the definiendum by one attribute that all of them share, so if one were to mention a comprehensive attribute in its definition, it would be invalid. Therefore, if the matter is so, and it is possible to control what is asked about by mentioning two attributes, the definition encompassing the definiendum – one of the two concerning one category of the definiendum and the other concerning the other category – then its definition would be sound. Al-Qāḍī said: If this were realized, the disagreement would cease. For if one who defines by two attributes were asked: 'Do you claim that the two categories share one attribute?' he would not claim it. And if his opponent were asked: 'Do you deny the realization of encompassment when the two attributes are mentioned?' he would find no way to deny it. The definition is not a cause; rather it is a clarification and disclosure, and this meaning is realized in two attributes just as it is realized in one. For the matter comes down to a quibble over wording.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from the Radd al-Mantiqiyyīn addresses the controversial issue of whether a definition (ḥadd) can be composed of two attributes. The author first reports that many mutakallimūn, including Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, reject composite definitions. However, Abū al-Ḥasan's own definition of 'ilm as 'what necessitates its locus being knowing' is not considered composite because it focuses on a single property (the necessitation of the ruling). Similarly, defining 'substance' as 'what accepts accidents' is not composite even though it mentions both the accident and acceptance; the essence is the single attribute of 'acceptance'. The author then distinguishes two types of composition: (1) invalid by consensus, where one attribute is independent and the other is superfluous; (2) disputed, as exemplified by the Muʿtazilite definition of 'the visible' (al-marʼī) as 'that which is either a color or colored'. The Muʿtazila argue that since substance and color share the attribute of being visible but have no single common attribute (like existence would include too much), they must be defined by listing their distinct natures. However, most mutakallimūn forbid this because the two attributes (extended entity and color quality) have conflicting rulings. The author then presents al-Qāḍī's (al-Bāqillānī) resolution: whether a definition can use one or two attributes depends on whether one attribute suffices to capture all individuals. If not, two attributes may be used, each covering a subset. Al-Qāḍī argues that this resolves the disagreement because no one claims that the two subsets share a single attribute, and the opponent cannot deny the encompassment achieved by the two attributes. The definition is merely a clarification, not a cause, so using two attributes is permissible if it clarifies the definiendum. The dispute is ultimately about wording.

Key Terms
التركيب
المختلَف فيه
الانحصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Abū al-Ḥasan's definition of 'ilm not considered composite despite having multiple words?
A) Because it uses only one word.
B) Because the words are not definitions but indicators.
C) Because the definition focuses on a single attribute: the necessitation of the ruling.
D) Because the definition is not a definition but a description.

Key Takeaway
A composite definition is invalid by consensus only if it includes two independent attributes where one is superfluous; multi-word definitions that express one attribute are not composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the definition of 'qudra' (power) in kalam: 'a quality by which the agent acts with intention'. Identify whether this is a single-attribute definition or composite according to the agreed rule. Justify your answer using the criteria from the passage.
