When Two Attributes Are Permissible: The Muʿtazila's Definition of al-Mar'ī
Ibn Taymiyya on the disputed composition in definitions and al-Qāḍī's resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a definition can be composed of two attributes when no single attribute covers all instances, using al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī's criterion.

Core Idea
If one attribute cannot encompass all instances, two attributes may be used, each covering a distinct subset.

In this part of the passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents the disputed case: the Muʿtazila define 'the visible' as 'that which is either a color or colored'. Most mutakallimūn reject this because the two attributes (being extended and being a color) have different rulings. However, al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī proposes a resolution: the definition's goal is to encompass the definiendum; if one attribute cannot do so, two attributes are permissible as long as together they cover all instances. This lesson will apply that method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed definition
The Muʿtazila's definition of al-mar'ī as 'that which is either a color or colored'.
This is an example of the disputed type of composition.
2
Understand the objection
Most mutakallimūn argue that the two attributes (extended entity and color quality) have contradictory rulings, so they cannot combine to define one thing.
They say: 'it is not proper to establish for them a ruling that has no diversity'.
3
Apply al-Qāḍī's method
Ask: can one attribute cover all visible things? No, because visible things are either substances (extended) or colors. No single attribute (like existence) works because it includes non-visible things.
Al-Qāḍī says: 'if it is possible to control what is asked about by mentioning two attributes... then its definition would be sound'.
4
Decide if the definition is sound
According to al-Qāḍī, if the two attributes together achieve encompassment (inḥiṣār), the definition is valid, as no one claims that the two subsets share one attribute.
The definition is a clarification, not a cause.

Source Text

According to al-Qāḍī's resolution in the passage, what is the key condition for allowing a two-attribute definition?
A) The two attributes must be synonymous.

Al-Qadi's Definition of Knowledge
Al-Qadi gives a definition of knowledge and begins a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in al-Qadi's definition of knowledge and assess its claimed comprehensiveness.

Core Idea
Al-Qadi's definition of knowledge as cognition of the known as it is aims for complete coverage and exclusion.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of knowledge (al-ʿilm) given by the Ashʿarī theologian al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī. His definition is concise but foundational. We will dissect it to see how it sets the boundaries of what counts as knowledge. Though the text cuts off before the proof is completed, we can still analyze the definition's structure and its intended scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-ʿilm' (knowledge). Find where al-Qadi says 'ḥadduhū' (its definition).
2
Extract the definition
The definition is 'maʿrifat al-maʿlūm ʿalā mā huwa bihī' (cognition of the known as it is). Note that 'maʿrifa' (cognition) is the genus, and 'al-maʿlūm ʿalā mā huwa bihī' (the known as it is) is the differentia.
In Ashʿarī usage, 'maʿrifa' is broader than 'ʿilm', so this is not circular.
3
Test for completeness (ṭard and ʿaks)
A complete definition should include all instances of the definiendum (ṭard) and exclude all non-instances (ʿaks). Ask: Does every instance of knowledge involve cognition of the known as it is? Does every such cognition count as knowledge? Consider cases like true belief without certainty, or dreaming.
The full proof is missing, but we can begin to reason.
4
Note the missing proof
The text begins 'al-dalīlu ʿalā dhālika anna hādhā al-ḥadda yaḥṣuruhū ʿalā...' (the evidence is that this definition encompasses...). The argument is incomplete. In a complete form, al-Qadi would likely show that the definition covers all types of knowledge (divine, human, necessary, acquired).
We can only note the structure; the full argument is not available.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال القاضي أبو بكر بن الطيب : وإن قال لنا قائل : ما حدّ «العلم عندكم؟ قلنا : حده معرفة المعلوم على ما هُوَ به». والدليل على ذلك أن هذا الحد يحصُرُه على
And al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr ibn al-Ṭayyib said: If a questioner says to us: What is the definition of 'knowledge' according to you? We say: Its definition is 'cognition of the known as it is'. And the evidence for that is that this definition encompasses...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This short passage presents al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī's definition of 'knowledge' (al-ʿilm). He defines it as 'cognition of the known as it is' (maʿrifat al-maʿlūm ʿalā mā huwa bihī). This is a standard definition in the Ashʿarī tradition. The text begins to provide a proof: 'the evidence for that is that this definition encompasses...' but cuts off. The intended proof likely argues that this definition covers all types of knowledge, both human and divine, as it requires correspondence to reality. Without the proof, we can note that this definition aims for both ṭard and ʿaks: every instance of knowledge is a cognition of something as it is, and anything that is not such cognition is not knowledge. The passage is incomplete, so we caution the reader.

The Dispute Over Definitions with Two Attributes
Author presents the Mu'tazila and majority disagreement on using two attributes in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Mu'tazila's argument for defining 'visible' with two attributes and explain why the majority rejected it.

Core Idea
A definition's purpose is encompassment; when one attribute fails, two may succeed.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya reports a classic theological dispute over the structure of definitions. The Mu'tazila defended defining 'visible' with two separate attributes (substance and color), arguing that no single attribute could encompass all visible things without including non-visible things. The majority of theologians objected, insisting that a definition must have a single unified attribute. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni offered a resolution, suggesting the disagreement is ultimately verbal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central problem
The Mu'tazila want to define 'visible' (al-mar'i) but cannot find a single attribute common to all visible things. They argue that substances and colors are fundamentally different, so a single attribute would either miss some visible things or include non-visible ones.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila's proposed definition
They propose defining visible as 'whatever is either a substance (mutaḥayyiz) or a color/colored'. They justify this by saying that evidence shows both substances and colors are visible, and they don't share an essence; so two attributes are needed.
3
Analyze the majority's objection
The majority argue that substance and color are contradictory judgments (one is self-subsistent, the other an accident), so they cannot share a single judgment like visibility in a definition. A definition should have one attribute.
4
Understand al-Qadi's resolution
Al-Qadi al-Baqillani distinguishes cases: sometimes a single attribute suffices (e.g., defining knowledge as cognition), but sometimes two are needed. The definition's purpose is encompassment (hasr), not unity of attribute. If two attributes together cover all instances, the definition is valid.
5
Recognize the final verdict
The passage concludes that the disagreement is terminological (munāqasha fī al-'ibāra). Both sides agree on the goal of clarification; the dispute is over what counts as a proper definition, not over the underlying facts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المختلف فيه فكما يقول المعتزلة في حدّ المَرْني: «ما يكون لوناً أو متلوّناً». فهم يصححون هذا الحدّ، ولا يرون هذا التركيب قادحاً. قالوا : لأن المقصود من الحد حصرُ المحدود مع التعرّض للحقيقة، فإذا قامت الدلالة على أن المتحيّزَ يرى، وعلى أن الألوان مرئيّة، ولا تجتمع الألوان والجوهر في حقيقة واحدة، إذ الأوصافُ الجامعة لها محدودة، منها الوجود والحدوث، ويبطل تحديد المرئي بالموجود أو المحدث، إذ يلزم منه رؤية الطعوم والروائح والعلوم ونحوها. فإذا لم يمكن الجمع بين الجواهر والألوان في صفة جامعة لها في حكم الرؤية غير منتقضة، فلا وجة إلا ذكرُ الجواهر بخاصتها وذكرُ الألوان بحقيقتها . ومعظم المتكلمين على الامتناع من مثل ذلك في الحدود. وقالوا: المتحيّز وكونُ اللون هيئة حُكمان متنافيان فينبغي أن لا يثبت لهما - مع تباينهما - حكم لا تباين فيه، وهو كونُ المَرْني مَرْنيا . ضبط آحاد واحدة و بصفتين قال الأستاذ أبو المعالي وأحسن طريقة في هذا ما ذكره القاضي، فإنه قال : ما المحدود بصفة يُذكر في معرفة الحدود ينقسم، فربما يتأتى ضبط آحاد المحدود بصفة واحدة تشترك فيها جملة الأحاد، نحو تحديد «العلم» بـ «المعرفة» و«الشيئية» بـ «الوجود»، وربما لا يتأتى ضبط جميع آحاد المحدود في صفة واحدة تشترك جميعها فيها، فلو ذكر في حدّها صفة جامعة لبطل. فإذا كان الأمر كذلك وتأتي ضبط ما يُسأل عنه بذكر صفتين يشتمل إحداهما على قبيل من المسئول عنه والأخرى على القبيل الآخر لصح تحديده . قال القاضي : ولو حَقَّق ذلك لزال فيه الخلاف ـ فإن الذي يحدّ بصفتين لو قيل له : «أتدّعي اجتماعَ القبيلين في صفة واحدة! لما ادّعاه، ولو قيل لمطالبه : أتنكر تحقق الانحصار عند ذكر الصفتين ! » لما وَجَد سبيلاً إلى إنكار ذلك . والحدّ ليس بموجب، وإنما هو بيان وكشف، وهذا المعنى يتحقق في الصفتين تحققه في الواحدة، فإن الكلام إلى مناقشة في العبارة.
As for the disputed matter, it is as the Mu'tazila say in the definition of 'visible': 'whatever is either a color or colored'. They validate this definition and do not consider this composition defective. They said: because the purpose of the definition is to encompass the defined while indicating the essence. So if evidence indicates that the substance is seen, and that colors are visible, and colors and substance do not share one essence, since the attributes that unite them are limited, among them existence and temporality, and defining the visible by the existent or the temporal is invalid because it would entail seeing tastes, smells, knowledge, and the like. So if it is not possible to combine substances and colors in one attribute that unites them in the judgment of visibility without exception, then there is no way but to mention substances by their particularity and colors by their reality. The majority of theologians prohibit such in definitions. They said: substance and being a color (a state) are two contradictory judgments, so there should not be established for them—given their disparity—a judgment without disparity, namely being visible. Controlling the individuals of one [thing] with two attributes. Al-Ustadh Abu al-Ma'ali said: The best method in this is what al-Qadi mentioned, for he said: The object defined by an attribute mentioned in knowledge of definitions is divided; sometimes it is possible to control the individuals of the defined by one attribute shared by all individuals, like defining 'knowledge' as 'cognition' and 'thingness' as 'existence'; and sometimes it is not possible to control all individuals of the defined by one attribute shared by all of them, so if one mentioned a universal attribute in its definition it would be invalid. So if this is the case and it is possible to control what is asked about by mentioning two attributes, one including one category of the asked about and the other including the other category, its definition would be valid. Al-Qadi said: If that were realized, the disagreement would disappear—for the one who defines with two attributes, if it were said to him: 'Do you claim the two categories converge in one attribute?' he would not claim it; and if it were said to his opponent: 'Do you deny the realization of encompassment when the two attributes are mentioned?' he would find no way to deny that. The definition is not a cause, but rather a clarification and disclosure, and this meaning is realized in two attributes just as it is realized in one; indeed the matter comes down to a dispute over wording.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute in definitional methodology between the Mu'tazila and the majority of theologians (mutakallimun). The Mu'tazila permit defining something using two separate attributes when a single attribute cannot encompass all instances. Their example is defining 'visible' (al-mar'i) as 'whatever is either a substance or a color'. They argue that since substances and colors are fundamentally different in essence but both are visible, a single definitional attribute cannot cover both without including non-visible things. They reject defining visible as 'existent' or 'temporal' because that would include tastes and smells. The majority object, saying that substance and color have contradictory judgments (substance is self-subsistent, color is an accident), so they cannot share a single definitional judgment (visibility). Al-Ustadh Abu al-Ma'ali (al-Juwayni) then cites al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) who distinguishes two cases: sometimes a single attribute suffices (e.g., defining 'knowledge' as 'cognition'), but sometimes not. When two attributes are needed, al-Qadi argues that the definition is still valid because it achieves the purpose of encompassment. He points out that the objector cannot deny that the two attributes together circumscribe the defined, and the definer does not claim they share one attribute. Ultimately, the disagreement is merely terminological (munāqasha fī al-'ibāra), as both sides accept the underlying goal of clarification.

Key Terms
الحد
المحدود
المتحيز
المرئي
الحصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because 'existent' and 'visible' are synonyms

Key Takeaway
The dispute over definitions using two attributes highlights that the primary goal of a definition is encompassment (hasr), and whether one or two attributes are needed depends on the nature of the defined.

Al-Qadi's Definition of Knowledge and Its Criterion
Al-Qadi's definition of 'ilm and his proof of its validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Qadi Abu Bakr's definition of 'ilm and explain why it is considered valid based on its comprehensive coverage.

Core Idea
A correct definition must include all instances and exclude all non-instances.

Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani offers a concise definition of knowledge ('ilm) as 'cognition of the known as it is.' He then provides a powerful justification: this definition covers every case of knowledge and nothing else. In this lesson, you will unpack that justification and learn the criterion for a valid definition that al-Qadi employs.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the exact phrase where al-Qadi defines 'ilm. It appears in the passage as: 'حده معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به'.
Notice that the definition uses the term 'ma'rifa' (cognition) synonymously with 'ilm.
2
Identify the two conditions
Al-Qadi says the definition 'لا يدخل فيه ما ليس منه، ولا يخرج منه شيء هو فيه'. Identify these two conditions: nothing extraneous enters, and nothing essential exits.
These are the criteria for a definition to 'encompass' (ihata) the defined.
3
Understand the mutual entailment
The passage states that all 'ilm is ma'rifa of the known as it is, and all such ma'rifa is 'ilm. Grasp how this bidirectional relationship seals the definition.
This shows the definition is neither too broad nor too narrow.
4
Apply the criterion to a test case
Take the definition 'faith is assent in the heart' (iman = tasdiq bil-qalb). Does it include all instances of faith? Does it include any non-faith? Use al-Qadi's conditions.
This trains you to evaluate definitions critically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال القاضي أبو بكر بن الطيب : وإن قال لنا قائل : ما حدّ «العلم عندكم؟ قلنا : حده معرفة المعلوم على ما هُوَ به». والدليل على ذلك أن هذا الحد يحصُرُه على حاله في معناه، ولا يدخُل فيه ما ليس منه، ولا يخرج منه شيء هو فيه . والحد إذا أحاط بالمحدود على هذا السبيل وجب أن يكون حداً ثابتاً صحيحاً. فكلُّما حُدّ به «العلم» وغيره وكانت حصر المحدود وتمييزه من غيره وإحاطته به حال ما حددنا به «العلم» وجب الاعتراف بصحته. وقد ثبت أن كل علم تعلّق بمعلوم» فإنه «معرفةٌ له على ما هو به» و «كل معرفة بمعلوم» فإنها «علم» به ، فوجب توفيق الحد الذي حددنا به «العلم» وجعلناه تفسيراً لمعنى منه بأنه «علم» . قلت : فقد بين القاضي أن كلّ ما أحاط بالمحدود بحيث لا يدخُل فيه ما ليس منه ولا يخرج منه ما هو منه كان حداً صحيحاً.
And al-Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib said: If someone asked us: 'What is the definition of knowledge ('ilm) according to you?' We would say: 'Its definition is the cognition of the known as it is.' The evidence for that is that this definition encompasses it in its state in its meaning, and nothing enters it that is not from it, nor does anything from it exit. And the definition, if it encompasses the defined in this way, must be a valid, correct definition. So whenever 'knowledge' and other things are defined by it, and it achieves encompassment of the defined, distinction from others, and full coverage in the way we defined 'knowledge', its correctness must be acknowledged. It has been established that every knowledge is attached to a known, it is cognition of it as it is, and every cognition of a known is knowledge of it. So the definition by which we defined 'knowledge' and made it an interpretation of its meaning, that it is 'knowledge', is necessarily correct. I (the author) say: Al-Qadi has shown that whatever encompasses the defined such that nothing from outside enters and nothing from inside exits is a correct definition.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's definition of 'ilm (knowledge) as 'the cognition (ma'rifa) of the known as it is.' The author provides a justification: this definition fully encompasses the concept of knowledge—it includes every instance of knowledge and excludes everything else. The argument is based on mutual entailment: all knowledge is cognition of the known as it is, and all such cognition is knowledge. The author (Ibn Taymiyya) then summarizes al-Qadi's principle: any definition that neither includes anything extraneous nor excludes anything essential is correct. This passage is a commentary that expands on al-Qadi's reasoning, affirming that a definition's validity depends on its comprehensiveness and exclusivity, not on its form or number of attributes.

Key Terms
العلم (al-ʿilm): Here defined as 'cognition of the known as it is' (maʿrifat al-maʿlūm ʿalā mā huwa bihi). [Note: In later Ash'ari kalam, 'ilm is often defined as 'the attribute by which the known is disclosed', but al-Baqillani's definition is a standard one.]
المعرفة (al-maʿrifa): Used synonymously with 'ilm in this definition; cognition that grasps the known as it is. [Note: Some later scholars distinguish ma'rifa as a deeper, intuitive knowledge, but here it is interchangeable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi, what is the criterion for a correct definition?
A) It must contain a genus and differentia.
B) It must be concise.
C) It must encompass the defined without including extraneous things or excluding anything of the defined.
D) It must be approved by other scholars.

Key Takeaway
A correct definition must neither include what is not part of the defined nor exclude what is part of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Given the mutual entailment between 'ilm and ma'rifa in the passage, explain why this relationship strengthens the definition.

Ibn Taymiyya's Commentary on the Principle of Correct Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya's commentary on al-Qadi's definition and principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general principle about definitions that Ibn Taymiyya extracts from al-Qadi's argument and apply it to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Valid definitions are those that delineate perfectly, regardless of form.

After presenting al-Qadi's definition and its justification, Ibn Taymiyya adds his own comment: he distills a universal principle for correct definitions. This lesson focuses on that principle and how it can be applied to evaluate definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Find Ibn Taymiyya's concluding statement
Look for the sentence starting with 'قلت:' where the author comments. It reads: 'فقد بين القاضي أن كلّ ما أحاط بالمحدود بحيث لا يدخُل فيه ما ليس منه ولا يخرج منه ما هو منه كان حداً صحيحاً.'
This is the key principle.
2
Identify the principle
Ibn Taymiyya states that whatever encompasses the defined such that nothing from outside enters and nothing from inside exits is a correct definition. Paraphrase this in one sentence.
The principle is about perfect circumscription (al-ihata).
3
Compare with al-Qadi's specific argument
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya is not just repeating al-Qadi; he is generalizing from 'ilm to any definition. Al-Qadi showed that his definition of 'ilm satisfied the condition; Ibn Taymiyya makes the condition itself the criterion.
This is an expansion probe: moving from a concrete instance to a general rule.
4
Apply the principle to a new definition
Consider the definition 'niyyah (intention) is the resolve to perform an act of worship.' Does it include all cases of niyyah? Does it include non-niyyah? Use Ibn Taymiyya's principle to evaluate.
This trains you to use the principle as a tool in fiqh and usul.

Al-Ghazali on the Difficulty of Definition: Identifying the Nearest Genus
Al-Ghazali states the difficulty of definition and explains the first obstacle: identifying the nearest genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify errors in definition caused by missing the nearest genus and correct them using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The nearest genus is the most common mistake in definition, even for scholars.

Al-Ghazali, in his Mi'yar al-'Ilm, warns that definition is extremely difficult for human intellects. He lists four obstacles; the first is identifying the nearest genus. The human mind often overlooks the immediate genus and takes a more remote one, leading to incomplete definitions. This lesson examines that first obstacle and shows how to avoid it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum in al-Ghazali's examples: wine (khamr) and human (insan).
2
Extract the erroneous genus
What genus does the mistaken definer use? For wine: 'intoxicating liquid' (mā'i' muskir); for human: 'body' (jism).
3
Identify the correct nearest genus
Determine the true nearest genus: for wine: 'beverage' (sharāb); for human: 'animal' (hayawān). Why is it nearer?
The nearest genus includes the species directly, without intermediate categories.
4
Understand the consequence
A definition with a remote genus is incomplete; it may include things that are not of the species (e.g., poison is intoxicating but not a beverage; a plant is a body but not an animal). This violates completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر الغزالي في كتابه الكبير في المنطق الذي سمّاه معيار العلم» مذهب المتكلّمين هذا بعد أن ذكر استعصاء الحدّ على طريقة المنطقيين، فقال : استعصاء (١) ا (١) الحدّ على القُوَى البشرية إلا عند غاية التشمير والجهد . من عَرَف ما ذكرناه من مُثَارات الاشتباه في الحدّ عَرَف أن القوة البشرية لا تقوى على التحفّظ [۱۷] عن كل ذلك إلا على الندور، وهي كثيرة، وأعصاها على الذهن اربعة أمور : أحدها: أنا شَرَطنا أن نأخذ «الجنس الأقرب». ومن أين للطالب أن لا يغفل عنه ؟ فيأخذ «جنساً يظن أنه أقرب، وربما يوجد ما هو أقرب منه ، فيَحُدُّ «الخمر» بأنه «مائع مُسْكِر ويَذْهَل عن «الشَّراب» الذي هو تحته وهو أقرب منه ، ويحدُّ «الإنسان» بأنه «جسم
And al-Ghazali mentioned in his great book on logic, which he titled 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm', the position of the theologians after mentioning the difficulty of definition according to the logicians' method. He said: The definition is difficult for human powers except at the utmost exertion and effort. Whoever knows what we have mentioned of the sources of confusion in definition knows that human power cannot guard against all of that except rarely, and they are many. The most difficult for the mind are four matters: The first: we stipulated that one should take the 'nearest genus'. How can the seeker be certain he does not neglect it? So he takes a genus he thinks is nearest, but perhaps there is something nearer than it. Thus he defines 'wine' as 'an intoxicating liquid' and neglects 'beverage' which is beneath it and nearer, and defines 'human' as 'a body'...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary by al-Ghazali on the difficulty of constructing definitions according to the logicians' method. He begins by noting that the theologians' approach (mentioned earlier) is an alternative. Then he explains that definitions are extremely difficult for human intellects. He lists four fundamental obstacles; here only the first is given. The first obstacle is the requirement to take the 'nearest genus' (al-jins al-aqrab). The human mind often fails to identify the nearest genus, either overlooking it or mistaking a more remote genus for it. Al-Ghazali provides two examples: defining wine (khamr) as 'an intoxicating liquid' misses the nearer genus 'beverage' (sharab). Defining human as 'body' misses the nearer genus 'animal' (hayawān). This passage serves as a warning: even experienced logicians can err in definition due to the subtlety of identifying the correct genus.

Key Terms
الجنس الأقرب (al-jins al-aqrab): The nearest genus under which the defined falls directly; the most specific genus above the species. [Note: Standard term in logic; stable across the tradition.]
مثارات الاشتباه (muthārāt al-ishtibāh): Sources of confusion or ambiguity that lead to errors in definition. [Note: Specific to al-Ghazali's usage in Mi'yar al-'Ilm; not common outside his works.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Difficulty in finding the differentia
B) Difficulty in identifying the nearest genus
C) Difficulty in avoiding circular definitions
D) Difficulty in defining immaterial things

Key Takeaway
The first obstacle to a correct definition is failure to identify the nearest genus, leading to overly broad or inaccurate definitions.

The Confusion Between Essential and Inseparable Accident
Ibn Taymiyya enumerates the second difficulty: confusion between essential and inseparable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (dhati) and inseparable accident (lazim) and explain why Ibn Taymiyya argues that logicians cannot reliably separate them.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the fatal ambiguity between what is essential and what merely accompanies.

In his critique of the logicians' method of definition, Ibn Taymiyya raises a critical problem: how can one be sure that a given property is truly part of the essence (dhati) and not just an inseparable accident (lazim)? This distinction is foundational for constructing valid definitions, yet, as he shows, it is far from clear in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logician's condition
Recall that logicians require all differentiae (fusul) in a definition to be essential (dhatiyya). This means each differentia must be part of the thing's essence, not an accident.
2
Understand the inseparable accident
An inseparable accident (lazim) is a property that never leaves the thing in existence or imagination, yet it is not part of the essence. For example, the potential to laugh is inseparable from humans but not essential.
Ibn Taymiyya warns that such properties are easily mistaken for essential ones.
3
Analyse Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that distinguishing dhati from lazim is 'among the most obscure matters' (min aghmad al-umur). A logician might inadvertently pick a lazim and present it as a differentia, thinking it is essential.
This undermines the reliability of any definition built on such distinctions.
4
Assess the consequence
If the condition cannot be reliably met, then definitions based on it are suspect. The entire edifice of essential definition collapses if we cannot distinguish essence from accidental accompaniment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الاشتباه في «الذاتي». الثاني : أنا إذا شرطنا أن تكون «الفصول» «ذاتية» كلُّها، و«اللازم» الذي لا يفارق في الوجود والوَهْم يشتبه بـ «الذاتي» غاية الاشتباه، ودَرْكُ ذلك من أغمض الأمور. فمن و اللازم أين له أن لا يغفل فيأخذ «لازماً» فيورده بدل «الفصل» ويظن أنه «ذاتي» ! الثالث : أنا شرطنا أن يأتي بجميع الفصول الذاتية حتى لا يُخل بواحد، ومن أين اشتراط جميع يأمن من شذوذ بعضها عنه لا سيما إذا وجد «فصلاً» حصل به التمييز والمساواة في الاسم في الحمل ، كَـ «الجسم، ذى النفس، الحَسّاس ومساواته لفظ «الحيوان» مع إغفال المتحرك بالإرادة». وهذا من أغمض ما يُدرك.
The second: confusion regarding the essential. The second: If we stipulate that the differentiae be all essential, then the inseparable accident that does not separate in existence and imagination is extremely confused with the essential, and perceiving that is among the most obscure matters. So where does one have [the ability] not to be negligent, taking an accident instead of the differentia and thinking it is essential? The third: We stipulated that one bring all essential differentiae so as not to miss one. But where [does one get] the stipulation of all, [such that] one is safe from some of them escaping him, especially when he finds a differentia by which distinction and coextension in predication occur—like 'body, possessing soul, sentient' and its equivalence to the word 'animal' while omitting 'moving by will'? This is among the most obscure things to perceive.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents two objections (the second and third) to the logician's method of definition. Ibn Taymiyya first argues that the condition that all differentiae be essential is extremely difficult to fulfill because the 'essential' (dhati) and the 'inseparable accident' (lazim) are easily confused. The lazim is something that never leaves the thing in existence or imagination, yet it is not part of the essence. The logician might mistakenly think a lazim is a differentia (fasl). This is a serious problem because a definition must consist of essential differentiae. Second, the logician requires that all essential differentiae be brought so that none is omitted. But it is nearly impossible to be certain that no differentia is missing, especially when one finds a differentia that gives distinction and coextension, such as 'body, soul-possessing, sentient' which is coextensive with 'animal', but one might omit 'moving by will' which is also essential. Thus the conditions are unattainable.

Key Terms
الذاتي
اللازم
الفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Logicians cannot agree on what is essential.
D) The number of essential differentiae is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The confusion between essential and inseparable accident renders the logician's condition of essential differentiae practically unattainable.

The Impossibility of Completing All Essential Differentiae
Ibn Taymiyya enumerates the third difficulty: completeness of essential differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyya argues that the requirement to list all essential differentiae is impossible, using the example of 'animal'.

Core Idea
The demand to list every essential differentia is impossible in practice.

Ibn Taymiyya's third objection targets the logician's requirement that a definition must include all essential differentiae. He argues that one can never be certain that no differentia has been omitted, even when the definition appears complete. His example of defining 'animal' shows how an essential property can be overlooked.

Guided Path
1
State the logician's requirement
Logicians require that a definition include all essential differentiae of the definiendum. No essential property may be omitted; otherwise, the definition is incomplete and thus defective.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He asks: where does one get the assurance that no differentia has been missed? He uses the word 'aman' (safety) to highlight the impossibility of being certain.
3
Examine his example
He gives the sequence 'body, soul-possessing, sentient'. This set is coextensive with 'animal' – every sentient, soul-possessing body is an animal. However, 'moving by will' is another essential differentia of animal, but it is omitted here. The definition based on those three would be incomplete.
The missing differentia shows that even a seemingly complete definition can lack an essential property.
4
Assess the consequence
If one cannot guarantee completeness, then definitions can never be trusted as fully accurate. This undermines the logician's ideal of a perfect definition (hadd tamm).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage presents two objections (the second and third) to the logician's method of definition. Ibn Taymiyya first argues that the condition that all differentiae be essential is extremely difficult to fulfill because the 'essential' (dhati) and the 'inseparable accident' (lazim) are easily confused. The Consequence of the Mutakallimun's Definition of Definition
Author explains that due to difficulty, the mutakallimun accepted a definition as a discriminating description, but this leads to using mere properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the problematic consequence of the mutakallimun's definition of 'al-hadd' as 'al-qawl al-jami' al-mani'' and explain why it fails to fulfill the purpose of definition.

Core Idea
If 'definition' only requires discrimination, you could define 'man' as 'laughing' – a property, not an essence.

In the previous lesson, you saw the logicians' complex conditions for a valid definition. Here, Ibn Taymiyya explains that the mutakallimun (theologians), finding those conditions too difficult, opted for a simpler criterion: a definition need only be a 'comprehensive exclusive statement' that discriminates the defined from everything else. But Ibn Taymiyya points out a serious flaw: this minimal condition allows definitions based on mere properties (khawass), which tell you nothing about the essence of the thing. Let us unpack his critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mutakallimun's definition of 'al-hadd'
Find the Arabic phrase they used to define definition. It is 'al-qawl al-jāmiʿ al-māniʿ' – the comprehensive, exclusive statement. They stipulated only that it discriminates (tamyīz).
2
Understand what the mutakallimun dropped
Compare with the logicians' conditions: they required order, essential constituents (genus and differentia), etc. The mutakallimun removed all except discrimination. Why? Because the full art of definition is extremely difficult ('asur).
3
Recognize the consequence: sufficing with properties
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'And it follows from that sufficing with properties (khawass).' If only discrimination is needed, any unique feature – even a non-essential accident – can serve as the definition.
Example: defining 'horse' as 'the neighing' (al-suhāl) – neighing is a property, not an essence.
4
Evaluate the examples against the purpose of definition
The examples ('horse is neighing', 'man is laughing', 'dog is barking') are clearly insufficient. The purpose of definition (gharaḍ al-taʿrīf) is to define the essence (dhāt) of the thing. These examples only give a characteristic, not what the thing really is.
5
Formulate why the mutakallimun's approach fails
A good definition must reveal the essential nature (māhiyya). A mere property can be accidental or shared. 'Laughing' is not the essence of man. Thus the mutakallimun's easier path misses the true goal of definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرعاية الترتيب في هذه الأمور شرط للوفاء بصناعة الحد، وهي في غاية العُسر. ولذلك، لما عَسُر، اكتفى المتكلّمون بالتمييز وقالوا: إن الحد هو «القول الجامع المانع» ولم يشترطوا فيه إلا التمييز. ويلزم عليه الاكتفاء بـ«الخواص»، فيقال في حد «الفَرَس» إنه «الصهال»، وفي حد الإنسان» إنه «الضحاك»، وفي «الكلب»: إنه «النباح». وذلك في غاية البعد عن غرض التعريف لذات المحدود.
So observing the order in these matters is a condition for fulfilling the art of definition, and it is extremely difficult. Therefore, when it became difficult, the mutakallimun [theologians] sufficed with discrimination and said: definition is 'the comprehensive, exclusive statement' and they stipulated nothing in it except discrimination. And it follows from that sufficing with properties, so it is said in the definition of 'horse' that it is 'the neighing', and in the definition of 'man' that he is 'the laughing', and in 'dog' that it is 'the barking'. And that is extremely far from the purpose of definition, which is to define the essence of the defined.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents the response of the mutakallimun (theologians) to the difficulty of the logicians' conditions. Because the logicians' requirements are so hard to fulfill, the mutakallimun accepted a simpler definition: they defined 'al-hadd' as 'al-qawl al-jami' al-mani''—a comprehensive, exclusive statement—and only required that it distinguish the defined from others. However, Ibn Taymiyya points out a necessary consequence: if only discrimination is required, one can resort to properties (khawass) that are not essential. For example, one could define 'horse' as 'the neighing', 'man' as 'the laughing', and 'dog' as 'the barking'. These are mere properties, not essences. This shows that the mutakallimun's approach, while easier, fails to achieve the true goal of definition, which is to reveal the essence (dhat) of the thing. Thus the passage criticizes both the logicians' overly strict conditions and the theologians' overly lax alternative.

Key Terms
القول الجامع المانع
التمييز
الخاصة

The Two Purposes of Detailed Definitions: Practice and Understanding Philosophers' Terms
Author explains why he provides detailed definitions: for training and to understand philosophical terms used in refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two purposes Ibn Taymiyya sets forth for providing detailed definitions and explain the epistemological status he assigns to them.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya gives definitions for practice, not as ultimate truths, unless proven by demonstration.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians explains why he bothers to include a long list of definitions from the philosophers. He admits that definable things are infinite, so covering all is impossible. But he provides selected definitions for two crucial reasons: to train the reader in the art of definition, and to equip the reader with the philosophers' terminology so that meaningful debate is possible. However, he cautions that these definitions are mere explanations of names unless verified by demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two benefits explicitly stated
Read the Arabic and locate the two benefits (al-fa'idatan) that Ibn Taymiyya mentions. They are introduced with 'li-fa'idatayn'.
The benefits are: 1) gaining practice in composing definitions, and 2) understanding the meanings of terms used by philosophers.
2
Understand the first benefit: practice (al-durba)
Ibn Taymiyya says practice and testing in defining something inevitably strengthens one's ability. Why is practice necessary? Because definition is difficult (usur al-tahdid).
This benefit is about skill development, not about acquiring new knowledge.
3
Understand the second benefit: understanding philosophers' terms
Ibn Taymiyya references his earlier work Tahafut al-Falasifa, where he debated the philosophers. To debate them, one must use their language and terminology. Hence, he provides definitions of terms from metaphysics, physics, and a few from mathematics.
The terms are not exhaustive; he selected only those relevant to his critique.
4
Grasp the epistemological caution: sharh al-ism vs. hadd
Ibn Taymiyya states that these definitions should be taken as explanations of the name (sharh al-ism), not as true definitions (hadd), unless demonstration (burhan) confirms that the meaning matches reality. Why is this distinction crucial?
Sharh al-ism clarifies conventional meaning; hadd reveals essence. Without proof, a definition is provisional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأجل عُسْر التحديد قد رأينا أن نورد جملة من الحدود المعلومة المحرره في الفن الثاني من كتاب الحدود». ثم قال : الفن الثاني : في الحدود المفصلة اعلم أن الأشياء التي يمكن تحديدها لا نهاية لها لأن العلوم التصديقية غيرُ متناهية، وهي تابعة لـ «التصوّرات»، وأقل ما يشتمل عليه «التصديق» تصوران. وعلى الجملة فكل ما له اسم يمكن تحريرُ حدّه» أو «رسمه» أو «شرح اسمه». وإذا لم يكن في الاستقصاء مطمع، فالأولى الاقتصار على القوانين المعرفة لطريقه، وقد حصل ذلك بالفن الأول. ولكنا أوردنا حدوداً مفصلة لفائدتين :إحداهما : أن تحصل الدُّرْبة بكيفية تحرير الحد وتأليفه ؛ فإن الامتحان والممارسة فائدة الحدود للشيء يفيد قوة عليه لا محالة . الثانية : أن يقع الاطلاع على معاني أسماء أطلقها الفلاسفة . وقد أوردناها في كتاب تهافتُ الفَلاسفة إذ لم يمكن مُناظَرَتُهم إلا بلغتهم وعلى حكم اصطلاحهم، وإذا لم نفهم ما أوردناه من اصطلاحهم لا يمكن مُناظرتهم . فقد أوردنا حدود ألفاظ أطلقوها في «الإلهيات» و «الطبيعيات» وشيئاً قليلاً من «الرياضيات». فلتؤخَذ هذه الحدود على أنها «شرح للاسم». فإن قام البرهان على أن ما شرحو هو كما شرحوه اعتقد «حداً»، وإلا اعتقد «شرحاً للاسم».
And because of the difficulty of definition, we have decided to present a selection of known, accurate definitions in the second section of the book 'al-Hudud'. Then he said: The second section: on detailed definitions. Know that the things that can be defined are endless, because the sciences of assent are infinite, and they are subordinate to concepts, and the least that a piece of assent contains is two concepts. In general, everything that has a name can be given a 'definition' (hadd), a 'description' (rasm), or an 'explanation of the name' (sharh al-ism). Since there is no hope of covering all, it is better to confine ourselves to the rules that teach its method, and that has been done in the first section. But we have presented detailed definitions for two benefits: One: to gain practice in how to formulate and compose a definition; for testing and practice in defining something inevitably gives strength in it. The second: to become acquainted with the meanings of names that the philosophers have coined. We have presented them in the book 'Tahafut al-Falasifa', for it is not possible to debate with them except in their language and according to their terminology, and if we do not understand what we have cited from their terminology, we cannot debate them. So we have cited definitions of terms they coined in theology, natural sciences, and a few from mathematics. So let these definitions be taken as 'explanations of the name'. For if proof establishes that what they explained is as they explained it, then it is considered a 'definition' (hadd), otherwise it is considered an 'explanation of the name' (sharh al-ism).
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction to the second section of his book, where he will provide detailed definitions. Ibn Taymiyya explains that definable things are infinite because the sciences of assent (tasdiq), which are the subject matters of knowledge, are infinite, and each assent depends on at least two concepts (tasawwurat). Therefore, it is impossible to define everything. The first section of the book already provided the general rules for definition. The second section provides specific examples for two reasons. First, to give the reader practice (durba) in the art of forming definitions: repeated exercise strengthens the skill. Second, to help readers understand the technical terms used by the philosophers (falasifa), since Ibn Taymiyya had debated them in his earlier work 'Tahafut al-Falasifa' and needed to use their terminology. He therefore provides definitions of terms from metaphysics, physics, and a few from mathematics. Importantly, he states that these definitions should be regarded as mere 'explanations of the name' (sharh al-ism) rather than true definitions (hadd), unless proof (burhan) confirms that the meaning they gave matches the reality. This distinction is crucial: sharh al-ism is a clarification of a term's conventional meaning, while hadd reveals the essence. The passage thus clarifies the epistemological status of the definitions that follow.

Key Terms
الحد
الرسم
شرح الاسم
التصديق
التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, under what condition should a definition provided in this section be considered a true definition (hadd) rather than a mere explanation of the name (sharh al-ism)?
A) When it is taken from the philosophers' books.
B) When it is based on logical rules.
C) When proof (burhan) establishes that the meaning matches reality.
D) When it is used in a debate.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's detailed definitions serve a dual purpose: practical training and intellectual preparation for debate, and they are epistemologically provisional pending verification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify which of the two benefits (practice or understanding terminology) is more relevant when reading a passage from Ibn Taymiyya's response to a philosopher's argument about the eternity of the world. Explain your reasoning.

Hadd and Rasm

The Difficulty of Essential Definitions and the Need for Description
Al-Ghazali introduces the difficulty of essential definitions and the use of accidental descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the insufficiency of accidental definitions (rasm) compared to essential definitions (hadd) using examples from al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Essential definitions are so difficult that logicians often settle for accidental descriptions.

Al-Ghazali begins by acknowledging a key problem: the logicians' method of essential definition is extremely difficult, so much so that they are forced to rely on accidental properties (khawass) to define things. This reduces definitions to mere descriptions like 'horse is the neighing one,' which fails to capture the essence. Understanding this limitation is crucial for appreciating why the tradition developed both hadd and rasm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about essential definition
Read al-Ghazali's opening: 'استعصاء الحد على طريقة المنطقيين' – he states that definitions are unattainable by the logicians' method. What does he mean by 'استعصاء'?
It implies that essential definitions are inherently resistant to formulation.
2
Examine the examples of accidental definitions
Al-Ghazali gives three examples: horse defined as 'الصهال' (neighing), man as 'الضحاك' (laughing), dog as 'النباح' (barking). Notice that these are not essential properties but actions or characteristics.
These are 'rasm' – accidental descriptions, not true hadd.
3
Assess the purpose of defining essences
Al-Ghazali says these definitions are 'في غاية البعد عن غرض التعريف لذات المحدود' – far from the purpose of defining the essence. Why would a definition that only gives an accidental property fail?
Because it does not tell you what the thing is in itself, only one of its incidental features.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - استعصاء الحد على طريقة المنطقيين
ويلزم عليه الاكتفاء بـ«الخواص»، فيقال في حد «الفَرَس» إنه «الصهال»، وفي حد الإنسان» إنه «الضحاك»، وفي «الكلب»: إنه «النباح». وذلك في غاية البعد عن غرض التعريف لذات المحدود.
ولأجل عُسْر التحديد قد رأينا أن نورد جملة من الحدود المعلومة المحرره في
الفن الثاني من كتاب الحدود». 
ثم قال :
الفن الثاني : في الحدود المفصلة
اعلم أن الأشياء التي يمكن تحديدها لا نهاية لها لأن العلوم التصديقية غيرُ متناهية، وهي تابعة لـ «التصوّرات»، وأقل ما يشتمل عليه «التصديق» تصوران. وعلى الجملة فكل ما له اسم يمكن تحريرُ حدّه» أو «رسمه» أو «شرح اسمه». وإذا لم يكن في الاستقصاء مطمع، فالأولى الاقتصار على القوانين المعرفة لطريقه، وقد حصل ذلك بالفن الأول.
ولكنا أوردنا حدوداً مفصلة لفائدتين :
:إحداهما : أن تحصل الدُّرْبة بكيفية تحرير الحد وتأليفه ؛ فإن الامتحان والممارسة فائدة الحدود للشيء يفيد قوة عليه لا محالة .
الثانية : أن يقع الاطلاع على معاني أسماء أطلقها الفلاسفة [١٩]. وقد أوردناها في كتاب تهافتُ الفَلاسفة إذ لم يمكن مُناظَرَتُهم إلا بلغتهم وعلى حكم اصطلاحهم، وإذا لم نفهم ما أوردناه من اصطلاحهم لا يمكن مُناظرتهم . / فقد أوردنا حدود ألفاظ / ٢٢ أطلقوها في «الإلهيات» و «الطبيعيات وشيئاً قليلاً من «الرياضيات».
فلتؤخَذ هذه الحدود على أنها «شرح للاسم». فإن قام البرهان على أن الفرق بين «الحد» و «شرح الاسم» هو كما شرحوه اعتقد «حداً»، وإلا اعتقد «شرحاً للاسم».
كما يقال : حد «الجنّى»: «حيوان هوائي، ناطق، مُشَفْتُ الجزم، من شأنه أن يتشكل بأشكال مختلفة»، فيكون هذا شرحاً للاسم في تَفَاهُم الناس . فأما وجود هذا الشيء على هذا الوجه فيعرف بالبرهان. فإن دل على وجوده كان «حدا» بحسب الذات، وإن لم يدل عليه، بل دل على أن الجنّى المراد به في الشرع موجود آخر كان هذا «شرحاً للاسم في تفاهم الناس .
وكما تقول في حد «الخَلاء» إنه «بُعْدُ ، يمكن أن تُفْرَض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة، قائم لا في من شأنه أن يملأه جسم ويخلو عنه. وربما دل الدليل على أن ذلك محال، فيؤخذ على أنه شرح للاسم في إطلاق النظار .
مادة،
وإنما قدمنا هذه المقدّمة ليعلم أن ما نورده من الحدود شرح لما أراده الفلاسفة بالإطلاق، لا حكم بأن ما ذكروه كما ذكروه، فإن ذلك مما يتوقف على ما يوجبه البرهان .
The Second Station - The Inability of Definition according to the Method of the Logicians. It entails that one be satisfied with 'accidents' (khawass), so it is said in the definition of 'horse' that it is 'the neighing one', and in the definition of 'man' that it is 'the laughing one', and in 'dog' that it is 'the barking one'. That is extremely far from the purpose of defining the essence of the defined. Due to the difficulty of definition, we intend to present a number of known established definitions in the Second Art of the Book of Definitions. Then he said: The Second Art: Concerning Detailed Definitions. Know that the things that can be defined are endless because assenting sciences (al-‘ulum al-tasdiqiyya) are infinite, and they are subordinate to 'concepts' (al-tasawwurat), and the least that 'assent' (al-tasdiq) contains is two concepts. In short, everything that has a name can have its 'essential definition' (hadd), 'accidental definition' (rasm), or 'explanation of its name' (sharh ismihi) formulated. Since there is no hope of exhaustiveness, it is better to confine oneself to the rules that inform one of its method, and that has been achieved in the First Art. But we have presented detailed definitions for two benefits: One: to acquire practice in the method of formulating and composing a definition; for examination and practice of defining something undoubtedly confers strength in it. The second: to become acquainted with the meanings of names used by the philosophers. We have presented them in the book Tahafut al-Falasifah, since one cannot debate them except in their language and according to the rule of their convention, and if we do not understand what we have presented from their convention, debating them is not possible. So we have presented definitions of terms they have used in Theology (al-Ilahiyyat), Natural Sciences (al-Tabi’iyyat), and a little from Mathematics (al-Riyadiyyat). Let these definitions be taken as 'explanation of the name' (sharh al-ism). For if the proof establishes that the difference between 'essential definition' (al-hadd) and 'explanation of the name' (sharh al-ism) is as they have explained it, then it is believed to be an 'essential definition', otherwise it is believed to be an 'explanation of the name'. Just as it is said: the definition of 'jinn' is: 'an aerial, rational, transparent animal, of nature that it can take different forms', so this is an explanation of the name in the mutual understanding of people. As for the existence of this thing in this manner, it is known by proof. If proof indicates its existence, then it is an 'essential definition' with respect to the essence; if it does not indicate it, but rather indicates that the jinn meant in the Law is a different existent, then this is an 'explanation of the name in the mutual understanding of people'. And just as you say in the definition of 'void' (al-khala') that it is 'a dimension in which three dimensions can be supposed, subsisting not in a material, of nature that a body can fill it and be empty of it'. Perhaps proof indicates that that is impossible, so it is taken as an explanation of the name in the usage of the thinkers. We have advanced this introduction so that it be known that what we present of definitions are explanations of what the philosophers intended by usage, not a judgment that what they mentioned is as they mentioned it, for that depends on what proof necessitates.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a methodological explanation from al-Ghazali (or the logicians) regarding definitions. It begins by acknowledging the difficulty of formulating essential definitions according to the logicians' strict conditions, which leads to inadequate definitions like defining a horse as 'the neighing one'. Because of this difficulty, al-Ghazali presents detailed definitions from his book 'al-Hudud' (Definitions). He introduces the Second Art of that book, explaining that definitions are needed for countless terms, and he offers two benefits of providing detailed definitions: first, to give the reader practice in constructing definitions, and second, to acquaint the reader with the technical terms of the philosophers in order to enable debate with them. Al-Ghazali then clarifies a key distinction: the definitions he provides are to be taken as 'sharh al-ism' (explanation of the name) rather than true essential definitions (hadd). Only if a proof (burhan) confirms that the explanation matches the essence does it become a hadd. He illustrates this with the example of 'jinn', where the definition given is an explanation of what the name means in common usage, but its actual existence and true essence must be established by proof. Similarly, the definition of 'void' (al-khala') is given as a dimensional entity, but proof may show that such a thing is impossible, so it remains an explanation of the term as used by thinkers. The passage concludes by affirming that the definitions presented are merely explanations of the philosophers' intended meanings, not judgments of the truth of those definitions.

حد
رسم
خاصة

B) Because it does not capture the essence of being a horse.
C) Because it is too long.
D) Because it is only used in Persian.

Key Takeaway
Essential definitions are often unattainable, forcing logicians to use accidental descriptions which fail to capture the true nature of things.

Hadd and Rasm

The Two Benefits of Detailed Definitions
Al-Ghazali enumerates two benefits of providing detailed definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two reasons al-Ghazali provides detailed definitions: to gain practice in definition formulation and to understand philosophers' terminology for debate.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali gives detailed definitions not as definitive truths but as tools for practice and cross-philosophical communication.

After noting the impossibility of exhaustive definitions, al-Ghazali explains why he still provides detailed definitions in the Second Art of his Book of Definitions. He gives two clear benefits: first, to train the reader in the method of composing definitions, and second, to enable readers to understand the philosophers' technical language so they can engage in debate (munazara). This practical orientation shows that definitions are not ends in themselves but aids to scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the announcement of the Second Art
Al-Ghazali states: 'ولأجل عُسْر التحديد قد رأينا أن نورد جملة من الحدود المعلومة المحرره في الفن الثاني من كتاب الحدود'. Notice that he is providing 'established definitions' despite the difficulty.
2
Identify the first benefit: practice (durbah)
He says: 'أن تحصل الدُّرْبة بكيفية تحرير الحد وتأليفه' – to gain exercise in the how of formulating and composing a definition. This is a pedagogical aim.
Practice through examples strengthens the learner's ability.
3
Identify the second benefit: understanding philosophers' terms
He adds: 'أن يقع الاطلاع على معاني أسماء أطلقها الفلاسفة' – to become acquainted with the meanings of terms used by philosophers. This is necessary for debating them (munazara).
Al-Ghazali emphasizes that without knowing their language, debate is impossible.
4
Reflect on the implication
These definitions are not presented as ultimate truths but as functional tools. They are 'explanations of names' (sharh al-ism) subject to verification by proof (burhan).
This anticipates the next lesson's distinction.

Hadd and Rasm

Distinguishing Hadd from Sharh al-Ism: The Role of Proof
Al-Ghazali distinguishes between hadd and sharh al-ism using examples and the requirement of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an essential definition (hadd) and an explanation of the name (sharh al-ism) using the criteria of proof (burhan) and apply this distinction to examples like jinn and void.

Core Idea
A definition is only a hadd if proof confirms it matches the essence; otherwise it is merely a sharh al-ism.

Al-Ghazali now clarifies a crucial distinction: the definitions he presents are to be taken as explanations of the name (sharh al-ism) unless proof (burhan) establishes that they capture the true essence. He illustrates this with two examples: the definitions of 'jinn' and 'void'. In both cases, the given definitions may be common explanations, but their truth depends on proof. This epistemological humility is central to the Islamic approach to definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the key directive
Al-Ghazali says: 'فلتؤخَذ هذه الحدود على أنها «شرح للاسم».' – these definitions are to be taken as sharh al-ism. This is a conditional statement: if proof shows the definition matches the essence, then it becomes a hadd.
2
Analyze the jinn example
He defines jinn as: 'حيوان هوائي، ناطق، مُشَفْتُ الجزم، من شأنه أن يتشكل بأشكال مختلفة'. Identify the genus (حيوان) and differentia (هوائي، ناطق، etc.). Then note: this is a 'شرح للاسم في تفاهم الناس' unless proof confirms its existence.
The definition uses elements like 'aerial' and 'rational' which are not empirically verifiable.
3
Analyze the void example
He defines void as: 'بُعْدٌ يمكن أن تُفْرَض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة، قائم لا في مادة، من شأنه أن يملأه جسم ويخلو عنه'. This defines void as a dimension without matter. But proof may show this is impossible, so it remains an explanation of usage.
This shows that even widespread definitions among thinkers (النظار) may be false in reality.
4
Summarize the epistemological condition
Al-Ghazali concludes: the definitions are 'شرح لما أراده الفلاسفة بالإطلاق، لا حكم بأن ما ذكروه كما ذكروه' – they explain what philosophers mean, not a judgment that their claims are true. Truth depends on proof.
This is a principle of methodological neutrality for engaging with opposing doctrines.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - استعصاء الحد على طريقة المنطقيين
ويلزم عليه الاكتفاء بـ«الخواص»، فيقال في حد «الفَرَس» إنه «الصهال»، وفي حد الإنسان» إنه «الضحاك»، وفي «الكلب»: إنه «النباح». وذلك في غاية البعد عن غرض التعريف لذات المحدود.
ولأجل عُسْر التحديد قد رأينا أن نورد جملة من الحدود المعلومة المحرره في
الفن الثاني من كتاب الحدود». 
ثم قال :
الفن الثاني : في الحدود المفصلة
اعلم أن الأشياء التي يمكن تحديدها لا نهاية لها لأن العلوم التصديقية غيرُ متناهية، وهي تابعة لـ «التصوّرات»، وأقل ما يشتمل عليه «التصديق» تصوران. وعلى الجملة فكل ما له اسم يمكن تحريرُ حدّه» أو «رسمه» أو «شرح اسمه». وإذا لم يكن في الاستقصاء مطمع، فالأولى الاقتصار على القوانين المعرفة لطريقه، وقد حصل ذلك بالفن الأول.
ولكنا أوردنا حدوداً مفصلة لفائدتين :
:إحداهما : أن تحصل الدُّرْبة بكيفية تحرير الحد وتأليفه ؛ فإن الامتحان والممارسة فائدة الحدود للشيء يفيد قوة عليه لا محالة .
الثانية : أن يقع الاطلاع على معاني أسماء أطلقها الفلاسفة [١٩]. وقد أوردناها في كتاب تهافتُ الفَلاسفة إذ لم يمكن مُناظَرَتُهم إلا بلغتهم وعلى حكم اصطلاحهم، وإذا لم نفهم ما أوردناه من اصطلاحهم لا يمكن مُناظرتهم . / فقد أوردنا حدود ألفاظ / ٢٢ أطلقوها في «الإلهيات» و «الطبيعيات وشيئاً قليلاً من «الرياضيات».
فلتؤخَذ هذه الحدود على أنها «شرح للاسم». فإن قام البرهان على أن الفرق بين «الحد» و «شرح الاسم» هو كما شرحوه اعتقد «حداً»، وإلا اعتقد «شرحاً للاسم».
كما يقال : حد «الجنّى»: «حيوان هوائي، ناطق، مُشَفْتُ الجزم، من شأنه أن يتشكل بأشكال مختلفة»، فيكون هذا شرحاً للاسم في تَفَاهُم الناس . فأما وجود هذا الشيء على هذا الوجه فيعرف بالبرهان. فإن دل على وجوده كان «حدا» بحسب الذات، وإن لم يدل عليه، بل دل على أن الجنّى المراد به في الشرع موجود آخر كان هذا «شرحاً للاسم في تفاهم الناس .
وكما تقول في حد «الخَلاء» إنه «بُعْدُ ، يمكن أن تُفْرَض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة، قائم لا في من شأنه أن يملأه جسم ويخلو عنه. وربما دل الدليل على أن ذلك محال، فيؤخذ على أنه شرح للاسم في إطلاق النظار .
مادة،
وإنما قدمنا هذه المقدّمة ليعلم أن ما نورده من الحدود شرح لما أراده الفلاسفة بالإطلاق، لا حكم بأن ما ذكروه كما ذكروه، فإن ذلك مما يتوقف على ما يوجبه البرهان .
The Second Station - The Inability of Definition according to the Method of the Logicians. It entails that one be satisfied with 'accidents' (khawass), so it is said in the definition of 'horse' that it is 'the neighing one', and in the definition of 'man' that it is 'the laughing one', and in 'dog' that it is 'the barking one'. That is extremely far from the purpose of defining the essence of the defined. Due to the difficulty of definition, we intend to present a number of known established definitions in the Second Art of the Book of Definitions. Then he said: The Second Art: Concerning Detailed Definitions. Know that the things that can be defined are endless because assenting sciences (al-‘ulum al-tasdiqiyya) are infinite, and they are subordinate to 'concepts' (al-tasawwurat), and the least that 'assent' (al-tasdiq) contains is two concepts. In short, everything that has a name can have its 'essential definition' (hadd), 'accidental definition' (rasm), or 'explanation of its name' (sharh ismihi) formulated. Since there is no hope of exhaustiveness, it is better to confine oneself to the rules that inform one of its method, and that has been achieved in the First Art. But we have presented detailed definitions for two benefits: One: to acquire practice in the method of formulating and composing a definition; for examination and practice of defining something undoubtedly confers strength in it. The second: to become acquainted with the meanings of names used by the philosophers. We have presented them in the book Tahafut al-Falasifah, since one cannot debate them except in their language and according to the rule of their convention, and if we do not understand what we have presented from their convention, debating them is not possible. So we have presented definitions of terms they have used in Theology (al-Ilahiyyat), Natural Sciences (al-Tabi’iyyat), and a little from Mathematics (al-Riyadiyyat). Let these definitions be taken as 'explanation of the name' (sharh al-ism). For if the proof establishes that the difference between 'essential definition' (al-hadd) and 'explanation of the name' (sharh al-ism) is as they have explained it, then it is believed to be an 'essential definition', otherwise it is believed to be an 'explanation of the name'. Just as it is said: the definition of 'jinn' is: 'an aerial, rational, transparent animal, of nature that it can take different forms', so this is an explanation of the name in the mutual understanding of people. As for the existence of this thing in this manner, it is known by proof. If proof indicates its existence, then it is an 'essential definition' with respect to the essence; if it does not indicate it, but rather indicates that the jinn meant in the Law is a different existent, then this is an 'explanation of the name in the mutual understanding of people'. And just as you say in the definition of 'void' (al-khala') that it is 'a dimension in which three dimensions can be supposed, subsisting not in a material, of nature that a body can fill it and be empty of it'. Perhaps proof indicates that that is impossible, so it is taken as an explanation of the name in the usage of the thinkers. We have advanced this introduction so that it be known that what we present of definitions are explanations of what the philosophers intended by usage, not a judgment that what they mentioned is as they mentioned it, for that depends on what proof necessitates.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a methodological explanation from al-Ghazali (or the logicians) regarding definitions. It begins by acknowledging the difficulty of formulating essential definitions according to the logicians' strict conditions, which leads to inadequate definitions like defining a horse as 'the neighing one'. Because of this difficulty, al-Ghazali presents detailed definitions from his book 'al-Hudud' (Definitions). He introduces the Second Art of that book, explaining that definitions are needed for countless terms, and he offers two benefits of providing detailed definitions: first, to give the reader practice in constructing definitions, and second, to acquaint the reader with the technical terms of the philosophers in order to enable debate with them. Al-Ghazali then clarifies a key distinction: the definitions he provides are to be taken as 'sharh al-ism' (explanation of the name) rather than true essential definitions (hadd). Only if a proof (burhan) confirms that the explanation matches the essence does it become a hadd. He illustrates this with the example of 'jinn', where the definition given is an explanation of what the name means in common usage, but its actual existence and true essence must be established by proof. Similarly, the definition of 'void' (al-khala') is given as a dimensional entity, but proof may show that such a thing is impossible, so it remains an explanation of the term as used by thinkers. The passage concludes by affirming that the definitions presented are merely explanations of the philosophers' intended meanings, not judgments of the truth of those definitions.

شرح الاسم
برهان
الجنّى
الخَلاء

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Essentialist Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya refutes al-Ghazali's claim that definitions are difficult.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key objections Ibn Taymiyya raises against the logicians' theory of definition and explain why he argues that definitions only produce distinction (tamyiz).

Core Idea
Definitions do not reveal essences; they only distinguish one thing from another.

Ibn Taymiyya here directly confronts al-Ghazali's claim that definitions are difficult because they must capture the essence. He argues that the logicians' entire framework is built on false distinctions and impossible goals. Understanding his critique is crucial for anyone who wants to use definitions rigorously in Islamic scholarship without falling into metaphysical speculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
The logicians claim that a proper definition (hadd) must depict the essence (taswir al-mahiyya) by listing its constitutive essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyya al-muqawwima) and must include both a genus and differentia.
Al-Ghazali's position represents this view.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's concession
He concedes that if the purpose were truly to depict the essence, it would indeed be difficult. But he immediately denies that this is possible.
This concession is rhetorical; it sets up his critique.
3
Extract his three objections
First, definition cannot depict the essence. Second, the distinction between constitutive essential attributes and external necessary attributes is baseless. Third, requiring two attributes (genus and differentia) is arbitrary; the theologians (mutakallimun) rightly reject it.
Each objection undermines a pillar of the logicians' theory.
4
Recognize his positive claim
Definitions only provide distinction (tamyiz). Since distinction can be achieved by a single differentia (fasl) or even a property (khassa), the logicians' conditions are unnecessary.
Ibn Taymiyya redefines the purpose of definition.
5
Compare with the theologians' method
The mutakallimun, by avoiding metaphysical commitments, achieve the goal of definition more soundly. Their definitions are practical and focus on distinguishing things, not revealing essences.
This aligns with Ibn Taymiyya's overall epistemological realism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الردّ على كلام الغزالي
قلت : ما ذكره من صعوبة الحدّ على الشروط التي ذَكَرها حق لو كان المقصود بالحد تصوير المحدود كما يدَّعونه وكان ذلك ممكناً، لكنّ ما ذكروه في الحد باطل، فإنه يمتنع [٢٠] أن يحصل بمجرد الحد تصوير المحدود . وما ذكروه من الفرق بين الصفات الذاتية المقوّمة الداخلة في الماهية والصفاتِ الخارجة اللازمة أمر باطل لا حقيقة له. وما أوجبوه من ذكر الصفتين في الحدود هو مما يحظره المتكلمون فيمتنعون منه في الحد. والتحقيق أنه لا واجب ولا محظور، كما قد بيناه في موضع آخر .
وكل ما يذكرونه من الحدود فإنما يفيد التمييز، وإذا كان لا يحصل بالحد إلا التمييز فالتمييز قد يحصل بـ «الفصل» و«الخاصة». فعلم أن طريقة المتكلمين أسد في الحدود اسد تحصيل المقصود الصحيح بالحدود. وأما قوله : إن ذلك في غاية البعد عن غَرَض التعريف لذات المحدود» فيقال:
The refutation of al-Ghazali's statement. I say: What he mentioned regarding the difficulty of definition on the conditions he stated is true if the purpose of definition were to depict (taswir) the defined as they claim and if that were possible. However, what they mentioned regarding definition is false, for it is impossible to obtain by mere definition a depiction of the defined. What they mentioned regarding the distinction between constitutive essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyya al-muqawwima) that are internal to the quiddity and external necessary attributes (al-sifat al-kharija al-lazima) is a false matter with no reality. What they required of mentioning two attributes in definitions is something that the theologians (mutakallimun) prohibit; they refrain from it in definitions. The truth is that there is no requirement and no prohibition, as we have explained elsewhere. All the definitions they mention only provide distinction (tamyiz). If the only thing obtained by definition is distinction, then distinction can be obtained by the differentia (fasl) and the property (khassa). Thus, it is known that the method of the theologians is sounder in definitions for achieving the correct purpose of definitions. As for his statement: 'That is extremely far from the purpose of defining the essence of the defined', it is said: (the text cuts off).
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's critical response to al-Ghazali's earlier discussion on the difficulty of definition. He begins by conceding that if the purpose of definition were to produce a mental depiction of the essence (taswir al-mahiyya), then the difficulty would be real. However, he argues that this goal is impossible—definitions cannot depict the essence. He then attacks the logicians' distinction between constitutive essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyya al-muqawwima) that enter into the quiddity (mahiyya) and external necessary attributes (al-sifat al-kharija al-lazima), calling it baseless. He also points out that the logicians' requirement of mentioning two attributes (presumably genus and differentia) is something the theologians (mutakallimun) reject. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that there is no binding rule requiring or prohibiting specific components in a definition. In his view, definitions serve only one function: distinction (tamyiz) of one thing from others. Since distinction can be achieved by a single differentia (fasl) or even a single property (khassa), the theologians' approach is simpler and more effective. He then directly responds to al-Ghazali's claim that defining a horse as 'the neighing one' is far from the purpose of defining the essence, but the text cuts off at 'it is said:'.

Key Terms
تصوير (taswir)
صفات ذاتية مقومة (sifat dhatiyya muqawwima)
صفات خارجية لازمة (sifat kharija lazimah)
تمييز (tamyiz)
فصل (fasl)
خاصة (khassa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's central argument is that definitions cannot reveal essences; their only function is to distinguish one thing from another, which can be achieved by a single attribute.
