Real vs Nominal Definitions: Ibn Taymiyyah's Distinction
Author gives examples of definitions as mere explanations of names and introduces his method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definition (hadd bi hasab al-dhat) and a nominal explanation (sharh al-ism) in any given passage from the Islamic sciences, and apply Ibn Taymiyyah's criterion of proof.

Core Idea
A definition is only as good as the proof that its object exists; otherwise it is just a name.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyyah introduces a crucial distinction for anyone dealing with definitions in Islamic thought. He argues that many definitions given by philosophers are merely explanations of what a name means in common usage (sharh al-ism), not genuine definitions that capture the essence (hadd). Using the examples of 'jinn' and 'void', he shows that a definition becomes real only if the existence of the defined thing is proven by demonstration (burhan). This insight is vital for evaluating claims in kalam, usul al-fiqh, and other disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined terms
Locate the two entities being defined in the Arabic passage: 'al-jinn' (الجنّى) and 'al-khala' (الخَلاء). Note the definitions given for each.
The definition of jinn includes 'airy animal, rational, transparent of determination, capable of various forms'. The definition of void includes 'a distance in which three dimensions can be supposed, subsisting not in a substance'.
2
Extract genus and differentia
For each definition, separate the genus (the broader category) from the differentia (the distinguishing features). For jinn: genus = 'airy animal', differentia = 'rational, transparent of determination, capable of various forms'. For void: genus = 'distance' (or 'dimension'), differentia = 'subsisting not in a substance, can be filled or emptied'.
The logicians use these components to claim a complete definition, but Ibn Taymiyyah questions their status.
3
Check for an existence claim
Determine whether the definition implicitly or explicitly claims that the defined thing actually exists in reality. Here, both definitions are presented as if they describe something real, but Ibn Taymiyyah notes that existence is not yet established.
The definition of jinn is what people commonly understand, but the actual jinn in the Shari'ah may be different.
4
Apply Ibn Taymiyyah's criterion
Ask: Is there a demonstration (burhan) proving the existence of this thing as defined? If yes, the definition is real (hadd bi hasab al-dhat). If no, it is merely a nominal explanation (sharh al-ism). Ibn Taymiyyah states that the philosopher's definitions are only explanatory until proven.
For void, the proof may show it is impossible, so the definition remains nominal.
5
Classify the definitions
Based on the criterion, classify the definitions of jinn and void as either real or nominal. According to Ibn Taymiyyah, both are nominal because their existence has not been demonstrated by proof; they are just explanations of the terms in usage.
This classification sets the stage for his critique of the logicians' claims to essential definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما يقال : حد «الجنّى»: «حيوان هوائي، ناطق، مُشَفْتُ الجزم، من شأنه أن يتشكل بأشكال مختلفة»، فيكون هذا شرحاً للاسم في تَفَاهُم الناس . فأما وجود هذا الشيء على هذا الوجه فيعرف بالبرهان. فإن دل على وجوده كان «حدا» بحسب الذات، وإن لم يدل عليه، بل دل على أن الجنّى المراد به في الشرع موجود آخر كان هذا «شرحاً للاسم في تفاهم الناس . وكما تقول في حد «الخَلاء» إنه «بُعْدُ ، يمكن أن تُفْرَض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة، قائم لا في مادة، من شأنه أن يملأه جسم ويخلو عنه. وربما دل الدليل على أن ذلك محال، فيؤخذ على أنه شرح للاسم في إطلاق النظار . وإنما قدمنا هذه المقدّمة ليعلم أن ما نورده من الحدود شرح لما أراده الفلاسفة بالإطلاق، لا حكم بأن ما ذكروه كما ذكروه، فإن ذلك مما يتوقف على ما يوجبه البرهان .
As they say: The definition of 'jinn' is: 'an airy animal, rational, transparent of determination, capable of taking various forms,' so this is an explanation of the name in people's mutual understanding. As for the existence of this thing in this way, it is known by demonstration. If it indicates its existence, it is a 'definition' in terms of essence; if it does not indicate it, but rather indicates that the jinn meant in the Shari'ah is another existent, then this is an 'explanation of the name in people's mutual understanding.' And similarly, you say in the definition of 'void' that it is 'a distance in which three dimensions can be supposed, subsisting not in a substance, such that a body can fill it and be empty of it.' And perhaps the proof indicates that this is impossible, so it is taken as an explanation of the name in the usage of the thinkers. And we have only presented this preface so that it be known that what we present of definitions is an explanation of what the philosophers meant by the term, not a judgment that what they mentioned is as they mentioned it, for that depends on what the proof necessitates.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of definitions according to the mutakallimun. It distinguishes between definitions that capture the essence (hadd bi hasab al-dhat) and those that merely explain the name (sharh al-ism). Examples from jinn and void illustrate that many so-called definitions are actually just nominal explanations until the existence of the thing is proven. The author emphasizes that his own definitions are only explanatory of the philosophers' usage, not endorsements of their truth. This sets the stage for his critique.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A definition that captures the essence (dhat) if it corresponds to reality; otherwise it is just a nominal explanation. [Note: In standard logic, hadd is always a real definition; here Ibn Taymiyyah distinguishes real from nominal.]
شرح الاسم (sharh al-ism): An explanation of the meaning of a term in common usage, not necessarily reflecting the essence. [Note: This term is not commonly used in the logical tradition; Ibn Taymiyyah introduces it to critique the logicians.]
برهان (burhan): Demonstration that proves the existence and nature of a thing, as opposed to mere definition. [Note: Stable across traditions.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whether it uses genus and differentia.
B) Whether the existence of the defined thing is proven by demonstration.
C) Whether it is used by philosophers.
D) Whether it is in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
A definition is real only if the existence of the defined thing is proven by demonstration; otherwise it is merely an explanation of the term.

Ibn Taymiyyah on the Conventionality of the Proximate Genus
Author analyzes the necessity of taking the proximate genus and shows it is conventional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyyah considers the requirement of the proximate genus to be conventional, and identify the types of signification (tadammun, iltizam, mutabaqa) in a given definition.

Core Idea
The differentia can imply the proximate genus; requiring explicit mention is conventional, not essential.

Logicians often insist that a proper definition must include the proximate genus and the differentia. But is this rule absolute? Ibn Taymiyyah challenges its necessity, arguing that the differentia itself can indicate the proximate genus through linguistic implication. In this lesson, we will dissect his critique and learn to recognize the three modes of signification—correspondence, implication, and commitment—that underlie definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The standard logical rule is that a complete definition requires the proximate genus (e.g., 'animal' for human) and the differentia ('rational'). Ibn Taymiyyah questions this necessity.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyyah's alternative
He argues that the differentia alone can indicate the proximate genus by implication (tadammun) or commitment (iltizam). For example, 'intoxicant' implies 'beverage', just as 'rational' implies 'animal'.
3
Recognize the three types of signification
Mutabaqa (correspondence): the term fully signifies the meaning. Tadammun (implication): the term signifies a part of the meaning. Iltizam (commitment): the term signifies something necessarily attached to the meaning.
Ibn Taymiyyah uses these terms to show that the differentia can substitute for the proximate genus.
4
Apply the analysis to examples
Take the definition 'liquid intoxicant' for khamr. The word 'intoxicant' indicates 'beverage' by tadammun or iltizam, so explicitly mentioning 'beverage' is not strictly necessary. Similarly, 'rational animal' for human: 'rational' implies 'animal'.

Source Text

فإن ما ذكره من لزوم أخذ الجنس القريب أمرُ اصطلاحي . وذلك أنه إذا أخذ الوجه الأول : «البعيد كان «الفصل» يدل على القريب بالتضمّن أو الالتزام كدلالة «القريب» على دلالة «البعيد» . فَـ «الناطق» عندهم يدل على الحيوان، والمسكر» على «الشراب»، كما يدل «الحيوان على الجسم، وكما يدل «الشراب» على «المائع، سواء جعلوا هذه القريب» دلالة تضمن أو التزام. فإن كانوا يكتفون بمثل هذه الدلالة كان «الفصل» كافياً، وإن كانوا لا يكتفون إلا بما يدل على الذاتيات بالمطابقة لم يكن ذكرُ الجنس القريب وحده كافياً. فإذا قال: «مائع مسكر كان لفظ «المسكر» يدل على أنه «الشراب»، فإن المسكر هنا أخص عندهم من «الشراب ومن المائع» وهو «فصل»، كـ «الناطق» لـ «الإنسان» [۲۱] . ومعلوم حينئذ أن كل مسكر «شراب كما أن «كل ناطق حيوان». كما أنه إذا قيل في «الإنسان» جسم ناطق» ، فَ «الناطق» عندهم أخص من «الجسم» ومن الحيوان»، وهو يدل على «الحيوان» بالتضمن أو الالتزام . ودل(۳) لفظ «المائع» (۳) على الجنس البعيد بالمطابقة . وإذا قال: شراب مسكر دل قوله «شراب» على أنه «مائع بالتضمن أو الالتزام عندهم، ودل على الجنس القريب» بالمطابقة . فصار الفرق بينهما أنه تارة يدلّ على /٢٤ «الجنس البعيد» بالمطابقة والقريب بالتضمن وتارةً بالعكس. فإذا قالوا : الأحسن أن يدلّ على القريب بالمطابقة لأنه أخص بالمحدود، وكلَّما الأمور الأربعة التي ذكرها الإمام الغزالي بأنها أعصى مُثارات الاشتباه في «الحد» على الذهن .
For what he mentioned about the necessity of taking the proximate genus is a conventional matter. That is because if one takes the first aspect: the 'distant [genus],' the 'differentia' indicates the proximate [genus] by implication (tadammun) or by commitment (iltizam), just like the indication of the 'proximate' indicates the 'distant.' So 'rational' according to them indicates 'animal,' and 'intoxicant' indicates 'beverage,' just as 'animal' indicates 'body,' and as 'beverage' indicates 'liquid,' whether they make this indication by implication or commitment. If they are satisfied with such indication, then the 'differentia' is sufficient; if they are not satisfied except with what indicates the essentials by correspondence (mutabaqa), then mentioning the proximate genus alone is not sufficient. So if one says: 'a liquid intoxicant,' the word 'intoxicant' indicates that it is 'beverage,' for the intoxicant here is more specific according to them than 'beverage' and 'liquid,' and it is a 'differentia,' like 'rational' for 'human.' And it is known then that every intoxicant is a beverage, just as every rational is an animal. Similarly, if it is said about 'human': 'a rational body,' then 'rational' according to them is more specific than 'body' and 'animal,' and it indicates 'animal' by implication or commitment. And the word 'liquid' indicates the distant genus by correspondence. And if one says: 'an intoxicating beverage,' his saying 'beverage' indicates that it is 'liquid' by implication or commitment according to them, and indicates the 'proximate genus' by correspondence. So the difference between them is that sometimes it indicates the 'distant genus' by correspondence and the proximate by implication, and sometimes the opposite. So if they say: 'It is better to indicate the proximate by correspondence because it is more specific to the defined, and the more... the four aspects that Imam al-Ghazali mentioned as the most arousing ambiguity regarding definition in the mind.'

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between genus and differentia in definitions. Ibn Taymiyyah argues that the requirement to mention the proximate genus is conventional rather than essential. He shows that the differentia can serve to indicate the genus by implication (tadammun or iltizam), so it is not necessary to explicitly state the proximate genus. He uses examples like 'liquid intoxicant' and 'rational animal' to illustrate that the differentia is sufficient. He also points out that the distinction between proximate and distant genus is flexible depending on which term is used by correspondence. This is a critical analysis of logical terminology, showing that the logicians' own rules are not absolute.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Proximate Genus Requirement
Ibn Taymiyya argues that specifying the proximate genus is conventional and unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument that specifying the proximate genus in definitions is conventional, and evaluate his reasoning using the concepts of tadammun and iltizam.

Core Idea
The proximate genus is not necessary; the differentia can indicate it by inclusion or implication.

In classical Islamic logic, it was often claimed that a proper definition must include the proximate genus (al-jins al-qarib) stated by correspondence (mutabaqa). Ibn Taymiyya challenges this requirement as merely conventional (istilahi), not essential for conceptualizing the essence. In this passage, he uses the relations of inclusion (tadammun) and implication (iltizam) to show that the differentia can fulfill the role of the genus.

Guided Path
1
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's thesis
Find the sentence where he says the requirement is conventional ('amr istilahi). This is the core claim.
The phrase is 'ما ذكره من لزوم أخذ الجنس القريب أمر اصطلاحي'.
2
Understand tadammun and iltizam
Distinguish between inclusion (tadammun): one term's meaning contains another, and implication (iltizam): one term necessitates another but is not part of its definition.
For example, 'human' includes 'animal' (tadammun) and implies 'capable of laughter' (iltizam).
3
Apply analysis to Ibn Taymiyya's examples
Trace how 'rational' (natiq) indicates 'animal' by tadammun or iltizam, and how 'intoxicant' (muskir) indicates 'beverage' (sharāb).
The differentia can serve to indicate the genus without explicitly stating it.
4
Compare the two definitional strategies
Ibn Taymiyya shows that whether one uses remote genus by correspondence and differentia by implication, or vice versa, is a conventional choice, not a logical necessity.
Neither strategy is inherently superior for depicting essence (taswir al-mahiyya).
5
Evaluate the wine example
See how the differentia 'intoxicant' works even without specifying 'beverage' as a proximate genus, especially given the prophetic definition of khamr as 'every intoxicant'.
This demonstrates the flexibility permitted by Islamic law and language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك سائر الحدود هي غير محصّلة لتصوير ذات المحدود، كما سنبينه إن شاء الله تعالى. وما ذكره من الوجوه حجة عليهم. على الجنس فإن ما ذكره من لزوم أخذ الجنس القريب أمر اصطلاحي. وذلك أنه إذا أخذ الوجه الأول: البعيد كان الفصل يدل على القريب بالتضمن أو الالتزام كدلالة القريب على دلالة البعيد. فالناطق عندهم يدل على الحيوان، والمسكر على الشراب، كما يدل الحيوان على الجسم، وكما يدل الشراب على المائع، سواء جعلوا هذه الدلالة تضمن أو التزام. فإن كانوا يكتفون بمثل هذه الدلالة كان الفصل كافياً، وإن كانوا لا يكتفون إلا بما يدل على الذاتيات بالمطابقة لم يكن ذكر الجنس القريب وحده كافياً. فإذا قال: مائع مسكر كان لفظ المسكر يدل على أنه الشراب، فإن المسكر هنا أخص عندهم من الشراب ومن المائع وهو فصل، كالناطق للإنسان. ومعلوم حينئذ أن كل مسكر شراب كما أن كل ناطق حيوان. كما أنه إذا قيل في الإنسان جسم ناطق، فالناطق عندهم أخص من الجسم ومن الحيوان، وهو يدل على الحيوان بالتضمن أو الالتزام. ودل لفظ المائع على الجنس البعيد بالمطابقة. وإذا قال: شراب مسكر دل قوله شراب على أنه مائع بالتضمن أو الالتزام عندهم، ودل على الجنس القريب بالمطابقة. فصار الفرق بينهما أنه تارة يدل على الجنس البعيد بالمطابقة والقريب بالتضمن وتارةً بالعكس. فإذا قالوا: الأحسن أن يدل على القريب بالمطابقة لأنه أخص بالمحدود، وكلما كان أخص كان أكثر تمييزاً قيل: ليس في ذلك اختصاص أحد الحدين بتصوير الماهية دون الآخر، بل بأنه أتم تمييزاً. وهذا تكلّم على المثال الذي ذكره في حد الخمر بحسب ما قاله. لكل مسكر عند الشارع الخمر اسم لكل مسكر عند الشارع سواء كان مائعاً أو جامداً، طعاماً أو شراباً، كما قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: كل مسكر خمر. فلو كانت الخمر جامدة وأكلها كانت خمراً باتفاق المسلمين.
And likewise all definitions are not productive of the conceptualization of the essence of the defined, as we will explain if God wills. And what he mentioned of the aspects is an argument against them. On the genus: what he mentioned about the necessity of taking the proximate genus is a conventional matter. That is because when taking the first aspect: the remote, the differentia indicates the proximate by inclusion or implication, just as the proximate indicates the remote. For 'rational' according to them indicates 'animal', and 'intoxicant' indicates 'beverage', just as 'animal' indicates 'body', and just as 'beverage' indicates 'liquid', whether they consider this indication as inclusion or implication. If they are satisfied with such indication, then the differentia is sufficient; if they are not satisfied except with what indicates the essentials by correspondence, then mentioning the proximate genus alone is not sufficient. So if one says: 'a liquid intoxicant', the word 'intoxicant' indicates that it is 'beverage', for the intoxicant here is more specific than 'beverage' and 'liquid' and it is a differentia, like 'rational' for 'human'. It is known then that every intoxicant is a beverage, just as every rational is an animal. Likewise, if one says for human: 'a rational body', then 'rational' for them is more specific than 'body' and 'animal', and it indicates 'animal' by inclusion or implication. And the word 'liquid' indicates the remote genus by correspondence. And if one says: 'an intoxicating beverage', his saying 'beverage' indicates that it is a liquid by inclusion or implication according to them, and it indicates the proximate genus by correspondence. So the difference between them is that sometimes it indicates the remote genus by correspondence and the proximate by inclusion, and sometimes the opposite. So if they say: 'It is better to indicate the proximate by correspondence because it is more specific to the defined, and the more specific, the more distinctive', it is said: 'There is no exclusivity in one of the two definitions in depicting the essence over the other, but rather it is more perfect in distinctiveness.' And this is speech about the example he mentioned in the definition of wine according to what he said. Wine is a name for every intoxicant according to the Lawgiver, whether it is liquid or solid, food or drink, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Every intoxicant is wine.' So if wine were solid and one ate it, it would be wine by agreement of the Muslims.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the requirement of logicians like al-Ghazali that definitions must use the proximate genus. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that all definitions (hudud) do not actually produce a true conceptualization (tasawwur) of the essence of the defined, as he will explain later. He then argues that the requirement to take the proximate genus is merely conventional (istilahi), not essential. He illustrates this by considering a definition that uses the differentia (fasl) with a remote genus: the differentia can indicate the proximate genus either by inclusion (tadammun) or by implication (iltizam). For example, the differentia 'rational' (natiq) indicates 'animal', and 'intoxicant' (muskir) indicates 'beverage' (sharāb). Similarly, if one says 'a liquid intoxicant' (ma'i' muskir), the word 'intoxicant' is more specific than both 'beverage' and 'liquid' and serves as the differentia. It implies that every intoxicant is a beverage, just as every rational is an animal. The discussion shows that it does not matter whether one indicates the proximate genus by correspondence (mutabaqa) or the differentia by implication; both are valid ways to capture the essence. Ibn Taymiyya then addresses the objection that it is better to indicate the proximate genus by correspondence because it is more specific and distinctive. He responds that this does not make one definition superior in depicting the essence (taswir al-mahiyya); it only makes it more perfectly distinctive (atammu tamyizan). The example of wine (khamr) is introduced to show that the definition 'an intoxicating beverage' (sharāb muskir) works even if the wine is solid, because the Lawgiver defines khamr as anything intoxicating, regardless of state. Thus, the requirement to use the proximate genus is not necessary; the differentia alone suffices.

الفصل
التضمن
الالتزام
المطابقة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the requirement to include the proximate genus in definitions is a conventional choice, not a logical necessity; the differentia can fulfill the same role through inclusion or implication.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Dhati/Lazim Distinction
Author critiques the logicians' distinction between essential and accidental properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the dhati/lazim distinction and explain why he considers the logicians' distinction arbitrary and conventional.

Core Idea
The dhati/lazim distinction is merely conventional, not grounded in reality.

Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at a foundational pillar of Aristotelian logic: the distinction between essential properties (dhati) and necessary concomitants (lazim). He begins by acknowledging the logicians' own admission that the two are extremely similar. Then he drives his critique: the difference is purely conventional, not real. This exposes the whole project of defining essences as based on arbitrary stipulation rather than objective reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' distinction
Find where the passage refers to dhati (essential property) and lazim (necessary concomitant). Note the logicians' claim that lazim never separates from the essence but is not part of its definition.
The logicians consider dhati as conceptually prior and part of the essence; lazim is a necessary accompaniment but not part of the essence.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's starting point
He quotes the logicians' statement that dhati and lazim are extremely similar. He agrees with this observation, but uses it to undermine their distinction.
3
Grasp the conventional nature of the distinction
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that there is no real difference between dhati and lazim except mere convention (wad') and terminology (istilah). The logicians have differentiated between similars and equated between differents.
This is the core of his critique: the distinction is arbitrary and not based on the realities themselves.
4
See the consequence for definitions
Since the distinction is conventional, the entire art of real definitions (sina'at al-hudud al-haqiqiyya) built upon it is invalid. A real distinction must be based on what things are in themselves, not on stipulation.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the definition of khamr in fiqh. Logicians might say intoxication is essential (dhati) while being a liquid is a concomitant (lazim). Ibn Taymiyya would argue both are equally real attributes; the distinction is merely conventional.
This shows why Islamic legal theorists need to be critical of metaphysical distinctions borrowed from Greek logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: فقوله: (اللازم الذي لا يفارق في الوجود والوهم يشتبه بالذاتي غاية الاشتباه) كلام صحيح، بل ليس بينهما في الحقيقة فرق إلا بمجرد الوضع والاصطلاح، كما قد بين في غير هذا الموضع وبين أنهم في هذا الوضع المنطقي والاصطلاح المنطقي فرقوا بين المتماثلين وسووا بين المختلفين. وهذا وضع مخالف لصريح العقل، وهو أصل صناعة الحدود الحقيقية عندهم، فتكون صناعة باطلة، إذ الفرق بين الحقائق لا يكون بمجرد أمر وضعي، بل بما هي عليه الحقائق في نفسها وليس بين ما سموه ذاتيا وما سموه لازما للماهية في الوجود والذهن فرق حقيقي في الخارج، وإنما هي فروق اعتبارية تتبع الوضع واختيار الواضع وما يفترضه في ذهنه.
The second aspect: His statement: 'The necessary concomitant that never separates in existence and imagination is extremely similar to the essential' is correct speech. Rather, there is no real difference between them except mere convention and terminology, as has been explained elsewhere. And it is explained that in this logical convention and logical terminology they differentiated between similars and equated between differents. This convention contradicts clear reason, and it is the foundation of the art of real definitions according to them, so the art becomes invalid. For the distinction between realities is not by mere convention, but by what the realities are in themselves. And there is no real external difference between what they called 'essential' and what they called 'necessary concomitant' of the essence in existence and mind. Rather, they are relational distinctions following convention, the choice of the positor, and what he assumes in his mind.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logical distinction between dhati (essential) and lazim (necessary concomitant). The author states that the logicians themselves admit that the necessary concomitant (lazim) that never separates in existence and imagination is extremely similar to the essential (dhati). He argues that in reality there is no real difference between them except mere convention (wad') and terminology (istilah). The logicians, through their conventional distinctions, have differentiated between things that are similar and equated between things that are different. This convention contradicts clear reason. Since the entire art of real definitions (sina'at al-hudud al-haqiqiyya) is built on this unsound foundation, the art is invalid. Real distinctions between realities must be based on what the realities are in themselves, not on conventional stipulations. The difference between dhati and lazim is merely relational and depends on the choice of the positor and his mental assumptions. An example from fiqh: the intoxicating property of khamr is sometimes considered essential by logicians, but in reality both intoxicating power (lazim) and being a liquid (accidental) are equally real attributes.

الذاتي
اللازم
الوضع

Key Takeaway

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Multiple Differentiae and Remote Genus
Critiques logicians' definition rules on differentiae and genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's two critiques against logicians' requirements for definitions: requiring all differentiae and using remote genus.

Core Idea
Logicians' definition rules are conventional, not essential; mutakallimun forbid multiple differentiae and prefer proximate genus.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of Aristotelian definition theory by attacking two more conditions logicians impose: that all differentiae must be mentioned, and that remote genus can substitute for proximate. He argues these rules do not aid in depicting the essence (taswir al-mahiyya) and are merely conventional. He contrasts them with the positions of the mutakallimun, who forbid combining two differentiae or genus with differentia in a single definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' rule
Logicians require that a definition include all differentiae (fusul) of the species. For example, 'animal' is defined as 'a body that grows, is sentient, and moves by will.' They require both 'sentient' and 'voluntarily moving' as differentiae.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's objection
Ibn Taymiyya argues that requiring all differentiae does not help depict the essence; distinction can be achieved with fewer. This is merely a conventional (wad'i) rule, not a necessary logical truth.
3
Contrast with the mutakallimun's position
The mutakallimun forbid combining two differentiae of equal generality and specificity. They allow only one and also forbid combining genus and differentia in one definition.
4
Apply to a real Islamic science example
In fiqh, defining 'khamr' as 'intoxicating drink' uses one differentia; adding 'liquid' would be superfluous per mutakallimun.
5
Evaluate the fourth critique on remote genus
Logicians sometimes use remote genus (e.g., 'substance' for human) instead of proximate ('animal'). Ibn Taymiyya criticizes this as similarly arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله في الثالث: إنه يشترط جميع الفصول: فهذا كاشتراط الجنس، وليس في ذلك ما يفيد تصوير الماهية، وأما التمييز فيحصل بدون ذلك. وهذا أمر وضعي. والمتكلمون قد اتفقوا على أنه لا يجوز الجمع بين وصفين متساويين في العموم والخصوص، فلا يجمع بين «فصلين». وهذا كما إذا حد «الحيوان» بأنه «جسم نام، حساس، متحرك بالإرادة». فالحساس والمتحرك بالإرادة فصلان، والمنطقيون يوجبون ذكرهما، والمتكلمون يمنعون من ذكرهما جميعاً ويأمرون بالاقتصار على أحدهما، ضد ما يوجبه هؤلاء، كما أن المتكلمين يمنعون من ذكر الجنس مع الفصل. وأما الوجه الرابع: فإنه من جنس أخذ «الجنس البعيد بدل القريب». فإن تقسيم الجنس بالفصول الأخيرة دون الأولية مثل تقويم النوع بالأجناس الأولية دون الأخيرة.
As for his statement in the third [aspect]: that all the differentiae are required: this is like requiring the genus, and there is nothing in that that benefits the depiction of the essence. As for distinction, it occurs without that. And this is a conventional matter. The mutakallimun have agreed that it is not permissible to combine two descriptions that are equal in generality and specificity, so two differentiae are not combined. This is like when one defines 'animal' as 'a body that grows, is sentient, moves by will'. Then 'sentient' and 'moving by will' are two differentiae. The logicians require mentioning both, while the mutakallimun forbid mentioning both together and command restricting to one of them, contrary to what these [logicians] require. Similarly, the mutakallimun forbid mentioning the genus together with the differentia. As for the fourth aspect: it is of the kind of taking 'the remote genus instead of the proximate'. For dividing the genus by the ultimate differentiae rather than the primary ones is like constituting the species by the primary genera rather than the ultimate ones.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage critiques two more aspects of logical definitions: the requirement of all differentiae and the use of remote genus. For the third aspect, the author states that requiring all differentiae (fusul) in a definition is like requiring the genus; it does not help depict the essence (taswir al-mahiyya). Distinguishing the thing can be achieved without all differentiae. This is merely conventional. The mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) have agreed that one should not combine two descriptions that are equal in generality and specificity, i.e., two differentiae, in one definition. For example, defining 'animal' as 'a body that grows, is sentient, and moves by will' uses two differentiae (sentient and moving by will). The logicians require both, but the mutakallimun forbid combining them and allow only one. They also forbid combining genus and differentia. As for the fourth aspect, the author says it is like taking the remote genus instead of the proximate genus. Dividing the genus by the ultimate differentiae (instead of primary ones) is analogous to constituting the species by primary genera (instead of ultimate ones). This is another arbitrary convention. An example from fiqh: defining 'khamr' as 'intoxicating drink' uses the differentia 'intoxicating'; requiring also 'being a liquid' would be superfluous according to the mutakallimun.

Key Terms
الفصل
الجنس البعيد
الجنس القريب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the mutakallimun say about combining two differentiae in one definition?
A) It is required for a complete definition.
B) It is permissible but not required.
C) It is forbidden; they command restricting to one.
D) It is obligatory according to all scholars.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique reveals that logicians' rules for definitions are conventional, not necessary; mutakallimun offer a more pragmatic approach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the two critiques (all differentiae or remote genus) applies to the following definition attempt: 'Khamr is an intoxicating liquid that is derived from grapes.' Explain why using the passage.
?
In a debate with a logician, a mutakallim defines 'human' as 'rational animal'. The logician objects that it should include 'animal, rational, and mortal'. Using Ibn Taymiyya's argument, write a short rebuttal.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Requiring All Differentiae
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the logicians' requirement for all differentiae and contrasts it with theologians' view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of requiring all differentiae in definitions and contrast it with the theologians' position.

Core Idea
Including all differentiae is conventional, not necessary for differentiation.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of the logicians' rules for definition. He challenges the requirement that a definition must include all differentiae (distinguishing characteristics). He argues this condition is like requiring the genus—it does not necessarily help in depicting the quiddity, and differentiation can occur without it. He then contrasts this with the theologians' view, which forbids combining two differentiae that are equal in generality and specificity. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to evaluate definitions used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' condition
Locate the logicians' claim that a definition must include all differentiae. Ibn Taymiyya states this in the first sentence: 'اشتراط جميع الفصول'.
This is a requirement for hadd (complete definition).
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that requiring all differentiae does not aid in 'taswir al-mahiyya' (depicting the quiddity) and that differentiation (tamyiz) can be achieved without them. He calls it a conventional (waḍʿī) matter.
The objection is epistemological: the condition is not logically necessary.
3
Examine the theologians' alternative rule
The mutakallimun agreed it is impermissible to combine two descriptions equal in generality and specificity—i.e., two differentiae cannot both be stated. They command restricting to one.
This rule contrasts directly with the logicians' requirement.
4
Analyze the example: defining 'animal'
The passage gives the definition of 'animal' as 'body, growing, sensitive, moving by will'. 'Sensitive' and 'moving by will' are two differentiae. Logicians require both; theologians forbid combining them.
Consider why each school might choose one differentia over the other.
5
Consider the fourth aspect: distant genus
Ibn Taymiyya mentions that logicians sometimes use a distant genus instead of a proximate genus, analogous to constituting species by primary genera rather than ultimate differentiae. This is another criticism.
This relates to the proper order of genus and differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) اشتراط جميع «الفصول» الجنس وأما قوله في الثالث : إنه يشترط جميع الفصول» : فهذا كاشتراط الجنس»، وليس الوجه الثالث : في ذلك ما يفيد تصوير ،الماهية وأما التمييز فيحصل بدون ذلك. وهذا أمر وضعي . والمتكلمون قد اتفقوا على أنه لا يجوز الجمع بين وصفين متساويين في العموم كاشتراط والخصوص، (۲) فلا يجمع بين «فصلين». وهذا كما إذا حَدَّ «الحيوان» بأنه «جسم نام، حساس، متحرك بالإرادة» . فَـ الحسّاس والمتحرك بالإرادة فصلان، والمنطقيون يوجبون ذكرهما، والمتكلمون يمنعون من ذكرهما جميعاً ويأمرون بالاقتصار على أحدهما (٢)، ضد ما ، ضد ما يوجبه هؤلاء ، (۳) كما أن المتكلمين يمنعون من ذكر الجنس مع الفصل ) وأما الوجه الرابع : فإنه من جنس أخذ « الجنس البعيد بدل القريب». فإن تقسيم الوجه الرابع / «الجنس» بـ «الفصول الآخِرِيَّة دون الأولية مثل تقويم النوع» بـ «الأجناس الأولية» /٢٦ دون الآخِرية.
(1) The condition of all the differentiae: the genus. As for his statement in the third (point): 'He requires all the differentiae': this is like requiring the genus. And the third aspect does not yield a depiction of the quiddity, while differentiation occurs without it. This is a conventional matter. The theologians have agreed that it is not permissible to combine two descriptions equal in generality and specificity, so two differentiae are not combined. This is like when one defines 'animal' as 'body, growing, sensitive, moving by will'. So 'sensitive' and 'moving by will' are two differentiae. The logicians require mentioning both, while the theologians forbid mentioning both together and command restricting to one of them, contrary to what these require. Also the theologians forbid mentioning the genus with the differentia. As for the fourth aspect: it is of the type of taking the distant genus instead of the proximate. So the division of the fourth aspect: the genus by the ultimate differentiae without the primary, like constituting the species by the primary genera without the ultimate.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage continues Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' rules for definition. He first addresses the logicians' claim that a definition must include all the differentiae (distinguishing characteristics). He argues that this condition, like requiring the genus, does not necessarily aid in depicting the quiddity; differentiation (tamyiz) can occur without it. He then contrasts the logicians' position with that of the theologians (mutakallimun), who have agreed that it is impermissible to combine two descriptions that are equal in generality and specificity, meaning two differentiae cannot both be stated. The example given is defining 'animal' as 'body, growing, sensitive, moving by will'. Here 'sensitive' and 'moving by will' are two differentiae. The logicians require both, but the theologians forbid combining them and command to restrict to just one. Additionally, the theologians forbid mentioning the genus along with the differentia. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya discusses the fourth aspect: the logicians sometimes use a distant genus instead of a proximate genus, which he criticizes as analogous to constituting the species by primary genera rather than ultimate ones.

الفصل
النوع

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that requiring all differentiae in a definition is a conventional rule, not a logical necessity, and the theologians' rule to restrict to one differentia is sounder for achieving clarity and avoiding redundancy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Art of Definition is a Conventional Invention
Ibn Taymiyya argues definition is a conventional art, not a real science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the art of definition as a conventional invention and distinguish it from legitimate conventions.

Core Idea
Definition is a human convention, not a real science — and it contradicts reason and existence.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a direct attack on the logicians' claim that the art of definition (sina'at al-hadd) is a rigorous scientific discipline. He asserts that it is merely a conventional (wad'i) invention, and what is worse, it contradicts clear reason and the nature of existence. In this lesson, we will trace his argument and understand why he believes this art cannot serve as a genuine standard for knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target
Ibn Taymiyya is responding to al-Ghazali's statement that observing order in definitions is a condition for fulfilling the art of definition and is extremely difficult. Find this reference in the passage.
The passage quotes al-Ghazali indirectly: 'وقوله «رعاية الترتيب في هذه الأمور شرط للوفاء بصناعة الحد، وهو في غاية العُسر»'.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's core claim
He says the art of definition is a conventional invention (`wad' istilahi`), not a real scientific matter. This is his key thesis.
Look for the phrase 'صناعة الحد وضع اصطلاحي غيرُ فطري'.
3
Distinguish conventions that are beneficial
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts the art of definition with purely conventional matters like giving proper names, which have benefit and do not contradict reason or existence.
He says: 'ليست من الأوضاع المجرَّدة كوضع أسماء الأعلام؛ فإن تلك فيها منفعة، لا تخالف عقلاً ولا وجوداً'.
4
Analyze the critique
The logicians' convention, however, contradicts both reason and existence, making it false. This is Ibn Taymiyya's core critique: it is not a harmless convention but a harmful one.
He says: 'وأما وضعهم فمخالف لصريح العقل والوجود'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صناعة الحد وضع اصطلاحي غيرُ فطري وقوله «رعاية الترتيب في هذه الأمور شرط للوفاء بصناعة الحد، وهو في غاية العُسر»، فيقال : ذلك هذه صناعة وضعيّة اصطلاحية، ليست من الأمور الحقيقية العلمية. وهي مع مخالفة لصريح العقل ولما عليه الوجود في مواضع فتكون باطلة، ليست من الأوضاع المجرَّدة كوضع أسماء الأعلام؛ فإن تلك فيها منفعة، لا تخالف عقلاً ولا وجوداً. وأما وضعهم فمخالف لصريح العقل والوجود، ولو كان وضعاً مجرّداً لم يكن ميزاناً للعلوم والحقائق فإن الأمور الحقيقية العلمية لا تختلف باختلاف الأوضاع والاصطلاحات كالمعرفة بصفات الأشياء وحقائقها. فالعلم بأن الشيء حيُّ، أو عالم، أو قادر أو مُريد أو متحرك، أو ساكن، أو حسّاس، أو غير حسّاس، ليس هو من الصناعات الوضعية، بل هو من الأمور الحقيقية الفطرية التي فطَرَ الله تعالى عباده عليها ، كما فَطَرهم على أنواع الإرادات الصحيحة والحركات المستقيمة. (1) لا سيما وهؤلاء يقولون : إن المنطق ميزان العلوم العقلية»، ومراعاتُه تَعْصِم الذهن عن أن يغلط في فكره ، كما أن «العَرُوض ميزان الشعر، و «النحو» و«التصريف» ميزانُ الألفاظ العربية - المركبة والمفردة وآلاتُ المواقيت موازين لها . العلوم العقلية ولكن ليس الأمر كذلك، فإن العلوم العقلية تُعلم بما فَطَر الله عليه بني آدم من أسباب الإدراك، لا تقف على ميزان وضعي لشخص معين، ولا يُقلد في/ «العقليات» تعلم بالفطرة أحد؛ بخلاف العربية، فإنها عادة لقوم لا تُعْرَف إلا بالسماع، وقوانينها لا تعرف إلا بالاستقراء؛ بخلاف ما به يُعرف مقادير المَكِيلات، والمَوْزونات، والمذروعات،
The art of definition is a conventional, non-innate invention. And his statement: 'Observing order in these matters is a condition for fulfilling the art of definition, and it is extremely difficult' — so it is said: This is a conventional, invented art, not among the real scientific matters. And it, while contradicting clear reason and what existence is upon in many places, is false. It is not like the purely conventional matters such as giving proper names, for those have benefit and do not contradict reason or existence. But their convention contradicts clear reason and existence. Even if it were a mere convention, it would not be a standard for sciences and realities, for real scientific matters do not differ with differences of conventions and terminologies, like knowledge of the attributes of things and their realities. So knowledge that a thing is alive, or knowing, or powerful, or willing, or moving, or stationary, or sensitive, or not sensitive — this is not among the conventional arts, but rather among the real innate matters that Allah has created His servants upon, just as He created them upon types of correct desires and upright movements. (1) Especially since these people say: 'Logic is the standard of rational sciences, and observing it safeguards the mind from error in its thinking,' just as prosody is the standard of poetry, and grammar and morphology are the standards of Arabic words — compound and singular — and timekeeping instruments are standards for them. But it is not so; for rational sciences are known by what Allah has created the children of Adam upon from means of perception; they do not depend on a conventional standard for a specific person, nor is anyone imitated in intellectual matters learned by innate disposition. Unlike Arabic, for it is a custom of a people and is only known by hearing, and its laws are only known by induction; unlike how measures, weights, and lengths are known.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a sharp critique of the logicians' claim that the art of definition (sina'at al-hadd) and logic in general are essential for rational knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya begins by asserting that the art of definition is a conventional (wad'i) and invented (istilahi) art, not a real scientific matter. He refers to al-Ghazali's statement that observing order in definitions is a condition and is extremely difficult. Ibn Taymiyya responds that this art contradicts clear reason and the nature of existence, making it false. He distinguishes it from purely conventional matters like naming, which have benefit and do not contradict reason or existence. The logicians' convention, however, does contradict both. Even if it were a mere convention, it would not serve as a standard for sciences and realities, because real scientific knowledge—such as knowing that something is alive, knowing, powerful, etc.—does not change with conventions. Such knowledge is innate (fitri), placed by Allah in His servants. Ibn Taymiyya then addresses the logicians' claim that logic is the standard of rational sciences, comparing it to prosody for poetry or grammar for language. He rejects this analogy entirely: rational sciences are known through the innate means of perception that Allah has given humans; they do not require a conventional standard. Unlike Arabic, which is a custom learned by hearing and induction, rational knowledge is innate and not subject to imitation or convention. Thus, the logicians' framework is fundamentally misguided.

Key Terms
صناعة الحد
وضع اصطلاحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, how does the art of definition differ from conventional naming?
A) It is easier to learn.
B) It contradicts reason and existence.
C) It is innate to humans.
D) It is purely conventional with no benefit.

Key Takeaway
The art of definition is a human convention that, unlike beneficial conventions, contradicts reason and existence, and thus cannot be a standard for real knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a claim by a contemporary author that the art of definition is necessary for any rigorous thought. Then, using Ibn Taymiyya's criteria, evaluate whether that claim falls into the same category of conventional artifice that contradicts reality.
